Statistics

Complete edition · all 14 chapters · by Dr. Vidya Aggrawal · Nightingale College
Statistics · Chapter 1

What Statistics Is

Statistics is not a branch of mathematics that exists to punish you. It is a set of honest habits for handling one stubborn fact about the world: things vary. Two people on the same diet lose different amounts of weight, two identical machines produce slightly different parts, two polls of the same election disagree by three points. Statistics is the discipline of describing that variation clearly, and of deciding what a limited pile of data does — and does not — allow you to conclude about the much larger world it came from. This chapter builds the vocabulary that every later chapter leans on, and it does so with arithmetic no harder than what you already do to split a restaurant bill.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • Explain what statistics is and why variation makes it necessary.
  • Distinguish descriptive from inferential statistics and say which one a given claim uses.
  • Define population, sample, parameter, and statistic, and use the correct symbols (μ, σ, N versus x̄, s, n).
  • Explain why we sample, and tell sampling error apart from sampling bias.
  • Classify a variable as qualitative or quantitative, and quantitative variables as discrete or continuous.
  • Assign a variable to its level of measurement — nominal, ordinal, interval, or ratio — and say which calculations that level permits.
  • Compute and interpret a mean, median, and range, and explain when the mean misleads.
  • Read a statistical claim critically, naming the most common ways numbers are used to mislead.

Section 1Why statistics — turning data into decisions

Variation is the reason this subject exists

If every patient responded to a drug identically, you would need one patient to test it. If every light bulb failed at exactly 1,200 hours, you would need one bulb to write the warranty. Nothing behaves that way. Measurements of the same thing differ, individuals differ from one another, and repeating a study gives slightly different answers each time. Statistics is the science of collecting, organizing, summarizing, and drawing conclusions from data in the presence of that variation.

The practical payoff is decisions. A hospital deciding whether to change a protocol, a company deciding which of two web pages to keep, a city deciding where to put a bus route — all of them face the same problem. They have some data, the data are noisy, the data cover only part of the situation, and a choice still has to be made. Statistics is how you make that choice defensible rather than merely confident.

The field splits cleanly in two, and knowing which half you are in is most of the battle. Descriptive statistics summarizes the data you actually have. Inferential statistics uses those data to make a claim about a larger group you did not measure, and attaches an honest statement of uncertainty to that claim.

Descriptive statistics: making a pile of numbers speak

Suppose you record your commute, in minutes, on nine workdays:

22, 25, 19, 31, 24, 27, 21, 88, 23

Nine numbers is already too many to hold in your head. Descriptive statistics compresses them. Start with the mean (the arithmetic average), written x̄ and read “x-bar”:

x̄ = Σx ÷ n

The symbol Σ (capital sigma) just says “add them all up,” and n is how many values you have. Adding: 22 + 25 + 19 + 31 + 24 + 27 + 21 + 88 + 23 = 280, and n = 9, so

x̄ = 280 ÷ 9 = 31.1 minutes

Now the median, the middle value once the data are sorted. Sorted, the nine values are 19, 21, 22, 23, 24, 25, 27, 31, 88. With n = 9 the middle position is the 5th, so the median is 24 minutes. And the range, the crudest measure of spread, is largest minus smallest: 88 − 19 = 69 minutes.

Look at what those three numbers reveal. The mean says 31.1 minutes; the median says 24. They disagree because of a single day — the 88 — when something went badly wrong. Delete that one day and the remaining eight sum to 192, giving a mean of 192 ÷ 8 = 24.0 minutes, exactly matching the median. One unusual value dragged the average up by seven minutes. If you budgeted 31 minutes for your commute you would be early almost every single day.

That is the first real lesson of the subject: a summary is a choice, and different summaries answer different questions. The median answers “what is a typical day?” The mean answers “what happens on average over many days?” — which is the right question if you are totalling up hours lost to commuting over a year.

Inferential statistics: from the part to the whole

Descriptive work stops at the data in front of you. The moment you say “and therefore the population is like this,” you have crossed into inference. If 640 of 1,000 surveyed voters favor a measure, the descriptive fact is that 64% of those 1,000 favored it. The inferential claim — “about 64% of all voters favor it, give or take three points” — is a leap, and inference is the machinery that makes the leap safely and quantifies how far it might land off target.

Two tools do most of that work, and both are routinely misdescribed, so meet them correctly now even though the details come later. A confidence interval is a range built from your sample by a procedure with a known long-run success rate; “95% confidence” describes the procedure, meaning that if the study were repeated over and over, about 95% of the intervals produced that way would contain the true value. It does not mean there is a 95% chance that the one interval in front of you contains it. A p-value is the probability of getting data at least as extreme as yours if a specified “nothing is going on” hypothesis were true. It is emphatically not the probability that the hypothesis is true.

Both tools carry conditions, and the conditions are the part most often skipped. Their advertised performance — the 95%, the p-value — holds only if the sample was actually drawn at random from the population being described, if the observations are independent of one another, and if the model behind the calculation is a reasonable fit to the data. Feed either procedure a self-selected sample and it will still return a tidy interval and a crisp p-value; those numbers simply will not mean what they claim to. Assumptions are not fine print appended to a result. They are the load-bearing wall the result stands on, and every later chapter will state them before it states a conclusion.

 Descriptive statisticsInferential statistics
QuestionWhat do these data look like?What does the larger group look like?
Scope of claimOnly the cases measuredBeyond the cases measured
Typical toolsMean, median, range, standard deviation, tables, histograms, box plotsConfidence intervals, hypothesis tests, regression, margin of error
UncertaintyNone to report — the numbers are what they areMust be reported — it is the whole point
Example“The 30 students in this class averaged 78 on the exam.”“Students taught this way score about 5 points higher, 95% CI 2 to 8.”
Descriptive statements cannot be wrong; inferential ones can. If you computed the mean of your 30 students correctly, no future data can refute it — it is a fact about those 30 people. But the claim that all students taught this way gain 5 points is a bet about people you never measured, and it can be flatly wrong. Every argument about statistics is really an argument about that second kind of statement.
Interactive Draw real samples from a simulated city of 20,000 heart rates: describing the sample gives exact facts, while inferring about the population produces a confidence interval whose long-run capture rate you can watch converge on 95%. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Statistics exists because measurements and individuals vary; its job is turning noisy data into defensible decisions.
  • Descriptive statistics summarizes the data you have; inferential statistics generalizes beyond them and reports the uncertainty.
  • The mean (x̄ = Σx ÷ n) is pulled by extreme values; the median is not. Report both when they disagree.
  • “95% confident” describes the long-run behavior of the procedure, not the chance that this one interval is right.

Section 2Population and sample

The whole group and the piece you can afford

A population is the entire collection of individuals, objects, or measurements you actually want to know about. A sample is the subset of that population you manage to observe. Notice that “population” does not have to mean people: it can be every weld on a bridge, every transaction last quarter, or every possible roll of a die. What defines a population is the question you are asking. If you want to know about licensed electricians in Ohio, that is your population; a study of the electricians employed by one Ohio contractor is a sample from it, and possibly a poor one.

Measuring every member of a population is a census. It is the ideal, and it is usually impossible. Populations are too big, too expensive to reach, too spread out, still being created (next year's customers do not exist yet), or destroyed by measurement — you cannot crash-test every car you intend to sell, or taste-test every jar of sauce. A well-chosen sample of 1,200 people can describe a nation of 300 million to within a few percentage points, for the price of a few days of phone calls. That efficiency is the whole reason sampling exists.

Parameter versus statistic

This distinction carries the rest of the book, so learn it once and properly. A parameter is a number that describes a population. A statistic is a number that describes a sample. The parameter is what you want; the statistic is what you can get. Both get their own symbols, and mixing them up is the single most common notation error in a first course.

QuantityPopulation parameterSample statisticStatus
SizeNnCounts
Meanμ (mu)x̄ (x-bar)μ is fixed and unknown; x̄ varies by sample
Standard deviationσ (sigma)sσ is fixed and unknown; s varies
Proportionpp̂ (p-hat)p is fixed and unknown; p̂ varies

A memory hook that actually works: Population goes with Parameter and with Greek letters; Sample goes with Statistic and with ordinary Roman letters. Greek for the truth you cannot see, Roman for the estimate you can compute.

Worked example: one parameter, many statistics

Take a tiny population of five employees whose years of service are 2, 4, 6, 8, and 10. Because this population is small enough to measure completely, we can compute the parameter directly:

μ = (2 + 4 + 6 + 8 + 10) ÷ 5 = 30 ÷ 5 = 6 years

The population standard deviation uses σ = √[ Σ(x − μ)2 ÷ N ]. The deviations from 6 are −4, −2, 0, 2, 4; squaring gives 16, 4, 0, 4, 16, which sum to 40. So σ2 = 40 ÷ 5 = 8 and σ = √8 ≈ 2.83 years.

Now pretend we could only afford to look at three employees. Different samples of three give different answers:

Sample of n = 3Sumx̄Σ(x − x̄)2s2 = Σ(x − x̄)2 ÷ (n − 1)s
2, 4, 6124.04 + 0 + 4 = 88 ÷ 2 = 42.00
2, 6, 10186.016 + 0 + 16 = 3232 ÷ 2 = 164.00
6, 8, 10248.04 + 0 + 4 = 88 ÷ 2 = 42.00

The parameter μ never moved: it is 6, permanently. The statistic x̄ came out 4.0, 6.0, and 8.0. That spread of answers is sampling error — the unavoidable difference between a sample statistic and the population parameter that arises purely because you looked at a part instead of the whole. Sampling error is not a mistake and it cannot be eliminated, only measured and shrunk (larger samples make it smaller).

Notice also the n − 1 in the sample variance. Deviations are measured from x̄, which is itself pulled toward the sample's own values, so the squared deviations come out slightly too small on average. Dividing by n − 1 instead of n corrects that tilt, so that s2 neither systematically over- nor under-estimates σ2. Population variance divides by N; sample variance divides by n − 1.

Sampling bias is a different animal entirely. Bias is a systematic tilt in how the sample was chosen that pushes the statistic away from the parameter in a consistent direction — surveying only people who answer landlines, only customers who bothered to leave a review, only patients healthy enough to return for follow-up. The defense against sampling error is a bigger sample. The defense against bias is a properly randomized one, in which every member of the population has a known, non-zero chance of selection.

A biased sample does not get better by getting bigger. In 1936 the Literary Digest mailed ten million straw ballots and received about 2.4 million back — a colossal sample — and confidently predicted Alf Landon would defeat Franklin Roosevelt. Roosevelt won in a landslide. The magazine had drawn its addresses from car registrations, telephone directories, and its own subscriber list, which in the Depression skewed wealthy, and only motivated people mailed the ballot back. Two and a half million wrong people are still the wrong people. Size fixes sampling error; only good design fixes bias.
Interactive Draw real random samples from a fully visible population of 400 and watch the sample mean miss the population mean every time — then hide the population to see the honest view of research. Open full ↑
Key points
  • A population is everyone or everything you want to describe; a sample is the part you observe; a census measures the whole population.
  • Parameters (μ, σ, p, N) describe populations and are fixed but unknown; statistics (x̄, s, p̂, n) describe samples and change from sample to sample.
  • We sample because censuses are too costly, too slow, incomplete, or destructive.
  • Sampling error is the natural sample-to-sample wobble and shrinks with larger n; sampling bias is a systematic tilt from bad selection and shrinks only with better design.

Section 3Types of data

Qualitative versus quantitative

A variable is any characteristic that can differ from one individual to the next; the recorded values are the data. The first fork in the road is whether the variable names a quality or measures a quantity.

Qualitative (also called categorical) variables place each individual into a category: blood type, brand of phone, county of residence, whether a part passed inspection. The values are labels. You can count how many fall in each category and compute percentages, but the labels themselves cannot be added, averaged, or subtracted.

Quantitative (also called numerical) variables record an amount or a count: height, salary, number of siblings, reaction time in milliseconds. Their values are genuine numbers on which arithmetic means something. A useful test: if the answer to “how much” or “how many” is a number that you would meaningfully add up, the variable is quantitative.

Discrete versus continuous

Quantitative variables split again. A discrete variable can take only separated values, typically from counting — you can list the possibilities, and there are gaps between them. The number of children in a household is 0, 1, 2, 3, …; no household contains 2.4 children. A continuous variable can take any value in an interval, typically from measuring — between any two possible values there is always another. A person's weight might be 68 kg, 68.4 kg, or 68.4173 kg; the only limit is the precision of the scale.

The rule of thumb: counted things are discrete, measured things are continuous. Money is the classic gray case — strictly discrete because it comes in cents, but with so many closely spaced possible values that we treat it as continuous in practice.

VariableQualitative or quantitative?Discrete or continuous?Why
Blood type (A, B, AB, O)Qualitative—A label, not an amount
Eye colorQualitative—A category
Number of siblingsQuantitativeDiscreteCounted; only whole numbers occur
Cars sold per dayQuantitativeDiscreteCounted; gaps between values
Body temperatureQuantitativeContinuousMeasured; any value in a range
Time to finish a raceQuantitativeContinuousMeasured; limited only by the clock
ZIP codeQualitative—Digits used as a name for a place

Numbers that are not quantities

Plenty of data look numeric and are not. ZIP codes, jersey numbers, patient ID numbers, and phone numbers are made of digits, but the digits function as names. The average ZIP code of your customers is a meaningless number that a spreadsheet will cheerfully compute for you. The same trap appears when categories are coded for a computer — entering 1 = single, 2 = married, 3 = divorced does not make marital status quantitative, and the “mean marital status of 1.8” is nonsense. Always ask what the number stands for, not what it looks like.

A worked point on the discrete side that trips people up: an average of a discrete variable need not be a possible value. Take five households with 1, 2, 0, 3, and 2 children. The sum is 1 + 2 + 0 + 3 + 2 = 8, and n = 5, so

x̄ = 8 ÷ 5 = 1.6 children per household

No household has 1.6 children, and that is fine. The mean is a summary of the group, not a description of any individual member. “The average family has 1.6 children” is a correct statement about families collectively and an absurd one about any family in particular.

Ask what the number means before you do arithmetic on it. A spreadsheet will average ZIP codes, jersey numbers, and coded categories without complaint, because software checks the data type, not the meaning. You are the only safeguard. If adding two values together produces something that cannot exist — ZIP 12345 plus ZIP 67890 — the variable is qualitative no matter how numeric it looks.
Interactive Classify eight real columns from a 120-household survey as qualitative or quantitative, discrete or continuous — with a live dot plot and a measuring-precision slider that reveals which variables keep splitting into new values and which stay frozen on the whole numbers. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Qualitative (categorical) data name categories; quantitative (numerical) data measure amounts or counts.
  • Quantitative data are discrete (counted, separated values) or continuous (measured, any value in an interval).
  • Digits are not automatically quantities — ZIP codes and coded categories are qualitative.
  • The mean of a discrete variable can be a value no individual has (1.6 children); a summary describes the group, not a member.

Section 4Levels of measurement

Four levels, each adding one property

Classifying data as qualitative or quantitative is a good first cut, but it is too coarse to tell you which calculations are legitimate. The levels of measurement refine it into four rungs. Each rung keeps everything the rung below it has and adds exactly one new capability, so the ladder is easy to hold in memory.

Nominal is the bottom rung: categories with no natural order. Blood type, religion, marital status, department. You can count and compare frequencies, and that is all. The only measure of center available is the mode — the most common category.

Ordinal adds order. Categories can be ranked, but the distances between ranks are unknown or unequal. Class rank, military rank, letter grades, survey answers from “strongly disagree” to “strongly agree”, cancer stage I–IV. First, second, and third place tell you who beat whom but not by how much: the winner may have finished a tenth of a second ahead of second place and second place five minutes ahead of third. Mode and median are available; the mean is questionable.

Interval adds equal, meaningful distances. The gap between 20 and 30 is the same size as the gap between 70 and 80, so subtraction and addition are legitimate. What interval scales lack is a true zero — zero is a chosen landmark rather than the absence of the thing. Temperature in Celsius or Fahrenheit is the standard example; so are calendar years and IQ scores. Mode, median, and mean are all fair game, but ratios are not.

Ratio adds the true zero, where 0 genuinely means “none of it.” Height, weight, age, income, distance, reaction time, counts of anything. Because zero is real, ratios finally mean what they say: 40 kg is twice 20 kg, and a 60-minute task takes three times as long as a 20-minute task. Every summary statistic is permitted.

LevelCategories?Ordered?Equal intervals?True zero?ExamplesCenter you may use
NominalYesNoNoNoBlood type, ZIP code, eye colorMode only
OrdinalYesYesNoNoLetter grade, cancer stage, race placingMode, median
IntervalYesYesYesNoCelsius, Fahrenheit, calendar year, IQMode, median, mean
RatioYesYesYesYesHeight, weight, income, time elapsedAll, plus meaningful ratios

The zero test, worked out in numbers

The interval-versus-ratio line is the one students actually get wrong, and a single calculation settles it. Is 20°C twice as hot as 10°C? Naively 20 ÷ 10 = 2, so it looks like it. Test that claim by re-expressing the same two temperatures on a different scale and recomputing the ratio.

Convert to Fahrenheit with °F = (9 ÷ 5) × °C + 32:

10°C → (9 ÷ 5)(10) + 32 = 18 + 32 = 50°F
20°C → (9 ÷ 5)(20) + 32 = 36 + 32 = 68°F
Ratio: 68 ÷ 50 = 1.36

Convert to kelvin, which does have a true zero, with K = °C + 273.15:

10°C → 283.15 K
20°C → 293.15 K
Ratio: 293.15 ÷ 283.15 = 1.035

The same two physical states produce ratios of 2, 1.36, and 1.035 depending on which scale you happen to write them in. A quantity whose “twice as much” changes when you change units is not really a ratio at all. The kelvin figure is the honest one, because 0 K is the actual absence of thermal energy, while 0°C is just the freezing point of water — a convenient landmark someone chose. Differences, however, survive the translation perfectly: 20°C − 10°C = 10 Celsius degrees, and 293.15 K − 283.15 K = 10 kelvins. That is exactly what “interval level” means: differences are trustworthy, ratios are not.

Why the level dictates the arithmetic

Consider four patients who rate their pain on a 0–10 scale as 2, 3, 3, and 8. The mean is (2 + 3 + 3 + 8) ÷ 4 = 16 ÷ 4 = 4.0, and the median is the average of the two middle values, (3 + 3) ÷ 2 = 3.0. Which should you report? A self-reported pain rating is ordinal: nothing guarantees that the step from 2 to 3 hurts as much as the step from 7 to 8, and one patient's 8 is not another's 8. The mean of 4.0 quietly assumes equal spacing that the scale does not provide, and it has been dragged upward by the single 8. The median of 3.0 assumes only that the ratings can be ranked, which is true, so it is the safer summary.

In practice, researchers do sometimes average ordinal scales — averaging Likert responses is nearly universal — and it is often defensible when the categories are many and roughly evenly spaced. But it is a modeling assumption, not a mathematical entitlement, and it should be made knowingly. The general rule holds: statistics computed at a level higher than your data support will produce numbers that look precise and mean nothing.

The zero test in one question. Ask: does 0 on this scale mean “none of the thing”? If yes, the scale is ratio and “twice as much” is a real statement. If 0 is just a convenient marker — 0°C, the year 0, an IQ of 0 — the scale is interval, and you may add and subtract but never divide one value by another.
Interactive Re-number, re-space and re-scale four real variables in ways each one actually permits, and watch live which statistics survive — the mode, the median, the mean, the difference, the ratio — and which were only ever artifacts of the numbers you chose. Open full ↑
Key points
  • The four levels are nominal → ordinal → interval → ratio, each adding one property: order, then equal intervals, then a true zero.
  • Nominal allows only counts and the mode; ordinal adds the median; interval adds the mean; ratio adds meaningful ratios.
  • Interval scales lack a true zero, so 20°C is not twice as hot as 10°C — the same pair gives ratios of 2, 1.36, or 1.035 depending on the unit.
  • Averaging ordinal data assumes equal spacing the scale does not guarantee; the median is the safer summary.

Section 5How statistics mislead

Correlation is not causation

Two variables are correlated when they move together. That fact alone permits four very different explanations, and the arithmetic cannot tell them apart. A may cause B. B may cause A (hospitals are full of sick people; hospitals do not cause sickness). Some third factor — a confounding variable — may cause both. Or it may be coincidence, which is far more common than intuition suggests: sift enough series against enough other series and you will find pairs that track each other beautifully and have nothing whatever to do with one another.

The classic confounding example: ice cream sales and drownings rise and fall together across the year. Ice cream does not drown anyone. Hot weather independently drives both. The way to break a confound is a randomized experiment, in which the researcher assigns the treatment by chance so that all other factors — known and unknown — are balanced across groups on average. Observational data can suggest causes; randomization is what lets you claim one.

The standard repertoire of tricks

Most misleading statistics are not fabricated. They are true numbers presented so that a reasonable reader draws a false conclusion. A short catalogue covers most of what you will meet.

The trickWhat it looks likeThe question that defeats it
Truncated axisA bar chart whose y-axis starts at 94 instead of 0, turning a 2% gap into a cliffWhere does the axis start, and how big is the change really?
Cherry-picked window“Crime is up 30%” measured from an unusually quiet yearWhy that start date? What does the full series look like?
Relative risk alone“Doubles your risk” with no baseline givenDoubles it from what to what, in absolute terms?
Missing denominator“More accidents happen within 5 miles of home”Out of how many trips? Is the rate actually higher?
Survivorship bias“Our graduates earn six figures” — measured only on those who repliedWho is missing from this data, and why?
Average hides the spread“Average wait 12 minutes” when half wait 2 and half wait 40What is the median? What is the spread? Show me the distribution.
Selective outcome reportingTwenty outcomes measured, the one “significant” result publishedHow many things were tested? Was this planned in advance?
Tiny or unstated sample“80% of users preferred it” (from ten users)What is n? Who were they? How were they chosen?
Precision theater“Satisfaction rose to 73.62%” from a survey of 200Do the data support that many digits? Where is the margin of error?

The relative-risk trick deserves the arithmetic, because it is the one that changes real decisions. Suppose a condition affects 2 in 1,000 untreated people, and a new drug cuts that to 1 in 1,000. Two headlines are available, both true:

Relative risk reduction = (0.002 − 0.001) ÷ 0.002 = 0.001 ÷ 0.002 = 0.50 = 50% — “the drug halves your risk.”

Absolute risk reduction = 0.002 − 0.001 = 0.001 = 0.1 percentage points — one fewer case per thousand people treated.

From the absolute figure you get the number needed to treat: NNT = 1 ÷ 0.001 = 1,000. One thousand people must take the drug for one to benefit. “Cuts your risk in half” and “999 out of 1,000 people get no benefit” describe the identical study. Marketing prints the first; a fair report prints both. Whenever you see a percentage change in a risk, find the baseline before you react.

Reading a claim critically

You do not need advanced technique to catch most bad statistics. You need a short list of questions asked stubbornly.

  • What exactly was measured, and on whom? Definitions do enormous work. “Unemployment,” “homelessness,” and “engagement” all have several defensible definitions that give different numbers.
  • Who was in the sample, and how were they chosen? Volunteers, subscribers, and people who answered the phone are not random samples of anyone.
  • What is n, and what is the margin of error? A result with no stated uncertainty is a result you cannot evaluate.
  • Compared with what? A number without a comparison group is not evidence. “70% improved” means nothing if 70% improve without treatment.
  • Is this an experiment or an observation? Only randomized assignment supports a causal claim.
  • Who is missing? Dropouts, non-responders, and failed businesses are invisible in the data and often the most informative cases.
  • Who paid, and what result did they want? Not disqualifying, but it tells you where to look hardest.
  • Does the conclusion actually follow? Statistical significance is not practical importance; a tiny, useless effect can be “significant” in a large enough study.

Two interpretations are worth nailing down permanently, because they are misstated constantly — including in published work. A p-value of 0.03 means: if there were truly no effect, data at least as extreme as ours would occur about 3% of the time. It does not mean there is a 3% chance the effect is absent, a 97% chance the finding is real, or that the effect is large. The mirror-image error is equally common and equally damaging: a large p-value — 0.40, say — does not show that there is no effect. Failing to find convincing evidence against a hypothesis is not the same as establishing that it is true, and a small or noisy study fails to detect real effects routinely. “We did not reject it” means only that these data are compatible with it; absence of evidence is not evidence of absence. And a 95% confidence interval of 2 to 8 points describes a procedure that, used repeatedly across many studies, captures the true value about 95% of the time. For the single interval printed in front of you, the true value is either inside it or it is not; the 95% is a property of the method, not a probability attached to this one result.

Be as skeptical of numbers you like as of numbers you don't. The hardest failure to catch is the study that confirms what you already believe, because you stop asking questions the moment you agree. Run the same checklist on both. Statistics is not a tool for winning arguments — it is a discipline for finding out that you were wrong before someone else does.
Interactive Seven real-looking statistical claims, each wired to a live simulation — drag the axis baseline, cherry-pick a window, move the outlier, isolate the confounder — until the flaw computes itself into the open. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Correlation permits four explanations — cause, reverse cause, confounding, or coincidence — and only a randomized experiment singles out cause.
  • Common tricks: truncated axes, cherry-picked baselines, relative risk without the absolute, missing denominators, survivorship bias, and averages that hide the spread.
  • A 50% relative reduction can mean a 0.1-percentage-point absolute reduction and an NNT of 1,000. Always ask for the baseline.
  • A p-value is not the probability the hypothesis is true; a 95% confidence interval describes the long-run procedure, not this one interval.

The chapter in one breath

  • Statistics exists because things vary; it turns noisy, partial data into decisions you can defend.
  • Descriptive statistics summarizes the data in hand; inferential statistics generalizes to a population and must report its uncertainty.
  • The mean is pulled by extreme values and the median is not — nine commutes averaging 31.1 minutes had a median of just 24.
  • A population is what you want to know about; a sample is what you can afford. Parameters (μ, σ, p) are fixed and unknown; statistics (x̄, s, p̂) vary from sample to sample.
  • Sampling error is unavoidable wobble that shrinks with larger samples; sampling bias is a design flaw that a bigger sample does not touch — extra data only pin down the wrong value more precisely.
  • Data are qualitative (categories) or quantitative, and quantitative data are discrete (counted) or continuous (measured) — digits alone do not make a quantity.
  • The four levels of measurement — nominal, ordinal, interval, ratio — each add one property, and they decide which calculations are legitimate; without a true zero, “twice as much” is meaningless.
  • Most misleading statistics use true numbers framed badly; ask what was measured, on whom, compared with what, out of how many, and who is missing.

ReferenceWords worth knowing

A quick reference for the terms introduced in this chapter. Skim it now to see the shape of the vocabulary, then come back whenever a word in a later chapter refuses to sit still.

Bias (sampling bias)
A systematic tendency for a sampling method to produce statistics that miss the parameter in a consistent direction. Unlike sampling error, it is not reduced by taking a larger sample.
Categorical (qualitative) variable
A variable whose values are labels placing each individual into a group, such as blood type or county. Counts and percentages are meaningful; arithmetic on the labels is not.
Census
A study in which every member of the population is measured, producing parameters directly rather than estimating them.
Confidence interval
A range of plausible values for a parameter, produced by a procedure with a stated long-run success rate. “95% confidence” describes how often the procedure captures the truth across many repetitions, not the chance that one particular interval does.
Confounding variable
An outside factor related to both variables under study, capable of producing a correlation between them even when neither causes the other.
Continuous variable
A quantitative variable that can take any value within an interval, limited only by measurement precision — height, time, temperature.
Correlation
A tendency for two variables to move together. A correlation is consistent with four different situations — one variable causing the other, the reverse, a confounding variable driving both, or coincidence — and the number itself cannot distinguish among them. Only a randomized experiment licenses a causal claim.
Descriptive statistics
Methods for organizing and summarizing the data actually collected, with no claim made about anyone not measured.
Discrete variable
A quantitative variable whose possible values are separated, usually arising from counting — number of children, cars sold per day.
Inferential statistics
Methods for drawing conclusions about a population from a sample, always accompanied by a statement of uncertainty.
Interval level
A level of measurement with ordered categories and equal, meaningful distances, but no true zero. Differences are meaningful; ratios are not. Celsius temperature and calendar years are examples.
Mean
The arithmetic average, Σx ÷ n for a sample and Σx ÷ N for a population. It uses every value, which is why a single extreme observation can pull it well away from the bulk of the data.
Median
The middle value of a sorted data set — the average of the two middle values when the count is even. Unaffected by how extreme the extreme values are, so it is the more honest summary of a skewed distribution.
Mode
The most frequently occurring value or category. The only measure of center available for nominal data.
Nominal level
The lowest level of measurement: unordered categories. Only counts, proportions, and the mode are meaningful.
Ordinal level
A level of measurement whose categories can be ranked, but with unknown or unequal distances between ranks. The median is appropriate; the mean requires an added assumption.
Parameter
A numerical characteristic of a population, such as μ or σ. Fixed in value and, in practice, unknown.
Population
The complete collection of individuals, objects, or measurements that a question is about.
P-value
The probability of observing data at least as extreme as those obtained, computed assuming a specified null hypothesis is true. It is not the probability that the hypothesis is true.
Quantitative (numerical) variable
A variable recording an amount or a count, on which arithmetic is meaningful.
Random sample
A sample chosen by a chance mechanism in which every member of the population has a known, non-zero probability of selection — the principal defense against bias.
Range
The largest value minus the smallest — the simplest measure of spread, and the most fragile, since it is determined entirely by the two most extreme observations.
Ratio level
The highest level of measurement: ordered, equally spaced, and possessing a true zero meaning “none.” Ratios such as “twice as much” are meaningful. Weight, income, and elapsed time are examples.
Sample
The subset of a population actually observed and measured.
Sampling error
The natural difference between a sample statistic and the population parameter, caused solely by observing a part rather than the whole. It shrinks as sample size grows.
Statistic
A numerical characteristic of a sample, such as x̄ or s. Computable from data, and different for every sample drawn.
Variable
Any characteristic that can differ from one individual to another; the recorded values of a variable are the data.

Check yourself

1. A researcher reports, “The 48 patients in our clinic averaged a systolic pressure of 134 mmHg.” Is this descriptive or inferential? What would have to change to make it the other kind?
Descriptive. The claim is confined to the 48 people actually measured, so it cannot be wrong if the arithmetic is right. It becomes inferential the moment it generalizes — for example, “patients at clinics like ours average about 134 mmHg, 95% CI 130 to 138” — because that is a claim about people who were never measured, and it therefore requires a statement of uncertainty.
2. Seven employees' commute times in minutes are 15, 18, 20, 22, 24, 27, and 94. Compute the mean and the median, and say which better represents a typical commute.
Mean = 31.4 minutes; median = 22 minutes; the median is the better summary of a typical day. Sum: 15 + 18 + 20 + 22 + 24 + 27 + 94 = 220, and n = 7, so x̄ = 220 ÷ 7 = 31.43. The data are already sorted, so with n = 7 the median is the 4th value, 22. Six of the seven commutes are below the mean — the single 94 pulls it upward — so the median describes a typical day better, though the mean is still the right number for computing total time spent commuting.
3. Match each symbol to what it describes: μ, s, p̂, N. Which are parameters and which are statistics?
μ = population mean (parameter); s = sample standard deviation (statistic); p̂ = sample proportion (statistic); N = population size (parameter). Greek letters and capital N belong to populations and are fixed but unknown; Roman letters with hats or bars belong to samples and change every time you draw a new one.
4. A population consists of the values 3, 5, 7, 9. Compute μ and σ. Then compute x̄ and s for the sample 3, 9.
μ = 6, σ ≈ 2.24; x̄ = 6, s ≈ 4.24. Population: sum = 3 + 5 + 7 + 9 = 24, so μ = 24 ÷ 4 = 6. Deviations −3, −1, 1, 3 square to 9, 1, 1, 9, summing to 20, so σ2 = 20 ÷ 4 = 5 and σ = √5 ≈ 2.24 (divide by N). Sample {3, 9}: x̄ = 12 ÷ 2 = 6. Deviations −3 and 3 square to 9 and 9, summing to 18, so s2 = 18 ÷ (2 − 1) = 18 and s = √18 ≈ 4.24 (divide by n − 1). The sample mean happened to hit μ exactly; the sample standard deviation is far off, which is exactly what small samples do.
5. An online poll on a news site draws 500,000 responses; a professional telephone poll surveys 1,100 randomly selected adults. Which gives the better estimate of national opinion, and why?
The random sample of 1,100. The online poll is a self-selected convenience sample: only visitors to that site who feel strongly enough to click are represented, which is a systematic tilt — sampling bias. Increasing the number of responses shrinks sampling error but does nothing to the bias, so a huge biased sample estimates the wrong thing very precisely. Random selection is what makes the small sample representative.
6. Explain the difference between sampling error and sampling bias, and give the remedy for each.
Sampling error is the natural sample-to-sample variation of a statistic around the parameter; sampling bias is a systematic tilt built into how the sample was selected. Sampling error is unavoidable, is not a mistake, and shrinks as n grows. Bias is a design flaw — it does not average out and a larger sample does not help; the remedy is proper random selection and high response rates.
7. Classify each variable as qualitative, quantitative discrete, or quantitative continuous: (a) number of emergency-room visits last month, (b) patient ID number, (c) blood cholesterol in mg/dL, (d) shirt size (S, M, L).
(a) Quantitative discrete; (b) qualitative; (c) quantitative continuous; (d) qualitative. Visits are counted, so only whole numbers occur. An ID number is digits used as a name — averaging it is meaningless. Cholesterol is measured and can take any value in a range. Shirt size is a category (specifically an ordinal one, since S < M < L).
8. Six households report 2, 1, 0, 4, 2, and 3 pets. Compute the mean. Is it a possible value for any single household, and does that matter?
Mean = 2.0 pets — and here it happens to be a possible value, but that is a coincidence. Sum = 2 + 1 + 0 + 4 + 2 + 3 = 12, and n = 6, so x̄ = 12 ÷ 6 = 2.0. Had one household reported 5 instead of 4, the sum would be 13 and the mean 13 ÷ 6 ≈ 2.17 — not a possible count for any household. The mean of a discrete variable need not be attainable by an individual, because it summarizes the group rather than describing a member.
9. Name the level of measurement for each: (a) jersey number, (b) finishing place in a race, (c) year of birth, (d) weight in kilograms. Justify (c).
(a) Nominal, (b) ordinal, (c) interval, (d) ratio. Year of birth is interval because the gap between 1990 and 2000 is genuinely the same length as the gap between 2000 and 2010, so differences are meaningful — but the year 0 is a historical convention, not the beginning of time, so ratios are not. The year 2000 is not “twice” the year 1000.
10. A colleague says a 30°C day is “three times as hot” as a 10°C day. Show numerically why this is wrong.
Celsius is an interval scale with no true zero, so the ratio depends entirely on the unit chosen. In Fahrenheit: 10°C = (9 ÷ 5)(10) + 32 = 50°F and 30°C = (9 ÷ 5)(30) + 32 = 86°F, giving a ratio of 86 ÷ 50 = 1.72. In kelvin, which does have a true zero: 283.15 K and 303.15 K, giving 303.15 ÷ 283.15 = 1.07. Three different “multiples” (3, 1.72, 1.07) for the same two days proves the ratio is not a real property. The difference, 20 degrees, is identical in Celsius and kelvin and is the only trustworthy comparison.
11. A supplement is advertised as cutting the risk of a condition by 40%. In the trial, the condition occurred in 5 of 1,000 controls and 3 of 1,000 treated. Compute the relative risk reduction, the absolute risk reduction, and the number needed to treat.
RRR = 40%, ARR = 0.2 percentage points, NNT = 500. Risks are 5 ÷ 1,000 = 0.005 and 3 ÷ 1,000 = 0.003. Relative: (0.005 − 0.003) ÷ 0.005 = 0.002 ÷ 0.005 = 0.40, so the advertised 40% is honest arithmetic. Absolute: 0.005 − 0.003 = 0.002, or 0.2 percentage points. NNT = 1 ÷ 0.002 = 500 — five hundred people must take it for one to avoid the condition. Both figures describe the same trial; quoting only the relative one makes a small effect sound large.
12. A study reports a difference between two teaching methods with p = 0.02 and a 95% confidence interval of 1 to 9 points. Two colleagues say: “There's a 98% chance the method works,” and “There's a 95% chance the true gain is between 1 and 9.” Correct both.
Both are wrong, and both make the same error — attaching a probability to the hypothesis or the parameter rather than to the procedure. p = 0.02 means that if the two methods truly did not differ, data at least this extreme would arise about 2% of the time; it says nothing directly about the probability that the method works, and nothing at all about how large the effect is. The interval 1 to 9 comes from a method that captures the true value in about 95% of repeated applications; for this one interval the true gain is either inside it or outside it. A fair reading: the data are hard to reconcile with “no difference,” and gains anywhere from a trivial 1 point to a substantial 9 points remain consistent with the evidence — which is a wide enough range that the practical value of the method is still undecided.
Statistics · Chapter 2

Sampling & Data Collection

Almost every number you will ever meet in a report, a news story, or a research paper came from a sample — a few people standing in for many. That fact should make you nervous until you understand why it works, and it works far better than most people expect: a well-chosen sample of a thousand can describe a nation of three hundred million to within a few percentage points. The catch is the word well-chosen. A sample earns its authority from how it was selected, never from how big it is, and the most spectacular statistical failures in history were enormous samples collected badly. This chapter shows you where good data comes from, how it goes wrong, and how to tell the difference between a study that can prove a cause and one that can only spot a pattern.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • Distinguish a population from a sample, and a parameter from a statistic.
  • Explain why a randomly chosen sample represents its population, and why precision depends on n rather than on population size.
  • Compute and interpret the standard error of a sample mean, σ/√n.
  • Describe and carry out simple random, stratified, cluster, and systematic sampling, and say why convenience sampling is not a probability method.
  • Separate sampling error from bias, and sampling error from nonsampling error.
  • Recognize undercoverage, voluntary response, nonresponse, and response bias in a described study.
  • Tell an observational study from an experiment and state exactly what each one licenses you to conclude.
  • Design a simple experiment using control, randomization, replication, blinding, and a placebo.

Section 1Why sampling works

Population, sample, parameter, statistic

The population is every individual you want to say something about — every registered voter, every bag of chips off the line, every patient with a certain diagnosis. The sample is the subset you actually measure. A number that describes the population is a parameter; a number computed from your sample is a statistic. Parameters are fixed and (almost always) unknown. Statistics are known and, because a different sample would have given different numbers, they wobble.

Keeping the two straight is the single most useful habit in this book, because the entire subject is one long answer to one question: how close is my statistic to the parameter I care about? Notation helps — Greek letters for the truth, Roman letters for the estimate.

QuantityParameter (population)Statistic (sample)Known?
Meanμx̄μ unknown; x̄ computed
Standard deviationσsσ unknown; s computed
Proportionpp̂p unknown; p̂ computed
SizeNnBoth usually known

A census measures the whole population, so it reports parameters directly. We sample instead of taking a census for four reasons: cost, time, inaccessibility (you cannot find every homeless person in a city), and destruction (testing whether a fuse blows destroys the fuse — a census of fuses would leave none to sell).

Why a random sample looks like its population

Representativeness is not something you can eyeball. A sample "looks representative" to a human only after the human has decided which features matter, and the features that matter are usually the ones nobody thought of. Random selection solves this without knowing anything about the population: if every individual has the same chance of being drawn, then every characteristic — age, income, opinion, blood type, the ones you measured and the ones you never imagined — is represented in the sample in roughly its population proportion, on average. Randomness does not guarantee a good sample every time. It guarantees that the misses are unbiased and, crucially, that their size is calculable.

That last point is what makes sampling a science rather than a hope. The wobble from sample to sample has a name, sampling variability, and a measurable size, the standard error. For a sample mean drawn from a population with standard deviation σ,

SE(x̄) = σ ÷ √n

That formula comes with conditions attached, and it is worth naming them now because every technique later in this book inherits them. It requires the observations to be independent — in practice, that the individuals were drawn at random and that the sample is no more than roughly 10% of the population, so that removing one unit barely changes the odds for the next. Violate either condition and the formula misreports the true wobble: sample a large slice of a small population and the real standard error is smaller than σ/√n; use a cluster design, where neighbors resemble neighbors, and it is larger. A standard error computed from a convenience sample is not conservative or approximate — it is meaningless, because the probabilities it is built from do not exist.

A worked example: how precision grows

Suppose commute times in a large city have a standard deviation of σ = 12 minutes, and we want to estimate the mean commute μ.

  • n = 36: SE = 12 ÷ √36 = 12 ÷ 6 = 2.0 minutes
  • n = 144: SE = 12 ÷ √144 = 12 ÷ 12 = 1.0 minute
  • n = 576: SE = 12 ÷ √576 = 12 ÷ 24 = 0.5 minutes

Read the pattern: to cut the standard error in half you must quadruple the sample, because n sits under a square root. Going from 36 to 144 people (four times the work) buys you twice the precision, not four times. This is the law of diminishing returns that governs every survey budget ever written.

It also tells you how far off a typical sample lands — provided one more assumption holds: that the sampling distribution of x̄ is approximately normal. That is exact when the population itself is normal, and approximately true for a large enough n whatever the population shape, by the Central Limit Theorem. Granting it, if the true mean commute is μ = 24 minutes and n = 36, then roughly 95% of samples produce an x̄ within about 2 standard errors of the truth — that is, between 24 − 2(2.0) = 20 minutes and 24 + 2(2.0) = 28 minutes. Notice what is random here: the sample, not μ. The population mean sits still; our estimate is what moves. The "95%" describes how often the procedure lands close, across all the samples we might have drawn — it is never a probability statement about μ, which has no probability distribution at all.

Now look hard at that formula. N, the population size, does not appear in it. As long as the sample is a small fraction of the population, a sample of 1,000 is essentially as precise for a country of 300 million as it is for a town of 30,000. This is the most counter-intuitive true fact in introductory statistics, and it is why national polls of about 1,000 people are not absurd. What you need is a big enough spoonful, not a spoonful proportional to the size of the pot — provided the soup is stirred.

Size never fixes a bad method. In 1936 the Literary Digest mailed about 10 million presidential straw ballots and got back roughly 2.4 million — then predicted a landslide for Alf Landon. Roosevelt won in one of the largest landslides on record. The lists had been built from car registrations, telephone books, and magazine subscribers, which in the Depression meant the affluent, and only about a quarter of recipients bothered to reply. George Gallup polled a few tens of thousands, chose them deliberately to mirror the electorate, and got it right. Two and a half million badly chosen people lost to fifty thousand well chosen ones. Stirring beats scooping.
Interactive Draw real random samples from a city of millions and watch the estimates pile up around the true 62% — then discover that precision comes from sample size, not city size, and that a convenience sample converges just as tightly on the wrong answer. Open full ↑
Key points
  • A parameter (μ, σ, p) describes the population and is unknown; a statistic (x̄, s, p̂) is computed from the sample and varies.
  • Random selection makes a sample representative on every variable at once, including the ones you never measured.
  • SE(x̄) = σ/√n — quadrupling n halves the standard error.
  • Precision depends on n, essentially not on population size; a bigger badly chosen sample is just a more confident wrong answer.

Section 2Sampling methods

The sampling frame comes first

Before you can select anybody you need a sampling frame: the actual list of units you will draw from. The frame is where good intentions quietly die. You may want "all adults in the county," but your frame is "all landline numbers in the county," and those are not the same population. Every method below is only as good as the frame it runs on, so write down your frame explicitly and ask who is missing from it.

The four probability methods

A probability sample is one in which every individual has a known, nonzero chance of selection. Only probability samples support the inference machinery in the rest of this book.

A simple random sample (SRS) of size n is one chosen so that every possible group of n individuals is equally likely to be the sample. In practice you number the frame 1 to N and let a computer draw n distinct numbers. Note the definition carefully: it is stronger than "everyone has an equal chance." Systematic sampling gives everyone an equal chance too, yet most groups of size n can never be selected by it, so it is not an SRS.

Stratified sampling splits the frame into strata — groups whose members resemble each other, such as class year, region, or age band — and then takes a separate random sample inside every stratum. When the strata really do differ from one another, this beats an SRS of the same size, because you have removed between-group variation from your estimate by force instead of leaving it to luck. It also guarantees you will not accidentally draw zero seniors.

Cluster sampling also divides the frame into groups, but the logic is reversed. Clusters are naturally occurring bundles — city blocks, clinics, classrooms — each of which ideally resembles the whole population in miniature. You randomly select whole clusters and then measure everyone inside the chosen ones. This is a cost method: visiting 30 blocks and interviewing every household is far cheaper than chasing 600 addresses scattered across a city. The price is precision. Neighbors resemble neighbors, so 600 people from 30 blocks carry less independent information than 600 people drawn at random.

Systematic sampling takes every k-th unit after a random start, with k = N/n. It is fast and needs no numbered list — you can do it at a factory exit or a store door. Its one real danger is periodicity: if the list repeats with a cycle that matches k, the sample locks onto one phase of the cycle. Sampling every 7th day of hospital admissions, for instance, means every observation falls on the same weekday.

A worked example: proportional allocation and a systematic plan

A student body of N = 12,000 breaks down as 4,200 first-years, 3,300 sophomores, 2,700 juniors, and 1,800 seniors (4,200 + 3,300 + 2,700 + 1,800 = 12,000 ✓). We want n = 400 students, allocated proportionally, so each stratum contributes nh = n × (Nh / N). Since 400/12,000 = 1/30, we simply divide each stratum by 30:

  • First-years: 4,200 ÷ 30 = 140
  • Sophomores: 3,300 ÷ 30 = 110
  • Juniors: 2,700 ÷ 30 = 90
  • Seniors: 1,800 ÷ 30 = 60

Check the total: 140 + 110 + 90 + 60 = 400 ✓. Every student's selection probability is 140/4,200 = 1/30 for a first-year, 60/1,800 = 1/30 for a senior — identical, which is exactly the point of proportional allocation.

The same 400 by systematic sampling instead: k = 12,000 ÷ 400 = 30. Draw a random start between 1 and 30 — say 17 — then take students 17, 47, 77, 107, and so on, each one 30 further down the list. The j-th pick is 17 + 30(j − 1), so the 400th is 17 + 30(399) = 17 + 11,970 = 11,987, comfortably inside the list ✓. Fast, tidy, and perfectly fine here — unless the enrollment list happens to cycle every 30 names.

MethodHow it worksBest whenMain risk
Simple randomEvery group of size n equally likelyYou have a clean, complete frameCostly to reach a scattered sample
StratifiedSplit into similar groups, sample within every groupGroups differ from each otherNeeds stratum sizes known in advance
ClusterSplit into mini-populations, sample whole groupsTravel or contact cost dominatesLess precise per person; clusters must be internally diverse
SystematicRandom start, then every k-th unit, k = N/nUnits arrive in a stream; no list existsHidden periodicity in the ordering
ConvenienceWhoever is easy to reachNever, for inferenceBias of unknown size and unknown direction

Convenience sampling — surveying the people already in front of you — and its cousin voluntary response sampling, in which people select themselves by calling in or clicking a link, are not probability methods at all. Nobody's chance of selection is known, so no standard error can be computed and no margin of error is meaningful. They are useful for generating hypotheses and for pilot-testing a questionnaire, and for nothing else. Real surveys often chain methods together into a multistage design: cluster on counties, then on blocks, then take an SRS of households, then randomly pick one adult inside each household.

Stratified versus cluster, settled in one line. Stratified takes some from every group; cluster takes everyone from some groups. And the ideal internal makeup is opposite: strata should be internally similar and different from each other, while clusters should each be internally diverse — little copies of the whole population.
Interactive Draw simple random, stratified, cluster, systematic and convenience samples from the same town of 300 households, and watch the exact standard errors, design effects and bias shift with every choice you make. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Define the sampling frame first; everyone missing from the frame is invisible to your study.
  • An SRS makes every group of size n equally likely — a stronger condition than equal individual chances.
  • Stratified samples from every group (usually more precise); cluster samples whole groups (usually cheaper, less precise).
  • Systematic uses k = N/n after a random start; watch for periodicity.
  • Convenience and voluntary response samples support no valid inference at any size.

Section 3Bias and error

Two completely different ways to be wrong

Every estimate misses the truth, but there are two unrelated reasons why, and confusing them is the most expensive mistake in applied statistics. Sampling error is the ordinary, unavoidable difference between a sample statistic and the population parameter that arises simply because you measured some individuals rather than all of them. It is random, it is centered on zero, and it shrinks predictably as n grows — that is the σ/√n from Section 1.

Bias is systematic: a tendency for your method to miss in the same direction sample after sample. Bias does not shrink with n. Collect ten times as much data with the same flawed method and you get the same wrong answer with a tighter, more persuasive error bar around it. Think of it as target shooting: random error is a wide shot group, bias is a tight shot group centered off the bullseye. More ammunition tightens the group; only fixing the sights moves it.

The named biases, and where they come from

Undercoverage occurs when part of the population cannot appear in the frame at all — a landline-only poll in a cell-phone world, or a mall survey that misses everyone who does not shop at malls. Voluntary response bias arises because people who take the trouble to respond hold stronger opinions, usually negative, than those who do not. Nonresponse bias hits after selection: your sample was chosen properly, but a large chunk did not answer, and the ones who answered differ from the ones who did not. Response bias is about the measurement itself — question wording ("Do you support wasteful spending on X?"), social desirability (people under-report drinking and over-report voting and flossing), interviewer effects, and faulty recall.

ProblemWhat goes wrongDirectionDoes a bigger n help?
Sampling errorLuck of the drawRandom, averages to zeroYes — shrinks in proportion to 1/√n
UndercoverageFrame excludes part of the populationSystematicNo
Voluntary responsePeople select themselves inSystematic (strong opinions over-represented)No
NonresponseSelected people do not answerSystematicNo
Response biasAnswers are inaccurate as givenSystematicNo
Processing / measurementBad instrument, data-entry or coding errorsEitherNo

The last three rows are nonsampling error — every source of error that has nothing to do with which individuals you picked. Nonsampling error is the reason a census can still be wrong: the U.S. census reaches for everybody and still misses people and miscodes answers. In most real surveys, nonsampling error is larger than sampling error, and it is the part nobody reports.

A worked example: bias dwarfs the margin of error

A researcher randomly selects 1,000 adults and asks whether they exercise at least three times a week. Only 400 respond, and 260 of those say yes:

p̂ = 260 ÷ 400 = 0.65, or 65%

The reported 95% margin of error for a proportion is about 1.96 × √(p̂(1 − p̂) / n). Before using it, check what it assumes: a random sample of independent individuals, and enough of both outcomes for the normal approximation to be trustworthy — the usual rule of thumb is at least 10 successes and 10 failures. Here we have 260 yes and 140 no ✓, so the arithmetic is legitimate. Watch what happens next anyway. Step by step:

  • p̂(1 − p̂) = 0.65 × 0.35 = 0.2275
  • 0.2275 ÷ 400 = 0.00056875
  • √0.00056875 ≈ 0.02385
  • 1.96 × 0.02385 ≈ 0.0467 → about ±4.7 percentage points

Now suppose a determined follow-up reaches the 600 nonrespondents and finds that only 25% of them exercise three times a week — 0.25 × 600 = 150 people. The truth across all 1,000 selected adults is

(260 + 150) ÷ 1,000 = 410 ÷ 1,000 = 0.41, or 41%

The estimate was off by 0.65 − 0.41 = 0.24, twenty-four percentage points, while the advertised uncertainty was under five. The bias is five times the entire margin of error. And here is the sting: repeat the study with 10,000 selected adults at the same 40% response rate and the estimate stays near 65% while the margin of error falls to about ±1.5 points. You will have bought yourself a far more precise version of the same wrong number.

A margin of error is a chance-error budget, nothing more. It counts only the randomness of who got drawn. It assumes the frame was complete, everyone answered, and everyone answered honestly — and it says nothing at all about whether those assumptions held. Read "±3 points" as "±3 points if the method was clean." Read it correctly too: the 95% is a property of the procedure across many hypothetical samples, not a 95% chance that this one interval has captured the truth.
Interactive Switch nonresponse and voluntary-response bias on and off in a live survey simulation, and watch the estimate walk away from a truth that never moves — discovering that the bias is set by the ratio of response rates, that more data never repairs it, and that a margin of error cannot see it. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Sampling error is random and shrinks in proportion to 1/√n; bias is systematic and does not shrink at all.
  • Nonsampling error (coverage, nonresponse, response, processing) can strike even a full census and usually exceeds sampling error.
  • Nonresponse bias is measured by who is missing, so a low response rate is a warning sign no sample size can cure.
  • A margin of error quantifies chance variation only — never bias.

Section 4Observational studies versus experiments

One question separates them

The dividing line is embarrassingly simple: who decided who got what? In an observational study the researcher measures individuals as they already are, recording an exposure that the individuals (or life) chose for them. In an experiment the researcher assigns the explanatory variable — deciding, by a chance mechanism, which subjects receive which treatment — and then compares the outcomes.

Observational studies come in familiar shapes. A cross-sectional study takes a snapshot at one point in time. A prospective cohort study identifies people now and follows them forward, waiting for outcomes to appear. A retrospective case-control study starts with people who already have the outcome, matches them to people who do not, and looks backward for differences in exposure. All three observe; none assigns.

Why observation cannot prove cause

A confounding variable (also called a lurking variable) is a third factor associated with the explanatory variable and related to the response, so that its effect cannot be untangled from the effect you care about. Since people sort themselves into the exposed and unexposed groups, and they sort by all sorts of hidden reasons, observational groups differ in a thousand ways besides the exposure.

Watch how badly this can go, with exact numbers. Take 1,000 coffee drinkers, of whom 600 smoke, and 1,000 non-coffee-drinkers, of whom 200 smoke. Let lung cancer strike 10% of smokers and 1% of nonsmokers — and let coffee itself do absolutely nothing.

  • Coffee drinkers: (600 × 0.10) + (400 × 0.01) = 60 + 4 = 64 cases → 64/1,000 = 6.4%
  • Non-coffee-drinkers: (200 × 0.10) + (800 × 0.01) = 20 + 8 = 28 cases → 28/1,000 = 2.8%

Coffee drinkers get lung cancer at 6.4% versus 2.8% — a rate ratio of 6.4 ÷ 2.8 ≈ 2.3, so a headline could truthfully announce that coffee drinkers are more than twice as likely to develop lung cancer. Every number above is real and correctly computed; the causal story is pure fiction manufactured by smoking. That is confounding, and no amount of extra data removes it, because more data means more of the same tangle.

So when you see an association between X and Y, five explanations compete: X causes Y; Y causes X (reverse causation — do sneakers cause fitness, or do the fit buy sneakers?); a confounder causes both; the association is a fluke of sampling; or the study was biased. Observation alone cannot referee among them.

What each design licenses you to say

Two different randomizations do two different jobs, and students mix them up constantly. Random sampling — how subjects got into the study — buys generalization to the population. Random assignment — how subjects got sorted once inside — buys causal conclusions. They are independent choices, so there are four cases.

Random assignmentNo random assignment
Random sampleCausal conclusion, generalizable to the population (the gold standard, and rare)Association only, generalizable to the population (a good survey or cohort study)
No random sampleCausal conclusion, but only for subjects like these (most clinical trials)Association only, and only for subjects like these (a convenience study)

Observational studies are not second-class citizens; they are often the only ethical or feasible option. You cannot randomly assign people to smoke for thirty years. What tips the balance toward causation without an experiment is an accumulated pattern — a large effect, a dose-response gradient, consistency across many populations and designs, correct time order, and a plausible mechanism — which is exactly how the case against tobacco was built.

The observational-study translation rule. Whenever an observational finding is reported with a causal verb — cuts, boosts, prevents, triggers — mentally rewrite it with "is associated with," then ask who chose the exposure and what else those people have in common. If the researchers did not assign the exposure, the causal verb was added by the headline writer, not earned by the data.
Interactive Plant a known effect in a walking program, then run the study two ways — let people choose or let a coin choose — and watch a lurking variable manufacture a benefit that only randomization erases. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Observational study: exposure is observed. Experiment: exposure is assigned by the researcher.
  • A confounder is linked to both the explanatory and response variables and makes association uninterpretable.
  • Random sampling → generalizability; random assignment → causation. They are separate guarantees.
  • Observational studies establish association; only a randomized experiment establishes causation directly.

Section 5Designing an experiment

The vocabulary, then the four principles

The individuals in an experiment are experimental units (called subjects when they are people). A factor is an explanatory variable you control; its settings are levels; a treatment is one combination of levels. The response is what you measure. A study of two drug doses at two delivery times has two factors, each at two levels, and therefore 2 × 2 = 4 treatments.

A good experiment rests on four ideas. Control means holding other conditions constant across groups and, above all, including a control group — a comparison arm receiving no treatment, a placebo, or the current standard of care. Without a comparison group you cannot separate the treatment's effect from the passage of time, from the illness resolving on its own, or from patients regressing toward their usual state. Randomization assigns units to treatments by chance. Replication means enough units per treatment that ordinary variation averages out — not merely repeating one measurement. Blocking groups similar units together and randomizes within each block, the experimental cousin of stratified sampling.

Randomization deserves its own sentence, because it is the whole reason experiments can prove things. It is not there to be fair. It is there to make the treatment groups equivalent, on average, in every respect — age, severity, genetics, diet, optimism, and the hundred variables nobody thought to record — so that when the outcomes differ, the only systematic difference left standing is the treatment. Matching on known variables cannot do this, because you can only match on what you thought of.

Placebo and blinding

A placebo is an inert imitation of the treatment — a sugar pill, a saline injection, a sham procedure. It exists because the placebo effect is real and often large: people improve because they believe they are being treated. If the control group gets nothing at all, the treatment group's improvement mixes the drug's effect with the belief's effect, and you cannot pull them apart. A placebo control makes the belief equal in both arms so the difference isolates the drug.

Blinding keeps people from knowing who got what. In a single-blind study the subjects do not know; in a double-blind study neither the subjects nor the people who interact with them and assess the outcome know. Double-blinding matters because expectation leaks: an enthusiastic assessor who knows a patient got the new drug rates that patient's "moderate" improvement as "marked." Whoever measures the response should be blind, always.

PrincipleWhat it doesWhat breaks without it
Control groupProvides the comparison baselineImprovement from time, healing, or regression is credited to the treatment
RandomizationBalances known and unknown variables across armsGroups differ systematically; confounding returns
ReplicationGives enough units for variation to average outOrdinary noise is mistaken for a real effect
BlockingRemoves a known nuisance variable's influenceA big known source of variation hides the effect
PlaceboEqualizes the psychological effect of being treatedBelief effects are counted as drug effects
Double-blindingRemoves expectation from subjects and assessorsReporting and rating drift toward what people expect

A worked example: building the design

We want to compare a new headache drug against the standard drug and against a placebo, with 180 volunteers.

Completely randomized design. Number the volunteers 1 to 180 and use a computer to shuffle them into three arms of 180 ÷ 3 = 60 each. Every subject gets an identical-looking capsule, so nobody — subject, nurse, or assessor — can tell the arms apart: double-blind. Measure minutes to meaningful relief. Suppose the group means come back as placebo 42 minutes, standard drug 30, new drug 26. The comparisons are 42 − 30 = 12 minutes faster for the standard drug than placebo, and 30 − 26 = 4 minutes faster for the new drug than the standard. Because assignment was random, no pre-existing difference between the groups can explain those gaps in any systematic way; the remaining question is whether a gap this size could plausibly arise from chance alone, which is what the inference tools later in this book are built to answer.

Now block it. Suppose baseline headache severity strongly affects recovery time. Split the 180 into blocks first — say 108 with moderate headaches and 72 with severe ones — and randomize within each block: 108 ÷ 3 = 36 moderate per arm and 72 ÷ 3 = 24 severe per arm, and 36 + 24 = 60 per arm as before, 180 in total ✓. Every arm now has an identical severity mix by construction rather than by luck, so severity can no longer muddy the comparison, and the leftover variation the treatment effect must stand out from is smaller.

Two design cautions worth carrying forward. Keep lurking differences out of the procedure: if the new drug is given in a quiet private room and the placebo in a crowded hallway, the room is confounded with the drug. And beware attrition — subjects who drop out are usually the ones doing worst, so dropping them from the analysis quietly re-creates the very selection bias randomization was meant to destroy.

Randomization is not about fairness — it is about the unknown. Any conscientious researcher can balance the groups on age and sex. Only chance can balance them on the variables nobody has heard of yet. That is precisely why a randomized experiment supports a causal claim and a matched observational study does not: the experiment controls for the confounders you have not imagined.
Interactive Assemble a clinical trial one safeguard at a time — control group, randomization, blinding — and watch each one delete a specific, named slab of bias from what the study reports, across 240 genuinely simulated studies. Open full ↑
Key points
  • The four pillars are control, randomization, replication, and (when useful) blocking.
  • A placebo equalizes the psychological effect of treatment; double-blinding removes expectation from both subjects and assessors.
  • Random assignment balances unmeasured variables, which is what licenses a causal conclusion.
  • Blocking removes a known nuisance variable; it is stratification applied to assignment rather than selection.

The chapter in one breath

  • A parameter describes the population and is unknown; a statistic comes from the sample and varies from sample to sample.
  • Random selection, not sample size, is what makes a sample representative — it balances every variable, including the ones you never measured.
  • SE = σ/√n: quadruple the sample to halve the error, and note that population size is essentially irrelevant.
  • The probability methods are simple random, stratified (some from every group), cluster (everyone from some groups), and systematic (every k-th after a random start); convenience and voluntary response support no inference.
  • Sampling error is random and shrinks with n; bias is systematic and never shrinks — a bigger bad sample is only a more confident wrong answer.
  • Nonsampling error — coverage, nonresponse, response, processing — strikes even a census, and a margin of error accounts for none of it.
  • Observational studies show association and are haunted by confounding; only experiments, which assign the treatment, demonstrate causation.
  • Good experiments use a control group, random assignment, adequate replication, a placebo where relevant, and double-blinding.

ReferenceWords worth knowing

A quick reference for the terms in this chapter — skim it now, or circle back whenever a word trips you up.

Bias
A systematic tendency for a method to miss the truth in the same direction; unlike random error, it does not shrink as the sample grows.
Blinding
Withholding treatment assignment from participants (single-blind) or from participants and the researchers assessing them (double-blind).
Blocking
Grouping similar experimental units together and randomizing treatments within each group, so a known nuisance variable cannot distort the comparison.
Census
An attempt to measure every member of a population rather than a sample; still vulnerable to nonsampling error.
Cluster sample
A sample obtained by randomly choosing whole naturally occurring groups and measuring everyone inside them.
Confounding variable
A third variable associated with the explanatory variable and related to the response, so that their effects cannot be separated.
Control group
The comparison arm of an experiment, receiving no treatment, a placebo, or the current standard of care.
Convenience sample
A sample of whoever is easiest to reach; selection probabilities are unknown, so no valid inference is possible.
Experiment
A study in which the researcher assigns the explanatory variable to units, ideally at random, and then compares responses.
Margin of error
The half-width of an interval estimate, quantifying chance variation from sampling only — never bias or nonsampling error.
Nonresponse bias
Distortion arising when selected individuals do not participate and differ systematically from those who do.
Nonsampling error
Any error unrelated to which individuals were chosen — coverage gaps, nonresponse, inaccurate answers, measurement and processing mistakes.
Observational study
A study that records an exposure the subjects already have rather than assigning it; establishes association, not causation.
Parameter
A fixed numerical summary of a population, such as μ, σ, or p; almost always unknown.
Placebo
An inert imitation of a treatment, used so that the control group's belief in being treated matches the treatment group's.
Population
Every individual about whom you want to draw a conclusion.
Random assignment
Using chance to allocate subjects to treatments; it balances known and unknown variables and is what permits causal conclusions.
Replication
Assigning enough units to each treatment that ordinary variation averages out; not the same as repeating a single measurement.
Response bias
Distortion in the answers themselves — from question wording, social desirability, interviewer effects, or faulty recall.
Sample
The subset of the population actually measured.
Sampling error
The random difference between a sample statistic and the population parameter caused by measuring only part of the population.
Sampling frame
The concrete list of units from which a sample is actually drawn; anyone absent from it cannot be selected.
Simple random sample (SRS)
A sample chosen so that every possible group of n individuals is equally likely to be selected.
Standard error
The typical sample-to-sample wobble of a statistic; for a sample mean it is σ/√n, assuming independent observations.
Statistic
A numerical summary computed from a sample, such as x̄, s, or p̂; it varies from sample to sample.
Stratified sample
A sample formed by dividing the population into similar groups (strata) and drawing a random sample from within every stratum.
Systematic sample
A sample taken by choosing every k-th unit after a random start, with k = N/n; vulnerable to periodicity in the list.
Undercoverage
A coverage gap in which part of the population cannot appear in the sampling frame at all, and so can never be selected.
Voluntary response bias
Distortion arising when people select themselves into a sample, over-representing those with strong opinions.

Check yourself
