  • Use the complement of "none" to solve "at least one" problems.
  • Compute and correctly interpret a conditional probability, and explain why P(A|B) is not P(B|A).
  • Read joint, marginal, and conditional probabilities off a two-way table and build the matching tree diagram.

Section 1What probability is

Random processes, outcomes, and the sample space

A random process is any situation where you know the list of things that could happen but not which one will happen this time: a coin flip, a die roll, a customer either renewing a subscription or not, a machine part either failing this month or not. Each single thing that could happen is an outcome. The complete list of all possible outcomes — every one of them, with no overlap and nothing left out — is the sample space, written S.

Listing the sample space is the most under-rated skill in this chapter, because once you have it, most beginner problems become counting problems. Flip a coin twice and the sample space is S = {HH, HT, TH, TT} — four outcomes, not three. Students frequently write three, reasoning "two heads, two tails, or one of each." That is a mistake worth making once and never again: HT and TH are genuinely different outcomes of the process, and merging them will quietly corrupt every probability you compute afterward.

An event is any collection of outcomes you care to name — a subset of the sample space. "At least one head" is the event {HH, HT, TH}. "Both flips match" is {HH, TT}. An event can hold one outcome, many outcomes, all of them (that is S itself), or none of them (the impossible event). We name events with capital letters: A, B, and so on.

The basic probability formula

When every outcome in the sample space is equally likely — a fair coin, a fair die, a well-shuffled deck, a name drawn blindly from a hat — probability is pure counting:

P(A) = (number of outcomes in A) ÷ (total number of outcomes in S)

That word equally likely is load-bearing. The formula is not a definition of probability; it is a shortcut that works only when the outcomes really are interchangeable. A weighted die, a survey where some people are more likely to respond, a deck someone has stacked — none of these qualify, and for those we get probabilities some other way (from data, from a model, or from a longer-run record).

Three facts follow immediately and are worth memorizing, because they are the sanity checks you will run on every answer for the rest of the book:

  • Every probability satisfies 0 ≤ P(A) ≤ 1. A negative probability or a probability of 1.4 is an arithmetic error, full stop.
  • P(S) = 1. Something in the sample space has to happen.
  • The complement rule: P(Ac) = 1 − P(A), where Ac ("A complement") is the event that A does not happen.

Worked example: rolling two fair dice

Roll a red die and a blue die. Keeping them distinguishable is the trick — each of the 6 red faces can pair with each of the 6 blue faces, so S holds 6 × 6 = 36 equally likely outcomes, written as ordered pairs like (3, 5).

What is P(sum = 7)? Count the pairs that add to 7: (1,6), (2,5), (3,4), (4,3), (5,2), (6,1). That is 6 outcomes.

P(sum = 7) = 6 ÷ 36 = 1/6 ≈ 0.167

What is P(sum ≥ 10)? Sum of 10: (4,6), (5,5), (6,4) — 3 outcomes. Sum of 11: (5,6), (6,5) — 2 outcomes. Sum of 12: (6,6) — 1 outcome. Total 3 + 2 + 1 = 6 outcomes, so P(sum ≥ 10) = 6/36 = 1/6 ≈ 0.167 as well.

What is P(sum is not 7)? Rather than counting 30 pairs, use the complement rule: P(not 7) = 1 − 1/6 = 5/6 ≈ 0.833. Whenever the event you want is messy and its opposite is tidy, flip to the complement.

SumWays it can happenProbabilityDecimal
211/360.028
322/360.056
433/360.083
544/360.111
655/360.139
766/360.167
855/360.139
944/360.111
1033/360.083
1122/360.056
1211/360.028
Total3636/361.000

Notice the last row. The probabilities of a complete, non-overlapping list of outcomes always sum to exactly 1 — that is not a coincidence, it is P(S) = 1 showing up in a table. If your column does not add to 1, you have either double-counted an outcome or lost one.

What a probability actually means

Saying P(sum = 7) = 0.167 does not promise you a seven in the next six rolls. It is a statement about the long run: if you rolled these dice ten thousand times, roughly 16.7% of the rolls would total seven, and the more you rolled the closer that percentage would sit to 16.7%. This is the law of large numbers — the observed proportion of an event converges to its true probability as the number of trials grows.

What the law of large numbers explicitly does not say is that the process "corrects" itself. Ten tails in a row does not make heads more likely on the eleventh flip; the coin has no memory, and P(heads) is still 0.5. The long-run proportion drifts back toward 0.5 not because future flips compensate, but because ten flips is a vanishing share of ten thousand — the early surplus gets diluted, not repaid.

The gambler's fallacy is a misunderstanding of the long run. "It's due" is the belief that a random process owes you a correction. It does not. A fair coin that has landed tails ten times running is still a fair coin, and the eleventh flip is still 0.5. The same error wears a suit in other settings: "we've had three good quarters, so a bad one is overdue." If the trials are truly independent, the past tells you nothing about the next one.
Interactive Lay out every outcome of dice and coin experiments, mark the ones an event contains, and read the probability straight off the count — then roll it thousands of times and watch the observed proportion close in on the arithmetic. Open full ↑
Key points
  • The sample space S is the complete, non-overlapping list of outcomes; an event is any subset of it.
  • For equally likely outcomes, P(A) = (outcomes in A) ÷ (outcomes in S).
  • Always 0 ≤ P(A) ≤ 1, P(S) = 1, and P(Ac) = 1 − P(A).
  • A probability is a long-run relative frequency, not a promise about the next trial — nothing is ever "due."

Section 2The addition rule

"Or" means at least one of them

In everyday speech "or" is often exclusive — "soup or salad" means pick one. In statistics, "or" is always inclusive. The event "A or B" (the union, written A ∪ B) happens if A happens, if B happens, or if both happen. Meanwhile "A and B" (the intersection, A ∩ B) happens only when both occur together. Get these two words nailed down now, because every rule in this chapter is a sentence about one or the other.

The general addition rule is:

P(A or B) = P(A) + P(B) − P(A and B)

Why subtract? Draw two overlapping circles. When you add the area of circle A to the area of circle B, the lens-shaped overlap gets counted twice — once inside each circle. Subtracting P(A and B) removes the duplicate. That is the entire content of the rule: add the pieces, then pay back the double-count.

Mutually exclusive events

Two events are mutually exclusive (also called disjoint) when they cannot both happen on the same trial. In the picture, the circles do not touch. In symbols, P(A and B) = 0 — and the moment that term is zero, the correction term in the addition rule vanishes:

If A and B are mutually exclusive: P(A or B) = P(A) + P(B)

So the "simple" addition rule is not a separate law to memorize — it is the general rule with a zero in it. Learn one formula, not two. Roll a single die: "a 1" and "a 2" are mutually exclusive (a die cannot land on both at once), so P(1 or 2) = 1/6 + 1/6 = 2/6 = 1/3 ≈ 0.333.

The complement rule from Section 1 is the tidiest special case of all. A and Ac are mutually exclusive and together they cover the whole sample space, so P(A) + P(Ac) = P(S) = 1, which rearranges to P(Ac) = 1 − P(A).

Worked example: one card from a standard deck

Draw one card from a well-shuffled 52-card deck. Let H = "the card is a heart" and F = "the card is a face card" (jack, queen, or king).

  • Hearts: 13 cards, so P(H) = 13/52 = 0.250.
  • Face cards: 3 ranks × 4 suits = 12 cards, so P(F) = 12/52 ≈ 0.231.
  • Both at once: the jack, queen, and king of hearts — 3 cards — so P(H and F) = 3/52 ≈ 0.058.

Apply the general addition rule:

P(H or F) = 13/52 + 12/52 − 3/52 = 22/52 = 11/26 ≈ 0.423

Check it by brute force: 13 hearts, plus the 9 face cards that are not hearts, equals 22 cards. It matches. Had you forgotten to subtract, you would have gotten 25/52 ≈ 0.481 — wrong by three cards, exactly the three you counted twice.

RelationshipP(A and B)Rule for P(A or B)Example (one draw or roll)
Mutually exclusive= 0P(A) + P(B)Die shows "a 1" or "a 2" → 1/6 + 1/6 = 1/3
Overlapping> 0P(A) + P(B) − P(A and B)Card is "a heart" or "a face card" → 22/52
Complementary= 0, and union is all of SP(A) + P(Ac) = 1Card "is a heart" or "is not a heart" → 1.000
Mutually exclusive is not the same as independent. This is the single most common mix-up in the chapter, and the two ideas are close to opposites. Mutually exclusive means the events cannot co-occur; independent (Section 3) means knowing one happened tells you nothing about the other. If A and B are mutually exclusive and both have nonzero probability, then learning that A occurred tells you a great deal — it tells you B definitely did not. Mutually exclusive events with nonzero probability are therefore always dependent.
Interactive Drag the overlap between two events and an area-true Venn diagram resolves itself to match — while the sum P(A) + P(B) is taken apart to expose the shared outcomes being counted twice, and a live run of seeded draws settles onto the corrected answer and never onto the naive sum. Open full ↑
Key points
  • "A or B" is inclusive — it includes the case where both happen.
  • General addition rule: P(A or B) = P(A) + P(B) − P(A and B); the subtraction repairs the double-count.
  • Mutually exclusive (disjoint) means P(A and B) = 0, which collapses the rule to P(A) + P(B).
  • Mutually exclusive ≠ independent; disjoint events with nonzero probability are strongly dependent.

Section 3The multiplication rule

"And" means both, and both means multiply

Addition answers "or" questions. Multiplication answers "and" questions. The general multiplication rule is:

P(A and B) = P(A) × P(B|A)

Read it as a two-step story: first A happens, and then, in the world where A has already happened, B happens. The symbol P(B|A) is the conditional probability of B given A, which Section 4 unpacks in detail. For now, read the vertical bar as the word "given" or "assuming."

Independent events

Two events are independent when knowing that one occurred does not change the probability of the other — that is, when P(B|A) = P(B). Substitute that into the general rule and the conditioning drops out:

If A and B are independent: P(A and B) = P(A) × P(B)

Again, one rule with a simplification, not two rules. And this equation runs both directions: it is simultaneously the shortcut you use when events are independent and the test you use to decide whether they are. If P(A and B) equals P(A) × P(B), the events are independent; if it does not, they are dependent.

The most reliable everyday source of dependence is sampling without replacement. Draw a card, keep it, draw again: the second draw faces a 51-card deck whose composition depends on the first card. Draw a card, put it back, shuffle, draw again: the second draw faces the same 52-card deck as the first, so the draws are independent. Same deck, same question, different answer — entirely because of whether the card went back.

Worked example: two hearts in a row

Without replacement. P(first card is a heart) = 13/52. Given that it was, only 12 hearts remain among 51 cards, so P(second is a heart | first was a heart) = 12/51.

P(both hearts) = (13/52) × (12/51) = (1/4) × (4/17) = 1/17 ≈ 0.0588

With replacement. The first card goes back and the deck is reshuffled, so the second draw is a fresh 52-card deck:

P(both hearts) = (13/52) × (13/52) = (1/4) × (1/4) = 1/16 = 0.0625

Replacing the card raises the answer from 0.0588 to 0.0625 — a small gap here, but it widens fast as the sample grows relative to the population. As a working rule, when you are sampling less than about 10% of a population, the dependence is small enough that treating draws as independent is a reasonable approximation.

The "at least one" trick

Questions that begin "what is the probability of at least one…" are almost always complement problems in disguise. Counting "at least one" means counting one, two, three, … separately — tedious. Counting the opposite, "none at all," is a single multiplication. So:

P(at least one) = 1 − P(none)

Example. Roll a fair die four times. What is the probability of at least one six? On any single roll, P(not a six) = 5/6. The four rolls are independent, so P(no six in four rolls) = (5/6)4 = 625/1296 ≈ 0.4823. Therefore:

P(at least one six) = 1 − 625/1296 = 671/1296 ≈ 0.518

Just over a coin flip. Note that the naive guess — "1/6 per roll, four rolls, so 4/6 ≈ 0.667" — is wrong, and you can see why instantly: eight rolls by that logic would give 8/6 = 1.33, which is not a probability. Adding probabilities across repeated trials is the classic beginner error; multiplying complements is the fix.

SetupIs P(B|A) = P(B)?Rule to useP(two hearts)
With replacementYes — independentP(A) × P(B)(13/52)(13/52) = 0.0625
Without replacementNo — dependentP(A) × P(B|A)(13/52)(12/51) = 0.0588
Two fair coin flipsYes — independentP(A) × P(B)(1/2)(1/2) = 0.25 for two heads
Card is king / card is heartYes — independentP(A) × P(B)(4/52)(13/52) = 1/52 = P(king of hearts)

That last row rewards a second look. On a single draw, "king" and "heart" are independent: P(king) × P(heart) = (1/13)(1/4) = 1/52, and there is in fact exactly 1 king of hearts in 52 cards, so P(king and heart) = 1/52 too. The numbers agree, so the events are independent — even though both describe the very same card. Independence is a statement about probabilities, not about physical separateness.

Multiplying probabilities that are not independent is how real people go to prison. The classic courtroom error takes several pieces of evidence, multiplies their individual rarities together, and announces an impossibly tiny number. But if the traits are correlated — and traits usually are — then P(B|A) is far bigger than P(B), and the true joint probability is far bigger than the product. Before you multiply, always ask: does the first event change the odds of the second?
Interactive Draw a chip, then draw another — put the first back and the two draws are independent, keep it out and they are not; a live probability tree, a convergence curve, and a real simulation show the dependence is exactly 1/(N−1) and costs you only spread, never accuracy. Open full ↑
Key points
  • General multiplication rule: P(A and B) = P(A) × P(B|A).
  • Independent means P(B|A) = P(B), which simplifies the rule to P(A) × P(B).
  • P(A and B) = P(A)P(B) is also the test for independence — if the two sides differ, the events are dependent.
  • Sampling without replacement creates dependence; "at least one" problems are solved as 1 − P(none).

Section 4Conditional probability

Conditioning shrinks the sample space

A conditional probability answers a narrower question: not "how likely is A in general" but "how likely is A among the cases where B already happened." Rearranging the multiplication rule gives the definition:

P(A|B) = P(A and B) ÷ P(B),  provided P(B) > 0

The intuition is worth more than the formula. Conditioning on B throws away every outcome where B did not happen and rebuilds the sample space out of what is left. The denominator is no longer "everything" — it is "everything inside B." The numerator counts the part of that shrunken world where A also holds. Whenever you see a vertical bar, ask yourself one question: what is my denominator now?

Worked example: one roll of a fair die

Roll one fair die. Let A = "the roll is a 6" and B = "the roll is even."

Unconditionally, P(A) = 1/6 ≈ 0.167. Now condition on B. We have P(B) = 3/6 = 1/2 (the faces 2, 4, 6), and P(A and B) = 1/6 (only the face 6 is both a six and even). So:

P(A|B) = (1/6) ÷ (1/2) = (1/6) × (2/1) = 2/6 = 1/3 ≈ 0.333

Sanity-check it by counting instead: told the roll is even, the live possibilities are {2, 4, 6}, and exactly one of those three is a six. One out of three. The formula and the counting agree, as they always will.

Now reverse the bar. P(B|A) = P(A and B) ÷ P(A) = (1/6) ÷ (1/6) = 1.000. If the roll is a 6, it is certainly even. So P(A|B) = 0.333 while P(B|A) = 1.000. These are not the same number, and they are not the same question.

Question askedIn symbolsDenominator (the "given" world)Value
Roll is a 6P(A){1,2,3,4,5,6} — all six faces1/6 ≈ 0.167
Roll is a 6, given it is evenP(A|B){2,4,6} — three faces1/3 ≈ 0.333
Roll is a 6, given it is oddP(A|Bc){1,3,5} — three faces0/3 = 0.000
Roll is a 6, given it is > 3P(A|C){4,5,6} — three faces1/3 ≈ 0.333
Roll is even, given it is a 6P(B|A){6} — one face1/1 = 1.000

Dependence, independence, and the confusion of the inverse

Conditional probability gives the cleanest definition of independence there is. Events A and B are independent exactly when P(A|B) = P(A) — the condition tells you nothing, so the probability does not budge. They are dependent when P(A|B) ≠ P(A). In the die example, P(A) = 0.167 but P(A|B) = 0.333, so "is a six" and "is even" are dependent — knowing the roll is even doubles your belief that it is a six.

Compare a single card draw: let K = "king" and H = "heart." P(K) = 4/52 = 1/13 ≈ 0.0769, and P(K|H) = 1/13 as well — among the 13 hearts, exactly one is a king. The condition changed nothing, so K and H are independent. Equivalently, P(K and H) = 1/52 = (1/13)(1/4) = P(K)P(H). Both tests always give the same verdict.

The mistake to guard against for the rest of your life is the confusion of the inverse: treating P(A|B) as if it were P(B|A). It shows up everywhere. "Most people who own a private jet are wealthy" is not "most wealthy people own a private jet." "Nearly everyone in the hospital with this condition tested positive" is not "nearly everyone who tests positive has this condition." The two conditional probabilities can differ by orders of magnitude, and which one you need depends entirely on what you already know.

Two famous interpretations that are conditional-probability errors. A p-value is P(data at least this extreme | the null hypothesis is true). It is not the probability that the null hypothesis is true, and it is not the probability your results were a fluke — those would require flipping the bar, which the p-value does not license. Likewise, a 95% confidence interval is a statement about the long-run behavior of the procedure: if you repeated the study many times, about 95% of the intervals built this way would contain the true parameter. It is not a 95% chance that the parameter sits inside this particular interval — the parameter is a fixed number, and this interval either caught it or it did not.
Interactive Build a screening test with live sliders, then condition on a positive result, a negative result, or the disease itself — a probability tree, a re-scaling bar, and a real seeded simulation show the denominator shrinking and the answer moving with it. Open full ↑
Key points
  • P(A|B) = P(A and B) ÷ P(B) — conditioning replaces the denominator with the given event.
  • Independence means P(A|B) = P(A); dependence means the condition moves the number.
  • P(A|B) ≠ P(B|A) in general — reversing the bar reverses the question.
  • A p-value is P(data | null true), not P(null true | data); a 95% CI describes the long-run procedure, not this one interval.

Section 5Two-way tables and trees

Reading probabilities off a two-way table

A two-way table (or contingency table) cross-classifies a group of individuals by two categorical variables at once. It is the single most useful object in this chapter, because every probability we have defined can be read straight off it — you never need a formula, only the right denominator.

Suppose 300 job applicants are classified by whether they hold a professional certification and whether they received an offer:

Received offerNo offerRow total
Certified9060150
Not certified45105150
Column total135165300

Three kinds of probability live in this table, and they differ only in what you divide by.

Joint probabilities use a single inner cell over the grand total — both things happening together:

  • P(certified and offer) = 90/300 = 0.300
  • P(not certified and no offer) = 105/300 = 0.350

Marginal probabilities use a row or column total over the grand total — one variable, ignoring the other. They are called marginal because they live in the margins of the table:

  • P(certified) = 150/300 = 0.500
  • P(offer) = 135/300 = 0.450

Conditional probabilities use a cell over a row or column total — you have restricted the world to one line of the table:

  • P(offer | certified) = 90/150 = 0.600  (denominator: the 150 certified applicants)
  • P(offer | not certified) = 45/150 = 0.300  (denominator: the 150 uncertified applicants)
  • P(certified | offer) = 90/135 ≈ 0.667  (denominator: the 135 applicants with offers)

Look hard at the last two bullets. P(offer | certified) = 0.600 and P(certified | offer) ≈ 0.667 come from the same cell, 90 — but from different denominators, so they are different numbers answering different questions. That is the confusion of the inverse, made visible.

The table also answers the other rules. The addition rule: P(certified or offer) = 0.500 + 0.450 − 0.300 = 0.650; verify by counting cells, 90 + 60 + 45 = 195, and 195/300 = 0.650. The independence test: P(certified)P(offer) = 0.500 × 0.450 = 0.225, but the actual joint probability is 0.300. Since 0.225 ≠ 0.300, certification and receiving an offer are dependent in this group. The conditional probabilities say the same thing more vividly: 0.600 versus 0.300 — certified applicants got offers at twice the rate.

The same data as a tree

A tree diagram takes the same information and stages it in time. The first set of branches splits on the first variable; each second-stage branch is conditional on the branch above it. Two rules govern trees, and they are just the multiplication and addition rules wearing different clothes:

  • Multiply along a path to get the probability of that complete path (this is P(A) × P(B|A)).
  • Add across paths that produce the outcome you want (this works because different paths are mutually exclusive).

Here is our applicant data as a tree. Stage 1 branches are the marginal probabilities of certification; stage 2 branches are the conditional probabilities of an offer given that certification status.

PathStage 1: certificationStage 2: offer, given stage 1Path probabilityCheck against counts
Certified → Offer0.5000.6000.500 × 0.600 = 0.30090/300 = 0.300
Certified → No offer0.5000.4000.500 × 0.400 = 0.20060/300 = 0.200
Not certified → Offer0.5000.3000.500 × 0.300 = 0.15045/300 = 0.150
Not certified → No offer0.5000.7000.500 × 0.700 = 0.350105/300 = 0.350
Total1.000300/300

The path probabilities sum to 1.000, which is your arithmetic check — a tree whose ends do not total 1 has an error in it.

Working the tree backwards

Adding across paths gives the law of total probability. To find the overall chance of an offer, collect every path that ends in an offer:

P(offer) = 0.300 + 0.150 = 0.450

which is exactly the marginal 135/300 we read off the table. Written out in full, this is P(offer) = P(cert)P(offer|cert) + P(no cert)P(offer|no cert) = (0.500)(0.600) + (0.500)(0.300) = 0.300 + 0.150 = 0.450.

Now the genuinely useful move: trees let you reverse the conditioning. Suppose you meet an applicant who received an offer and want the probability that they are certified — that is P(certified | offer), which is the opposite direction from the branches you were given. Use the definition of conditional probability, taking the numerator from the one path with both features and the denominator from the total you just computed:

P(certified | offer) = 0.300 ÷ 0.450 = 2/3 ≈ 0.667

which matches 90/135 from the table. This reversal is the whole engine behind Bayes' rule, and you have just done it with nothing but division. The pattern is always the same: the path you care about, divided by all the paths that end the same way.

Whenever a percentage confuses you, ask "percent of what?" "Sixty percent of certified applicants got offers" and "sixty-seven percent of offer-holders were certified" are both true of this table and sound almost identical, yet they have different denominators and support different conclusions. And note what none of these numbers establish: certification and offers are associated here, but this table cannot tell you that certification caused the offers — the certified group may differ in experience, field, or persistence. Association is not causation, no matter how clean the arithmetic.
Interactive Read joint, marginal, and conditional probabilities off a live contingency table — set the population with sliders, draw a real seeded sample, then tap any cell and watch the denominator shrink from the whole table to a single row or column. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Joint = cell ÷ grand total; marginal = row or column total ÷ grand total; conditional = cell ÷ row or column total.
  • Test independence from a table by comparing P(A and B) with P(A)P(B), or P(A|B) with P(A).
  • In a tree, multiply along a path and add across paths; all path probabilities must sum to 1.
  • Reverse the conditioning by dividing the path you want by the sum of all paths with that ending — the law of total probability supplies the denominator.

The chapter in one breath

  • The sample space is every possible outcome; an event is a subset of it; for equally likely outcomes, probability is just counting: outcomes in A over outcomes in S.
  • Probabilities live between 0 and 1, sum to 1 over a complete list, and mean a long-run relative frequency — nothing is ever "due."
  • The complement rule, P(Ac) = 1 − P(A), turns hard events into easy ones — especially "at least one."
  • Addition rule: P(A or B) = P(A) + P(B) − P(A and B); the subtraction disappears only when the events are mutually exclusive.
  • Multiplication rule: P(A and B) = P(A) × P(B|A); the conditioning disappears only when the events are independent.
  • Mutually exclusive ≠ independent — disjoint events with nonzero probability are in fact strongly dependent.
  • Conditional probability shrinks the denominator to the given event, and P(A|B) is not P(B|A) — the error behind misread p-values and misread test results alike.
  • Two-way tables and trees hold every one of these rules at once: joint, marginal, and conditional probabilities differ only in what you divide by.

ReferenceWords worth knowing

These are the terms that will keep appearing for the rest of the book — skim them now, and come back whenever a symbol stops making sense.

Addition rule
P(A or B) = P(A) + P(B) − P(A and B). The subtraction removes outcomes counted twice; it drops out when the events are mutually exclusive.
Complement
The event that A does not occur, written Ac. P(Ac) = 1 − P(A).
Conditional probability
The probability of A given that B has occurred: P(A|B) = P(A and B) ÷ P(B). Conditioning replaces the denominator with the given event.
Contingency table (two-way table)
A table cross-classifying individuals by two categorical variables, with row, column, and grand totals. Joint, marginal, and conditional probabilities can all be read from it.
Dependent events
Events for which P(A|B) ≠ P(A) — knowing one occurred changes the probability of the other.
Disjoint events
Another name for mutually exclusive events: they cannot both happen on the same trial, so P(A and B) = 0.
Event
Any collection of outcomes from the sample space, usually named with a capital letter.
Independent events
Events for which P(A|B) = P(A), equivalently P(A and B) = P(A)P(B). Knowing one tells you nothing about the other.
Intersection
The event that both A and B occur, written A ∩ B or "A and B."
Joint probability
The probability that two events occur together, P(A and B). On a two-way table it is a single inner cell divided by the grand total.
Law of large numbers
As the number of independent trials grows, the observed proportion of an event converges to its true probability. It does not imply that a process corrects past imbalances.
Law of total probability
Breaking an event into mutually exclusive cases and adding: P(B) = P(A)P(B|A) + P(Ac)P(B|Ac). On a tree, it is adding across all paths that end the same way.
Marginal probability
The probability of one event ignoring the other, P(A). On a two-way table it is a row or column total divided by the grand total.
Multiplication rule
P(A and B) = P(A) × P(B|A); it simplifies to P(A) × P(B) when the events are independent.
Mutually exclusive
Events that cannot occur on the same trial, so P(A and B) = 0. Not the same as independent — in fact the opposite in spirit.
Outcome
A single possible result of one run of a random process.
Probability
A number between 0 and 1 giving the long-run relative frequency with which an event occurs.
Random process
A situation whose set of possible outcomes is known but whose particular result is not determined in advance.
Replacement (with and without)
Whether a selected item is returned before the next selection. With replacement the draws are independent; without replacement they are dependent.
Sample space
The complete, non-overlapping list of all possible outcomes of a random process, written S. All its probabilities sum to 1.
Tree diagram
A staged picture of a random process where you multiply probabilities along a path and add across paths; second-stage branches are conditional on the first.
Union
The event that A occurs, B occurs, or both, written A ∪ B or "A or B." In statistics "or" is always inclusive.

Check yourself

1. Define the sample space, then list it for the process "flip a fair coin twice." How many outcomes are there, and what is the most common wrong answer?
The sample space is the complete, non-overlapping list of possible outcomes: S = {HH, HT, TH, TT}, so 4 outcomes. The common error is answering 3 by merging HT and TH into "one of each." They are distinct outcomes of the process, and collapsing them makes "one head, one tail" look like 1/3 instead of its true 2/4 = 0.500.
2. A fair six-sided die is rolled once. Find P(the roll is greater than 4), and state the rule you used.
P = 2/6 = 1/3 ≈ 0.333. The outcomes greater than 4 are {5, 6}, so 2 favorable outcomes out of 6 equally likely ones — the basic formula P(A) = (outcomes in A) ÷ (outcomes in S).
3. Two fair dice are rolled. Show the work for P(sum = 7) and P(sum = 2). Why are these different when each sum is "one number"?
P(sum = 7) = 6/36 = 1/6 ≈ 0.167; P(sum = 2) = 1/36 ≈ 0.028. The sample space has 36 equally likely ordered pairs. Seven can be made six ways — (1,6), (2,5), (3,4), (4,3), (5,2), (6,1) — while two can be made only one way, (1,1). The equally likely units are the pairs, not the sums.
4. A fair coin is flipped three times. Find P(at least one head) without listing all eight outcomes.
P(at least one head) = 1 − (1/2)3 = 1 − 1/8 = 7/8 = 0.875. The complement of "at least one head" is "no heads at all," i.e. TTT, whose probability is (1/2)(1/2)(1/2) = 1/8 because the flips are independent. Subtract from 1.
5. One card is drawn from a standard 52-card deck. Compute P(heart or face card), showing every term.
P = 13/52 + 12/52 − 3/52 = 22/52 = 11/26 ≈ 0.423. There are 13 hearts and 12 face cards, but 3 cards (jack, queen, king of hearts) are both, so they were counted twice and must be subtracted once. Forgetting the subtraction gives 25/52 ≈ 0.481, which overcounts by exactly those 3 cards.
6. Are "the card is a heart" and "the card is a face card" mutually exclusive? Are "the card is a heart" and "the card is a spade"?
Heart and face card are not mutually exclusive (3 cards are both, so P(A and B) = 3/52 ≠ 0). Heart and spade are mutually exclusive — no card has two suits, so P(A and B) = 0. For the second pair the addition rule simplifies to 13/52 + 13/52 = 26/52 = 0.500.
7. Explain the difference between "mutually exclusive" and "independent." Can two events with nonzero probability be both?
Mutually exclusive means they cannot co-occur, P(A and B) = 0. Independent means knowing one occurred does not change the other's probability, P(A|B) = P(A). No — with nonzero probabilities they cannot be both. If they are disjoint, then P(A|B) = 0, which cannot equal P(A) when P(A) > 0. Disjointness is a very strong form of dependence: learning B happened tells you A definitely did not.
8. Two cards are drawn without replacement. Find P(both are aces), and compare with the answer if the first card were replaced.
Without replacement: (4/52) × (3/51) = (1/13)(1/17) = 1/221 ≈ 0.00452. With replacement: (4/52) × (4/52) = (1/13)2 = 1/169 ≈ 0.00592. Without replacement the second draw is conditional — only 3 aces remain in 51 cards — so use P(A) × P(B|A). With replacement the draws are independent, so use P(A) × P(B).
9. A fair die is rolled four times. Find P(at least one six). Why is "4 × 1/6" wrong?
P(at least one six) = 1 − (5/6)4 = 1 − 625/1296 = 671/1296 ≈ 0.518. Each roll has P(no six) = 5/6 and the rolls are independent, so P(no six at all) = (5/6)4 ≈ 0.482. Adding 1/6 per roll is wrong because it double-counts rolls where more than one six appears — and it breaks visibly at eight rolls, where it would give 8/6 = 1.33, an impossible probability.
10. Roll one fair die. Let A = "a 6" and B = "even." Compute P(A|B) and P(B|A), and explain why they differ.
P(A|B) = (1/6) ÷ (1/2) = 1/3 ≈ 0.333; P(B|A) = (1/6) ÷ (1/6) = 1.000. They differ because they have different denominators: given "even," the live outcomes are {2, 4, 6} and one of three is a six; given "a 6," the only live outcome is 6, which is certainly even. Reversing the bar reverses the question — this is the confusion of the inverse.
11. From the chapter's table of 300 applicants (certified: 90 offers, 60 none; not certified: 45 offers, 105 none), find P(offer | not certified) and P(not certified | no offer), then decide whether the two variables are independent.
P(offer | not certified) = 45/150 = 0.300. P(not certified | no offer) = 105/165 ≈ 0.636. They are not independent. The independence test: P(certified)P(offer) = 0.500 × 0.450 = 0.225, but the observed joint probability is 90/300 = 0.300, and 0.225 ≠ 0.300. Equivalently, P(offer | certified) = 0.600 while P(offer) = 0.450 — the condition moved the number. Note the association does not by itself prove certification caused the offers.
12. A colleague reads a study with p = 0.03 and says, "So there is only a 3% chance the null hypothesis is true, and a 95% chance the true value is inside their confidence interval." Correct both statements.
Both reverse the conditioning. A p-value is P(data at least this extreme | the null hypothesis is true) — not P(null is true | data). And a 95% confidence interval describes the long-run performance of the procedure: about 95% of intervals built this way would capture the true parameter. A specific interval either contains the fixed parameter or it does not; there is no 95% chance attached to that one interval. Both errors are the same mistake as treating P(A|B) as P(B|A).
Statistics · Chapter 6

Discrete Random Variables

Up to now, probability has been about single events — one card, one coin, one roll. This chapter takes the next step: instead of asking about one outcome, we describe every outcome at once, with a number attached to each and a probability attached to each number. That package is called a probability distribution, and once you have one you can answer almost anything — how likely a result is, what to expect on average, and how far from average things typically wander. Three famous distributions do an enormous amount of the work in real life, and by the end of this chapter you will be able to recognize which one fits a situation and compute with it. None of this requires clever tricks; it requires a table, a formula, and the patience to work one line at a time.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • Define a random variable and use the notation X (the variable) versus x (a value it takes).
  • Classify a random variable as discrete or continuous and defend the choice.
  • State the two requirements of a valid probability distribution and test a table against them.
  • Compute probabilities of compound events — "at least," "at most," "more than" — from a distribution table.
  • Compute the mean (μ), variance (σ2), and standard deviation (σ) of a discrete random variable, and interpret μ as a long-run average.
  • State the four requirements of a binomial experiment and apply the binomial probability formula.
  • Use μ = np and σ = √(npq) for a binomial, and judge whether a result is unusual.
  • Recognize when the Poisson or geometric distribution applies and compute probabilities from each.

Section 1Random variables

From outcome to number

A random variable is a variable whose value is a number determined by the outcome of a chance process. That is the whole definition, and it is worth reading twice, because two ordinary words are doing precise work. Number: the result must be numeric, so if the raw outcome is "heads" or "defective," we have to convert it — count the heads, count the defects. Random: we do not know in advance which value will occur, though we may know how likely each one is.

Statisticians use a small notational habit that pays off immediately. A capital letter, usually X, names the random variable itself — the not-yet-known quantity. A lowercase letter, x, names a particular value it might take. So "X = the number of heads in three coin flips" defines the variable, and "x = 2" is one of its possible values. The expression P(X = x) then reads, in plain English, "the probability that the random variable turns out to equal this specific number." Keep the capital and lowercase straight and every formula in this chapter becomes readable.

Discrete: you can count the values

A random variable is discrete if its possible values can be listed — first value, second value, third value, and so on — with nothing squeezed in between. Counts are the classic case: the number of students absent, the number of defective units in a shipment, the number of calls arriving in an hour. You can have 3 absences or 4 absences, never 3.6.

A common misreading is that "discrete" means "only a few values." It does not. A discrete random variable may have infinitely many possible values, as long as they can be counted off in order. The number of calls arriving at a help desk in an hour could in principle be 0, 1, 2, 3, and onward forever — that list never ends, but it is still a list, so the variable is discrete. What matters is countability, not size.

Continuous: you can only measure them

A random variable is continuous if it can take any value in an interval, so that between any two possible values there is always another. Measurements behave this way: height, weight, elapsed time, temperature, blood pressure. Between 70.1 kg and 70.2 kg lies 70.15 kg, and between those lies 70.125 kg, without end.

The practical test is not "does the number have decimals?" but "was it counted or measured?" A reported time of 12.4 seconds looks like a decimal because our stopwatch stops there, but the underlying quantity is continuous — the rounding is a limitation of the instrument, not a property of the variable. Conversely, a dollar amount of $4.75 is technically discrete (money comes in cents), yet money is usually treated as continuous because the steps are so fine. Both facts can be true at once: the mathematical classification is exact, and the modeling choice is a judgment call.

Random variableCounted or measured?Possible valuesType
Number of heads in 3 flipsCounted0, 1, 2, 3Discrete (finite)
Number of emails received todayCounted0, 1, 2, 3, …Discrete (infinite but countable)
Number of cars in a householdCounted0, 1, 2, 3, …Discrete
Time to finish an examMeasuredAny value in (0, 120] minutesContinuous
Weight of a cereal boxMeasuredAny value near 500 gContinuous
Body temperatureMeasuredAny value in a rangeContinuous
Why the distinction earns its keep. For a discrete variable, P(X = 2) is a real, usually nonzero number you can look up in a table. For a continuous variable, P(X = 70.000… kg) is zero — there are infinitely many possible values, so no single one carries probability. With continuous variables we can only ask about intervals: P(69.5 < X < 70.5). That is why this chapter uses tables and formulas, and why the next one uses areas under a curve.
Interactive Sample ten random variables and record each at finer and finer precision: the counts run out of new values while the measurements never do, and the chance of any one exact value collapses to zero as the chance of landing in an interval settles down. Open full ↑
Key points
  • A random variable assigns a number to each outcome of a chance process; X is the variable, x is a value.
  • Discrete = values can be listed (usually counts); continuous = values fill an interval (usually measurements).
  • Discrete does not mean "few" — 0, 1, 2, 3, … forever is still discrete.
  • For continuous variables, the probability of any single exact value is 0; we ask about intervals instead.

Section 2Probability distributions

The two rules

A probability distribution lists every possible value of a discrete random variable together with the probability of each. It can be written as a table, as a formula, or drawn as a probability histogram whose bar heights are the probabilities. Whatever the format, a distribution is valid only if it satisfies exactly two requirements — and these two are not arbitrary rules to memorize; each one restates something you already believe about probability.

Requirement 1: ΣP(x) = 1. The probabilities of all the values must add up to exactly 1. This says the list is complete: something has to happen, and every "something" is on the list. (In practice, allow for rounding — a table of values rounded to three decimals may total 0.999 or 1.001, and that is fine.)

Requirement 2: 0 ≤ P(x) ≤ 1 for every value x. Each individual probability must be a legitimate probability — never negative, never above 1. A single 1.4 or −0.05 anywhere in the table kills it, no matter what the total comes to.

A table fails if it breaks either rule. Suppose someone offers P(0) = 0.30, P(1) = 0.45, P(2) = 0.15. The individual values are all legal, but they sum to 0.90, so 10% of the probability has gone missing — some outcome was left off the list. Now suppose P(0) = 0.60, P(1) = 0.55, P(2) = −0.15. These do sum to 1.00, but a negative probability is meaningless, so this table is invalid too.

Reading probabilities off a distribution

Let X be the number of heads in three flips of a fair coin. The eight equally likely outcomes are HHH, HHT, HTH, THH, HTT, THT, TTH, TTT. Counting heads in each gives the distribution below, and because each of the eight outcomes has probability 1/8, the probabilities are just counts out of 8.

x (heads)OutcomesP(x) as a fractionP(x) as a decimal
0TTT1/80.125
1HTT, THT, TTH3/80.375
2HHT, HTH, THH3/80.375
3HHH1/80.125
Total8 outcomes8/8 = 11.000

Check the two rules: every probability sits between 0 and 1, and the total is exactly 1. Valid. Now the payoff — once a distribution is in hand, compound events are just addition, because the values are mutually exclusive.

"At most 1 head" means X = 0 or X = 1, so P(X ≤ 1) = 0.125 + 0.375 = 0.500.
"At least 2 heads" means X = 2 or X = 3, so P(X ≥ 2) = 0.375 + 0.125 = 0.500.
"At least 1 head" would mean adding 0.375 + 0.375 + 0.125 = 0.875 — but the complement is faster: P(X ≥ 1) = 1 − P(X = 0) = 1 − 0.125 = 0.875. Same answer, one subtraction.

That complement trick is worth building into your reflexes. Whenever a question says "at least one," compute 1 − P(none). With three values it saves a little effort; with a binomial of 40 trials it saves you from adding forty terms.

Filling in a missing probability

Because the probabilities must total 1, one missing entry is always recoverable. Suppose X is the number of roommates a randomly selected student has, and a survey reports P(0) = 0.15, P(1) = 0.30, P(2) = k, P(3) = 0.25, with no other values possible. Then:

0.15 + 0.30 + k + 0.25 = 1
0.70 + k = 1
k = 1 − 0.70 = 0.30

Always finish by confirming the recovered value is itself a legal probability. Here 0.30 lies between 0 and 1, so the completed table is valid. If the algebra had produced −0.10, that would not mean "probability can be negative" — it would mean the reported numbers were wrong.

The histogram is the distribution. A probability histogram plots the values x along the bottom and P(x) as bar heights. Because the probabilities sum to 1, the bars together account for exactly 100% of what can happen — which is why, in the next chapter, "probability" becomes "area under a curve." The idea does not change; only the picture does.
Interactive Set each probability with a slider and watch the total re-compute live as both requirements are tested — every entry between 0 and 1, and the entries summing to exactly 1 — with candidate tables that pass one test while failing the other, and real seeded sampling from the ones that qualify. Open full ↑
Key points
  • A probability distribution pairs every possible value of a discrete random variable with its probability.
  • Rule 1: ΣP(x) = 1 (allowing for rounding). Rule 2: 0 ≤ P(x) ≤ 1 for every x. Both must hold.
  • Compound events are found by adding the probabilities of the values they include.
  • For "at least one," use the complement: P(X ≥ 1) = 1 − P(X = 0).

Section 3Expected value

The mean of a random variable is a weighted average

The mean of a discrete random variable, written μ and also called the expected value E(X), answers the question: if this chance process were repeated over and over, what would the average result settle down to? The formula is short:

μ = E(X) = Σ [ x · P(x) ]

Read it as an instruction: multiply each value by its probability, then add up those products. It is an average weighted by likelihood — values that happen often pull the mean toward themselves; values that happen rarely barely budge it. Notice we do not divide by anything at the end. The dividing is already built in, because the weights P(x) sum to 1.

Let X be the number of pets in a randomly selected household, with the distribution P(0) = 0.40, P(1) = 0.30, P(2) = 0.20, P(3) = 0.10. (Check: 0.40 + 0.30 + 0.20 + 0.10 = 1.00, and every entry is between 0 and 1 — valid.) Then:

μ = (0)(0.40) + (1)(0.30) + (2)(0.20) + (3)(0.10)
μ = 0 + 0.30 + 0.40 + 0.30
μ = 1.0 pet

Now the interpretation, which is where students most often go wrong. The expected value is not a prediction about the next household, and it need not even be a possible value. If the distribution had come out to μ = 1.3 pets, no household would ever own 1.3 pets; the number still describes what the average approaches across many, many households. Expected value is a statement about the long run, not about any single trial — that is the law of large numbers, and it is the whole reason insurers and casinos can plan a year in advance while having no idea what happens tonight.

Variance and standard deviation

The mean tells you where the distribution sits; it says nothing about how tightly the values cluster around it. That job belongs to the variance σ2 and the standard deviation σ. The definitional formula measures the average squared distance from the mean, again weighted by probability:

σ2 = Σ [ (x − μ)2 · P(x) ]   and   σ = √(σ2)

There is an algebraically identical shortcut that is far easier to compute, because it never requires subtracting the mean inside the sum:

σ2 = Σ [ x2 · P(x) ] − μ2

Both give the same answer; use whichever you find clearer. Here is the pet example carried all the way through in one table — build the columns left to right and the arithmetic takes care of itself.

xP(x)x · P(x)x2x2 · P(x)(x − μ)2 · P(x)
00.400.0000.00(0−1)2(0.40) = 0.40
10.300.3010.30(1−1)2(0.30) = 0.00
20.200.4040.80(2−1)2(0.20) = 0.20
30.100.3090.90(3−1)2(0.10) = 0.40
Totals1.00μ = 1.00—2.00σ2 = 1.00

By the shortcut: σ2 = 2.00 − (1.00)2 = 2.00 − 1.00 = 1.00. By the definition, the last column also totals 1.00. The two agree, as they must. Then σ = √1.00 = 1.0 pet.

Units matter here. The variance came out in "squared pets," which means nothing to anyone, and that is precisely why we take the square root: σ is back in the original units and can be read as "a typical distance from the mean." So households average 1.0 pet, and a typical household differs from that average by about 1.0 pet.

A rough but useful convention, the range rule of thumb, treats values more than two standard deviations from the mean as unusual. Here that boundary is μ ± 2σ = 1.0 ± 2.0, an interval from −1.0 to 3.0 pets. No possible value falls outside it — 3 sits exactly on the upper boundary — so nothing in this distribution counts as unusual — which fits: a household with 3 pets is uncommon, but not startling.

Expected value as a decision tool

Expected value earns its name in situations involving money, where it tells you what a repeated bet, policy, or purchase is worth per play. Suppose a club sells 500 raffle tickets at $3 each, with one $1,000 prize. What is the expected value of buying one ticket?

Define X as your net gain, which means the $3 you paid is subtracted in both branches. If you win, you gain 1000 − 3 = $997, and that happens with probability 1/500. If you lose, you gain −$3, with probability 499/500.

E(X) = (997)(1/500) + (−3)(499/500)
E(X) = 997/500 − 1497/500
E(X) = −500/500 = −$1.00

The interpretation is exact and slightly deflating: you cannot lose exactly $1 on this raffle — you will either be up $997 or down $3 — but if you bought a ticket in this same raffle over and over, your average result would settle near a loss of $1.00 per ticket. The club's side of the same arithmetic is the mirror image: it collects $3 per ticket and pays out $1,000 spread over 500 tickets, or $2 per ticket, so it clears an average net of $1.00 per ticket — exactly what the buyer loses. (Note that $1.00 is the club's net per ticket, not its revenue; revenue is the full $3.) That balance is how the fundraiser was planned in the first place.

"Expected" is a technical word, not an everyday one. The expected value is what the average converges to over many repetitions — it is not what you should expect to happen next, and it is often a value that cannot occur at all (1.3 pets, −$1.00 on a raffle). Say "the long-run average is …" out loud when you interpret μ, and you will never misstate it.
Interactive Compute E(X) = Σ x·P(x) line by line for seven real games, change the price or the prizes and watch the answer move, then play thousands of simulated rounds and watch the running average settle onto the number the formula predicted. Open full ↑
Key points
  • μ = E(X) = Σ[x · P(x)] — a probability-weighted average; no final division is needed.
  • σ2 = Σ[(x − μ)2P(x)] = Σ[x2P(x)] − μ2; then σ = √(σ2) to return to the original units.
  • μ describes the long run, not the next trial, and need not be a value the variable can actually take.
  • By the range rule of thumb, values outside μ ± 2σ are considered unusual.

Section 4The binomial distribution

The four requirements

The binomial distribution is the most heavily used discrete distribution in statistics, because an enormous number of real questions have the same skeleton: we repeat something a fixed number of times, and we count how often it works. A situation qualifies as a binomial experiment only if all four of the following hold. Miss one and the formula in the next subsection gives a wrong answer.

#RequirementWhat it meansWhat breaks it
1Fixed number of trialsn is decided in advance and does not depend on results"Keep testing until 3 fail" — n is not fixed
2Two outcomes per trialEach trial is a success or a failure — nothing elseA five-level rating scale (unless collapsed to two)
3Independent trialsOne trial's result does not change any other's probabilityDrawing cards without replacement
4Constant probability pThe success probability is identical on every trialA test-taker who learns as the quiz goes on

Two conventions clear up most confusion. First, "success" is just a label for the outcome you chose to count — it can be a defect, a failure, or a death. If you are counting defective parts, a defect is a "success," and p is the probability of a defect. Second, p is the probability of success on one trial, q = 1 − p is the probability of failure, and x is the number of successes in the n trials, so x can be any whole number from 0 to n.

The binomial formula

P(X = x) = nCx · px · qn−x   where   nCx = n! / [ x! (n − x)! ]

The formula is three ideas multiplied together, and it stops looking intimidating once you see them separately. The piece px is the probability of getting x successes; qn−x is the probability of the remaining n − x failures; multiplying them is legal precisely because the trials are independent (requirement 3). But that product is the probability of one specific arrangement, such as success-success-failure-failure. The binomial coefficient nCx counts how many arrangements produce x successes, and since each arrangement has the same probability, we multiply by that count.

Worked example. A quiz has 6 multiple-choice questions with 4 choices each. A student guesses on every question. What is the probability of exactly 2 correct?

Check the four requirements: n = 6 fixed ✓; each question is right or wrong ✓; guesses do not affect one another ✓; p = 1/4 = 0.25 on every question ✓. So n = 6, p = 0.25, q = 0.75, x = 2.

Step 1 — the coefficient: 6C2 = 6! / (2! · 4!) = (6 × 5) / (2 × 1) = 30/2 = 15.
Step 2 — the successes: (0.25)2 = 0.0625.
Step 3 — the failures: (0.75)4 = 0.31640625.
Step 4 — multiply: 15 × 0.0625 × 0.31640625 = 0.9375 × 0.31640625 = 0.2966.

So there is about a 29.7% chance of exactly 2 correct. Running the same formula for every value of x produces the complete distribution:

x correct6Cx(0.25)x(0.75)6−xP(x)
010.1779790.1780
160.0593260.3560
2150.0197750.2966
3200.0065920.1318
4150.0021970.0330
560.0007320.0044
610.0002440.0002
Total——1.0000

The column totals 1.0000, confirming this is a valid probability distribution — a good habit whenever you build a binomial table by hand. Compound questions now work exactly as in Section 2. "What is the probability of at least 1 correct?" Use the complement: P(X ≥ 1) = 1 − P(0) = 1 − 0.1780 = 0.8220. "At least 4 correct?" Add the tail: 0.0330 + 0.0044 + 0.0002 = 0.0376, under 4%.

Mean and standard deviation of a binomial

You could find μ and σ by running the Section 3 machinery on all seven rows — but for a binomial there are shortcuts, and they are exact, not approximations:

μ = np    σ2 = npq    σ = √(npq)

For the guessing student: μ = np = (6)(0.25) = 1.5 correct, which is sensible — guess on 6 questions with a 1-in-4 chance and you average one and a half. And σ = √[(6)(0.25)(0.75)] = √1.125 = 1.06 correct.

Apply the range rule of thumb: μ ± 2σ = 1.5 ± 2.12, an interval from −0.62 to 3.62. Scores of 0, 1, 2, or 3 are ordinary for a guesser; a score of 4 or more is unusual. That verdict matches the exact tail probability we just computed, P(X ≥ 4) = 0.0376. The rule of thumb and the exact calculation agree, which is the point of having both: the rule is quick, the calculation is precise.

The 5% rule for sampling without replacement. Strictly, drawing 20 people from a town of 40,000 without replacement violates independence — each draw changes the pool slightly. In practice, if the sample is no more than 5% of the population, the change is negligible and the binomial is used anyway. That is why polling of a large population is treated as binomial, while drawing 3 cards from a 52-card deck (nearly 6% of the deck, with sharply shifting probabilities) is not.
Interactive Drag n and p and watch the binomial distribution reshape in real time — with exact probabilities and worked formulas, cumulative "at most / at least" tails, a normal-curve overlay, and a live simulation whose bars converge onto the theory. Open full ↑
Key points
  • A binomial experiment needs all four: fixed n, two outcomes, independent trials, constant p.
  • P(X = x) = nCx px qn−x — arrangements × successes × failures.
  • "Success" is only a label for whatever you are counting; p must match that label.
  • μ = np and σ = √(npq); sampling under 5% of a population may be treated as binomial.

Section 5Other discrete distributions

The Poisson distribution

The binomial needs a fixed number of trials. But many real counts have no trials to fix: calls arriving at a help desk in an hour, typos on a page, potholes per kilometer of road. There is no n, because there is no upper limit on how many could occur. For these, the Poisson distribution models the number of events in a fixed interval of time, area, volume, or distance, given a known average rate.

P(X = x) = ( λx · e−λ ) / x!    for x = 0, 1, 2, 3, …

Here λ (lambda) is the mean number of events per interval, and e ≈ 2.71828 is the natural base. The distribution needs three conditions: the events occur independently, the average rate λ is constant over the interval, and the interval is fixed. It has one striking property — a single parameter controls everything:

μ = λ    σ2 = λ    σ = √λ

The mean and the variance are the same number. That is a genuinely useful diagnostic: if you have count data whose sample variance is far larger than its mean, the Poisson model is probably wrong for it.

Worked example. A help desk receives an average of 3 calls per hour. What is the probability of exactly 5 calls in a given hour?

Here λ = 3 and x = 5.
Step 1: λx = 35 = 243.
Step 2: e−3 = 0.049787.
Step 3: x! = 5! = 5 × 4 × 3 × 2 × 1 = 120.
Step 4: P(X = 5) = (243 × 0.049787) / 120 = 12.0983 / 120 = 0.1008.

So about a 10.1% chance of exactly 5 calls. The chance of a completely quiet hour is P(X = 0) = (30 · e−3)/0! = 0.049787, roughly 5% — recall that 30 = 1 and 0! = 1. And the chance of at least one call is the complement: 1 − 0.0498 = 0.9502. The standard deviation is σ = √3 ≈ 1.73 calls.

One more use worth knowing: when a binomial has a large n and a small p — a common rule of thumb is n ≥ 100 and np ≤ 10 — the Poisson with λ = np closely approximates it. This is why Poisson models rare events so well: accidents at an intersection, rare defects on a production line, unusual claims filed with an insurer.

The geometric distribution

The geometric distribution flips the binomial question around. A binomial fixes the number of trials and lets the number of successes vary. A geometric fixes the number of successes at exactly one — the first one — and lets the number of trials vary. It answers: how many attempts until the first success?

P(X = x) = qx−1 · p    for x = 1, 2, 3, …

The logic is transparent: to have the first success on trial x, you need x − 1 failures first (probability qx−1) followed by one success (probability p). There is no coefficient because there is only one arrangement that works. The requirements are the binomial's minus the fixed n: independent trials, two outcomes, and a constant p. Its mean and standard deviation are:

μ = 1/p    σ2 = q/p2    σ = √q / p

Worked example. A representative converts 20% of the calls she makes, independently. What is the probability her first conversion comes on the 4th call?

Here p = 0.20, q = 0.80, x = 4.
P(X = 4) = (0.80)3 × 0.20 = 0.512 × 0.20 = 0.1024, about 10.2%.

How many calls does it take on average to get that first conversion? μ = 1/p = 1/0.20 = 5 calls — the reciprocal, exactly as intuition suggests. Read that carefully: μ counts the successful call itself, so the average number of failed calls preceding the first conversion is 4, not 5. The spread is σ = √0.80 / 0.20 = 0.894/0.20 = 4.47 calls, which is large relative to the mean; waiting times of this kind are notoriously variable. And the probability she needs more than 3 calls is simply the probability the first 3 all fail: P(X > 3) = (0.80)3 = 0.512. So P(X ≤ 3) = 1 − 0.512 = 0.488.

One caution when reading other sources: some define the geometric variable as the number of failures before the first success, so it starts at 0 instead of 1 and has mean q/p. The mathematics is the same; only the starting point shifts. Always check which version a formula sheet or calculator is using before plugging in.

Choosing among the three

Nearly every discrete problem you meet at this level fits one of these three, and the fastest way to identify which is to ask what is fixed and what is random.

DistributionWhat is fixedWhat is randomValues of XMeanVariance
BinomialNumber of trials nNumber of successes0, 1, …, nnpnpq
PoissonThe interval (time, area, …)Number of events0, 1, 2, …λλ
GeometricNumber of successes (one)Number of trials needed1, 2, 3, …1/pq/p2
The coin has no memory. The geometric distribution is memoryless: if the first 9 calls all failed, the probability that the 10th converts is still 0.20 — unchanged. Independence means past failures build no credit toward a future success. The belief that a run of bad luck makes success "due" is the gambler's fallacy, and the geometric distribution is where it is most tempting and most wrong.
Interactive Two distributions with no fixed n: drag one parameter and watch the histogram rebuild live — Poisson counting events in a window, geometric counting trials until the first success — with exact probabilities, a decision guide for choosing between them, a live memorylessness check, and a real seeded simulation that converges on the theory. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Poisson: P(x) = λxe−λ/x! counts events in a fixed interval; uniquely, μ = σ2 = λ.
  • Poisson approximates a binomial when n is large and p is small, using λ = np.
  • Geometric: P(x) = qx−1p counts trials until the first success, with μ = 1/p.
  • Identify the distribution by asking what is fixed (trials, interval, or successes) and what is random.

The chapter in one breath

  • A random variable attaches a number to each outcome of a chance process; it is discrete if its values can be listed (counts) and continuous if they fill an interval (measurements).
  • A probability distribution is valid only if ΣP(x) = 1 and 0 ≤ P(x) ≤ 1 for every value; both rules must hold.
  • Compound events are sums of value probabilities, and "at least one" is fastest as 1 − P(0).
  • The expected value μ = Σ[xP(x)] is a probability-weighted average describing the long run — not the next trial, and not necessarily a possible value.
  • Spread comes from σ2 = Σ[x2P(x)] − μ2, with σ = √(σ2) restoring the original units; values beyond μ ± 2σ are conventionally called unusual.
  • A binomial needs fixed n, two outcomes, independence, and constant p; then P(x) = nCxpxqn−x, μ = np, σ = √(npq).
  • The Poisson counts events in a fixed interval with μ = σ2 = λ; the geometric counts trials until the first success with μ = 1/p.
  • Choose a model by asking what the problem holds fixed — the trials, the interval, or the single success you are waiting for.

ReferenceWords worth knowing

A quick reference for the terms in this chapter — skim it now, or circle back whenever a word trips you up.

Bernoulli trial
A single trial with exactly two possible outcomes, labeled success and failure, with success probability p. A binomial experiment is n independent Bernoulli trials.
Binomial coefficient
The count nCx = n!/[x!(n−x)!] of the different arrangements that give x successes in n trials.
Binomial distribution
The distribution of the number of successes in a fixed number of independent trials with constant success probability; P(x) = nCxpxqn−x.
Continuous random variable
A random variable that can take any value in an interval; probabilities are assigned to intervals, and the probability of any single exact value is 0.
Cumulative probability
The probability that a random variable is at most a given value, P(X ≤ x), found by adding the probabilities of all values up to and including x.
Discrete random variable
A random variable whose possible values can be listed, such as 0, 1, 2, 3; usually the result of counting. The list may be infinite.
Expected value
Another name for the mean of a random variable, E(X) = μ = Σ[xP(x)]; the long-run average over many repetitions.
Factorial
The product n! = n(n−1)(n−2)…(1), with 0! defined as 1.
Geometric distribution
The distribution of the number of independent trials needed to obtain the first success; P(x) = qx−1p, with mean 1/p.
Independent trials
Trials in which the outcome of one has no effect on the probabilities of any other.
Law of large numbers
The principle that as a chance process is repeated more and more times, the average of the results converges to the expected value.
Mean of a random variable (μ)
The probability-weighted average of the values, Σ[xP(x)]; the balance point of the distribution.
Memoryless property
The feature of the geometric distribution that past failures do not change the probability of success on the next trial.
Parameter
A number that describes a population or a probability model, such as n and p for a binomial or λ for a Poisson.
Poisson distribution
The distribution of the number of events occurring in a fixed interval at a constant average rate λ; P(x) = λxe−λ/x!, with mean and variance both equal to λ.
Probability distribution
A listing, formula, or graph giving every possible value of a discrete random variable together with its probability.
Probability histogram
A bar graph of a discrete distribution in which the values sit on the horizontal axis and the bar heights are the probabilities.
Random variable
A variable whose numeric value is determined by the outcome of a chance process; written X, with x denoting a particular value.
Range rule of thumb
A rough convention that values farther than 2 standard deviations from the mean — outside μ ± 2σ — may be considered unusual.
Standard deviation of a random variable (σ)
The square root of the variance; a typical distance of the values from the mean, expressed in the original units.
Success probability (p)
The probability of the outcome being counted on a single trial; its complement q = 1 − p is the failure probability.
Variance of a random variable (σ2)
The probability-weighted average squared distance from the mean, Σ[(x−μ)2P(x)], equal to Σ[x2P(x)] − μ2.

Check yourself

1. Classify each as discrete or continuous, and justify: (a) the number of text messages you send tomorrow, (b) the exact time you fall asleep tonight, (c) the number of eggs cracked before one is broken.
(a) discrete, (b) continuous, (c) discrete. (a) and (c) are counts, so their values can be listed — note (c) has no upper bound, which is fine because the list 1, 2, 3, … is still countable. (b) is a measurement filling an interval of time, so between any two values there is always another.
2. A table gives P(0) = 0.25, P(1) = 0.40, P(2) = 0.20, P(3) = 0.10. Is it a valid probability distribution? Show your check.
No — it fails the first requirement. Every entry lies between 0 and 1, so Rule 2 is satisfied, but 0.25 + 0.40 + 0.20 + 0.10 = 0.95, not 1. Roughly 5% of the probability is unaccounted for, meaning at least one possible value was left off the table.
3. For the distribution P(0) = 0.20, P(1) = 0.35, P(2) = 0.30, P(3) = 0.15, find P(X ≥ 1) two different ways.
P(X ≥ 1) = 0.80 either way. Direct addition: 0.35 + 0.30 + 0.15 = 0.80. Complement: 1 − P(0) = 1 − 0.20 = 0.80. The complement route is the one to reach for whenever a problem says "at least one."
4. A distribution has P(1) = 0.22, P(2) = k, P(3) = 0.31, P(4) = 0.12, with no other values. Find k and confirm it is legitimate.
k = 0.35. The known entries total 0.22 + 0.31 + 0.12 = 0.65, so k = 1 − 0.65 = 0.35. It lies between 0 and 1, so both requirements now hold and the table is valid.
5. Compute the mean and standard deviation of X with P(0) = 0.5, P(1) = 0.3, P(2) = 0.2. Show the steps.
μ = 0.7 and σ ≈ 0.78. μ = 0(0.5) + 1(0.3) + 2(0.2) = 0 + 0.3 + 0.4 = 0.7. Then Σ[x2P(x)] = 0(0.5) + 1(0.3) + 4(0.2) = 1.1, so σ2 = 1.1 − (0.7)2 = 1.1 − 0.49 = 0.61 and σ = √0.61 ≈ 0.781.
6. A friend computes an expected value of 2.4 children per family and says "so most families have between 2 and 3 children, and the next family I meet will probably have 2." What is wrong?
Expected value describes a long-run average, not any individual outcome, and 2.4 is not even a possible value. It means that averaging the number of children across very many families approaches 2.4. It says nothing about which value is most likely, nothing about the spread, and nothing reliable about the next family.
7. A charity sells 200 tickets at $5 each for a single $600 prize. Find the expected net gain of buying one ticket and interpret it.
E(X) = −$2.00 per ticket. Net gain is 600 − 5 = $595 with probability 1/200, and −$5 with probability 199/200. E(X) = 595(1/200) + (−5)(199/200) = (595 − 995)/200 = −400/200 = −$2.00. You will actually either gain $595 or lose $5; the −$2.00 is the average per ticket over many repetitions.
8. State the four requirements of a binomial experiment, then explain which one is violated by "deal 5 cards from a deck and count the hearts."
Fixed n; two outcomes per trial; independent trials; constant p. Dealing without replacement violates independence (and therefore constant p). After a heart is dealt, only 12 hearts remain in 51 cards, so p drops from 13/52 to 12/51. Five cards is nearly 10% of the deck, well past the 5% guideline, so the binomial should not be used here.
9. About 30% of a company's shipments arrive late. For 8 randomly chosen shipments, find the probability that exactly 3 arrive late.
P(X = 3) ≈ 0.2541, about 25.4%. Here n = 8, p = 0.30, q = 0.70, x = 3. The coefficient is 8C3 = 8!/(3!5!) = (8×7×6)/(3×2×1) = 336/6 = 56. Then (0.30)3 = 0.027 and (0.70)5 = 0.16807, so P = 56 × 0.027 × 0.16807 = 1.512 × 0.16807 ≈ 0.2541.
10. For the same shipment setting (n = 8, p = 0.30), find μ and σ, and decide whether 7 late shipments would be unusual.
μ = 2.4, σ ≈ 1.30, and yes — 7 late shipments would be unusual. μ = np = 8(0.30) = 2.4; σ = √(npq) = √[8(0.30)(0.70)] = √1.68 ≈ 1.296. The range rule gives μ ± 2σ = 2.4 ± 2.59, an interval from about −0.19 to 4.99, and 7 falls far outside it.
11. A clinic averages 4 walk-in patients per hour. Find the probability of exactly 2 walk-ins in an hour, and the probability of none.
P(X = 2) ≈ 0.1465 and P(X = 0) ≈ 0.0183. This is Poisson with λ = 4 and e−4 ≈ 0.018316. P(2) = (42 × 0.018316)/2! = (16 × 0.018316)/2 = 0.293056/2 ≈ 0.1465. P(0) = (40 × 0.018316)/0! = 0.018316, since 40 = 1 and 0! = 1.
12. Each part off a line has a 10% chance of being defective, independently. Find the probability that the first defective part is the 5th one inspected, and the average number of inspections until a defect appears.
P(X = 5) = 0.06561 and μ = 10 inspections. This is geometric with p = 0.10 and q = 0.90: P(5) = (0.90)4(0.10) = 0.6561 × 0.10 = 0.06561. The mean is μ = 1/p = 1/0.10 = 10. Note that four straight good parts change nothing — the 5th still has a 10% chance of being defective, because the trials are independent.
Statistics · Chapter 7

The Normal Distribution

Measure enough of almost anything that varies for lots of small, independent reasons — heights, birth weights, test scores, the error in a laboratory instrument — and the histogram tends to pile up in the middle and thin out symmetrically on both sides. That shape has a name: the normal distribution, or the bell curve. It matters far beyond its own good looks, because once you know a variable is roughly normal, two numbers — the mean and the standard deviation — let you answer questions about any value in the distribution. This chapter teaches you to read the curve, to reason with the 68–95–99.7 rule, to convert any score into a z-score, to turn that z-score into a probability, and to run the whole machine backward when you are handed a percentile instead of a score. There is arithmetic here, but it is all addition, subtraction, multiplication, and division — nothing harder.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • List the defining properties of a normal curve and explain what μ and σ each control.
  • Explain why probability for a continuous variable is an area, not a height, and why total area equals 1.
  • Apply the empirical rule (68–95–99.7) to estimate proportions above, below, and between values.
  • Compute a z-score with z = (x − μ) ÷ σ and interpret its sign and size correctly.
  • Use z-scores to compare values measured on different scales.
  • Read a standard normal table to find left-tail, right-tail, and between-two-values probabilities.
  • Work backward from a percentile to a raw score using x = μ + zσ.
  • Recognize when the normal model does not apply, and avoid the classic misreadings.

Section 1The normal curve

What the shape is telling you

A normal distribution is a smooth, symmetric, single-peaked curve — the bell curve. It is not a picture of data you collected; it is a model, an idealized mathematical shape that real data can resemble more or less closely. When we say "adult female heights are approximately normal," we mean the histogram of real heights looks a lot like that curve, close enough that we can use the curve's mathematics to make good predictions.

Every normal curve is completely pinned down by exactly two numbers, called parameters. The mean μ (mu) sits at the center and locates the peak. The standard deviation σ (sigma) controls the spread: a small σ gives a tall, narrow curve where values huddle near the middle; a large σ gives a short, wide curve where values sprawl. Change μ and the whole curve slides sideways without changing shape. Change σ and the curve stretches or squeezes without moving its center. Statisticians write this compactly as X ~ N(μ, σ2), read "X follows a normal distribution with mean μ and variance σ2."

The equation behind the picture is f(x) = [ 1 ÷ (σ √(2π) ) ] · e−(x − μ)² / (2σ²). You will never have to evaluate it by hand, and nothing in this chapter depends on it. It is worth seeing once only so you can confirm the claim above: the only symbols in it besides x are μ and σ. Two numbers, one curve.

The properties, one at a time

Six features define the shape, and every technique in the rest of this chapter leans on one of them.

PropertyWhat it meansWhy it matters
SymmetricThe left half is a mirror image of the right half about μLets you convert a right-tail question into a left-tail one, and vice versa
UnimodalExactly one peak, located at μA two-humped histogram is not normal — do not use these tools on it
Mean = median = modeAll three centers land on the same pointThe center is unambiguous; 50% of the area lies on each side
Total area = 1The area under the whole curve is exactly 1 (100%)Area is probability — this is what makes the curve usable
Asymptotic tailsThe tails approach the axis forever but never touch itNo value is strictly impossible, but extremes are vanishingly rare
Inflection at μ ± σThe curve switches from bending down to bending out one σ from centerYou can literally see σ on the picture — it is where the curve changes its bend

The fourth property deserves a slow read, because it is where most students first get uneasy. For a continuous variable — one that can take any value in a range, like height in inches carried out to as many decimals as you like — the probability of landing on exactly one specific value is zero. Nobody is exactly 64.000000… inches tall. So we never ask "what is the probability height equals 64?" We ask "what is the probability height falls between 63 and 65?", and the answer is the area under the curve over that stretch of the axis. Height of the curve is density, not probability. Area is probability.

A practical consequence: for continuous distributions, P(X < 64) and P(X ≤ 64) are the same number, because the single point 64 contributes zero area. That is not true for the discrete distributions of Chapter 6, and it trips people up on exams.

How close to normal does real data have to be?

Close enough, and no closer. Real heights are bounded below by zero and above by about nine feet; the normal curve technically extends to infinity in both directions. Real test scores are integers; the curve is smooth. None of that matters in practice, because the curve assigns so little area to the impossible regions that the error is negligible. What does matter is gross violations: strong skew, two peaks, or a hard boundary sitting close to the mean. Income is the classic bad fit — it is right-skewed, with a long tail of high earners, so its mean sits well above its median and the empirical rule you are about to learn will give you badly wrong answers.

Two numbers, and you know everything. That is the whole payoff of the normal model. For most distributions, knowing the mean and standard deviation tells you the center and the spread and little else. For a normal distribution, μ and σ determine the entire curve — so they let you compute the proportion of the population above any value, below any value, or between any two values, without ever looking at the original data again.
Interactive Drag the mean and the standard deviation — with sliders or by grabbing the peak and the inflection point directly — and watch the bell slide and stretch while the area inside ±1, ±2, and ±3 SDs is recomputed live and a real sample piles up underneath it. Open full ↑
Key points
  • A normal distribution is a symmetric, unimodal, bell-shaped model defined completely by μ (center) and σ (spread).
  • Mean, median, and mode coincide at μ; changing μ slides the curve, changing σ widens or narrows it.
  • Area under the curve is probability, and the total area is 1; the height of the curve is density, not probability.
  • The model is an approximation — do not use it on strongly skewed or two-peaked data.

Section 2The empirical rule in action

The rule, and the finer breakdown

Because every normal curve has the same shape, the proportion of area inside a given number of standard deviations is always the same, no matter what the units are. That constancy is the empirical rule, also called the 68–95–99.7 rule:

  • About 68% of values fall within μ ± 1σ (exactly 68.27%).
  • About 95% of values fall within μ ± 2σ (exactly 95.45%).
  • About 99.7% of values fall within μ ± 3σ (exactly 99.73%).

Split those bands in half using symmetry and you get a map of the curve precise enough for most quick reasoning. Each half-band below is one side of the center.

RegionShare of the areaRunning total from the left
Below μ − 3σ0.15%0.15%
μ − 3σ to μ − 2σ2.35%2.5%
μ − 2σ to μ − 1σ13.5%16%
μ − 1σ to μ34%50%
μ to μ + 1σ34%84%
μ + 1σ to μ + 2σ13.5%97.5%
μ + 2σ to μ + 3σ2.35%99.85%
Above μ + 3σ0.15%100%

Memorize the sequence 0.15, 2.35, 13.5, 34 — then 34, 13.5, 2.35, 0.15 going back out. Those eight numbers sum to 100% and answer a surprising share of exam questions with no table at all.

A worked example

Suppose adult female heights in a large population are approximately normal with μ = 64.0 inches and σ = 2.5 inches. First, build the ruler by marking off standard deviations from the center:

  • μ ± 1σ: 64.0 − 2.5 = 61.5 and 64.0 + 2.5 = 66.5 → about 68% of women are between 61.5 and 66.5 inches.
  • μ ± 2σ: 64.0 − 5.0 = 59.0 and 64.0 + 5.0 = 69.0 → about 95% are between 59.0 and 69.0 inches.
  • μ ± 3σ: 64.0 − 7.5 = 56.5 and 64.0 + 7.5 = 71.5 → about 99.7% are between 56.5 and 71.5 inches.
