  • Every hypothesis test is the same five steps: hypotheses, level and conditions, test statistic, p-value, conclusion in context.
  • A test statistic always answers one question: how many standard errors is the estimate from the claim?
  • Use the z-test for a mean, z = (x̄ − μ0)∕(σ∕√n), only when σ is genuinely known.
  • Use the t-test, t = (x̄ − μ0)∕(s∕√n) with df = n − 1, when σ is estimated — heavier tails, larger critical values.
  • Use the one-proportion z-test, z = (p̂ − p0)∕√(p0(1 − p0)∕n); the test uses p0 in the SE, the interval uses p̂.
  • A p-value is the probability of data this extreme given the null is true — never the probability the null is true.
  • We fail to reject rather than accept; “not significant” may only mean the sample was too small.
  • Every test assumes randomness, independence, and adequate normality — and randomness is the one nothing can repair.

ReferenceWords worth knowing

A quick reference for the vocabulary of this chapter — skim it now, and return to it whenever a term in a results section stops making sense.

Alpha (α), significance level
The threshold chosen in advance for how much evidence is required to reject H0; also the long-run rate of Type I errors. Commonly 0.05, but a convention rather than a law.
Alternative hypothesis (Ha)
The statement the researcher is looking for evidence of, expressed with ≠, >, or <. Chosen from the research question, never from the observed data.
Central limit theorem
The result that the sampling distribution of x̄ approaches a normal distribution as n grows, whatever the shape of the population.
Critical value
The cutoff (z* or t*) that marks the boundary of the rejection region for a chosen α and alternative.
Degrees of freedom (df)
The number of independent pieces of information available for estimating variability; n − 1 for a one-sample t-test, because x̄ consumed one.
Effect size
A measure of how big a difference is, on the scale of the data or standardized — the question a p-value does not answer.
Null hypothesis (H0)
The “no effect, no difference” statement containing the equality; the assumption under which the p-value is computed.
One-proportion z-test
The test of H0: p = p0 using z = (p̂ − p0) ∕ √(p0(1 − p0)∕n).
One-tailed (one-sided) test
A test whose alternative points in a single direction, placing the entire rejection region in one tail. Must be specified before data collection.
p-value
The probability, assuming H0 is true, of obtaining a test statistic at least as extreme as the one observed.
Power
1 − β: the probability of correctly rejecting a false null. Increases with larger n, larger true effects, less variability, and larger α.
Rejection region
The set of test-statistic values, in the tail or tails, that lead to rejecting H0 at the chosen α.
Robustness
A procedure's ability to perform acceptably when its assumptions are mildly violated. The t-test is robust to modest non-normality but not to outliers in small samples.
Sample proportion (p̂)
The count of successes divided by the sample size, x∕n; the point estimate of the population proportion p.
Standard error
The standard deviation of a sampling distribution: σ∕√n for a mean with σ known, s∕√n when estimated.
Statistically significant
Describes a result whose p-value falls below α — meaning “unlikely to be noise alone,” not “large” or “important.”
Student's t distribution
A symmetric, bell-shaped family of distributions indexed by degrees of freedom, with heavier tails than the normal; it converges to the standard normal as df grows.
Test statistic
A standardized measure of how far the sample estimate lies from the null value, in standard errors; here z or t.
Two-tailed (two-sided) test
A test whose alternative is ≠, splitting the rejection region between both tails and doubling the one-tail probability to get the p-value.
Type I error
Rejecting a null hypothesis that is actually true — a false alarm. Its long-run rate is α.
Type II error
Failing to reject a null hypothesis that is actually false — a miss. Its rate is β.
z-test
A test whose statistic is compared to the standard normal distribution; for a mean it requires that σ be known.

Check yourself

1. List the five steps of a hypothesis test in order, and state what changes from one test to another.
(1) State H0 and Ha; (2) choose α and check conditions; (3) compute the test statistic; (4) find the p-value or compare to the critical value; (5) decide and interpret in context. Only step 3's formula changes between tests — and even that is always (estimate − claim) ÷ standard error.
2. A study reports p = 0.03. A classmate says “so there's only a 3% chance the null hypothesis is true.” Correct them.
Wrong — the p-value is computed assuming the null is true, so it cannot be the probability that the null is true. The correct reading: if H0 were true, results at least this extreme would occur about 3% of the time. It is the probability of the data given the hypothesis, not of the hypothesis given the data.
3. Why do we say “fail to reject H0” instead of “accept H0”?
Because a non-significant result is consistent with the null being true or with a real effect that the study was too small to detect. Absence of evidence is not evidence of absence; the test never confirms the null, it only fails to overturn it.
4. A calibrated instrument has known σ = 4.0. In a random sample of n = 25, x̄ = 498.2, and H0: μ = 500 against a two-sided alternative. Compute the standard error, z, and the p-value, and state the conclusion at α = 0.05.
SE = 4.0∕√25 = 0.80; z = (498.2 − 500)∕0.80 = −2.25; p = 2(0.0122) = 0.0244; reject H0. At the 5% level there is evidence the mean differs from 500. Equivalently, |−2.25| exceeds the critical value 1.96.
5. Why does the t distribution have heavier tails than the standard normal?
Because s is estimated from the same sample and varies from sample to sample, adding a second source of randomness on top of the variation in x̄. That extra noise makes extreme standardized values more common, so the tails are thicker and the critical values larger — the honest price of not knowing σ.
6. Explain why the degrees of freedom for a one-sample t-test are n − 1.
Computing s requires x̄, which is calculated from the same data, using up one independent piece of information. Once you know n − 1 of the deviations from the mean, the last one is forced, because all n deviations must sum to zero.
7. A random sample of n = 16 gives x̄ = 33.4 and s = 6.4, testing H0: μ = 30 two-sided at α = 0.05 (t* = 2.131 at df = 15). Compute t and decide.
SE = 6.4∕4 = 1.60; t = 3.4∕1.60 = 2.125 with df = 15; since 2.125 < 2.131 (p ≈ 0.051), fail to reject H0. The 95% interval, 33.4 ± 2.131(1.60) = (29.99, 36.81), contains 30 — barely — which is the same conclusion in interval form.
8. Using the same x̄ = 33.4 and s = 6.4 but n = 25 (t* = 2.064 at df = 24), redo the test. What does the change teach you?
SE = 6.4∕5 = 1.28; t = 3.4∕1.28 = 2.656 > 2.064, so reject H0 (p ≈ 0.014). The estimated effect is identical (3.4 minutes); only precision changed. “Not significant” can simply mean the sample was too small, so sample size must be reported with every conclusion.
9. In a random sample of n = 400, 216 people support a measure. Test H0: p = 0.60 against a two-sided alternative at α = 0.05, showing the conditions and the arithmetic.
Conditions: np0 = 240 ≥ 10, n(1 − p0) = 160 ≥ 10. Then p̂ = 216∕400 = 0.54; SE0 = √(0.6 × 0.4∕400) = √0.0006 = 0.02449; z = −0.06∕0.02449 = −2.45; p = 2(0.00714) = 0.0143; reject H0. There is evidence the true support rate is not 60%.
10. Why does the one-proportion z-test use p0 in the standard error while the confidence interval uses p̂?
A test computes everything in the null's world, so it uses the null's variability, p0(1 − p0). A confidence interval assumes no particular value of p, so it must estimate variability from the data using p̂(1 − p̂). Because the two standard errors differ slightly, a borderline test and interval can occasionally disagree.
11. A researcher surveys 5,000 visitors to their own website and finds a highly significant result. Why does the enormous sample size not rescue the study?
The sample is not random — it is a self-selected, convenience sample — so the estimate is centered on the wrong value. The Central Limit Theorem fixes the shape of the sampling distribution, not bias. A larger biased sample just gives a narrower interval around the wrong number.
12. State the three conditions every one-sample test requires, rank them by how forgiving they are, and give one remedy for the least serious failure.
Random sampling (least forgiving — nothing repairs it), independence (sample randomly and keep n under 10% of the population), and approximate normality of the sampling distribution of x̄ (most forgiving). For a normality failure: increase n so the CLT takes over, or switch to a nonparametric test such as the sign or signed-rank test. For proportions, use the exact binomial test when np0 or n(1 − p0) falls below 10.
Statistics · Chapter 12

Two-Sample Tests

Almost every interesting question in statistics is a comparison. Does this treatment beat that one? Do these two groups of customers behave differently? Did the change we made actually change anything? A one-sample test asks whether a single group differs from a fixed number; a two-sample test asks whether two groups differ from each other — and that is the question the world usually cares about. This chapter builds the four workhorse procedures (two independent means, paired means, two proportions, and the confidence intervals that go with them), shows you exactly how to decide which one a given study calls for, and works the arithmetic all the way through so you can see there is no magic in it.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • Recognize when two samples are independent and when they are paired, and explain why the distinction changes the analysis.
  • State the hypotheses, check the conditions, and compute the test statistic for a two-sample t-test of independent means.
  • Reduce a matched-pairs study to a single sample of differences and run a paired t-test.
  • Carry out a two-proportion z-test, including the pooled proportion and the success–failure check.
  • Use a decision guide to choose the correct test from the study design, and name a sensible alternative when conditions fail.
  • Build and correctly interpret confidence intervals for a difference of two means or two proportions.
  • Explain the duality between a two-sided test and a confidence interval — and the one place it breaks down.
  • State precisely what a p-value and a 95% confidence interval do and do not mean.

Section 1Two independent means

Two groups, one question

You have measured a numeric outcome — a test score, a reaction time, a blood pressure, a delivery time — on two separate groups of subjects, and the group averages came out different. They always do. Two samples drawn from the very same population will differ by chance alone. The entire job of a two-sample test is to answer one question: is the gap we observed bigger than chance would comfortably produce?

The samples here are independent, which has a precise meaning: knowing any value in group 1 tells you nothing about any value in group 2, and no subject appears in both groups. Two randomly assigned treatment arms are independent. Men and women in one survey are independent. The same forty people measured in January and again in June are not — that is Section 2's problem.

Write the population means as μ1 and μ2. The hypotheses are about the difference:

H0: μ1 − μ2 = 0     Ha: μ1 − μ2 ≠ 0

Notice what the null says: the two populations have the same mean. It does not say the two samples had the same mean — they plainly did not, or you would not be testing anything.

The test statistic, and where it comes from

Every test statistic in this book has the same shape: (what we saw) minus (what the null predicted), divided by how much that quantity naturally wobbles. Our estimate of μ1 − μ2 is x̄1 − x̄2. Its wobble is measured by the standard error of the difference. Because the two samples are independent, their variances add (never their standard deviations):

SE = √( s12/n1 + s22/n2 )

and the test statistic is

t = ( x̄1 − x̄2 − 0 ) / SE

Since we estimated both standard deviations from data, the reference curve is a t-distribution rather than the normal. The degrees of freedom are the awkward part. Welch's approximation — what statistical software gives you by default and what you should use by default — is

df = ( s12/n1 + s22/n2 )2 ÷ [ (s12/n1)2/(n1−1) + (s22/n2)2/(n2−1) ]

This is rarely a whole number, and that is fine. Working by hand without software, you may round the df down to the nearest integer; that is deliberately conservative, giving a slightly wider interval and a slightly larger p-value, so you never overstate your evidence.

ConditionWhat it meansHow to check it
Independence withinObservations inside each group do not influence one anotherRandom sample, or random assignment; sample under 10% of the population
Independence betweenNo subject appears in both groups; no natural pairingRead the study design — this is a design fact, not a data fact
Normality (or size)Each population is roughly normal, or each sample is largeDot plot / box plot per group; n ≥ 30 per group makes mild skew harmless
OutliersNo single point is driving the differenceLook at the box plots; investigate, do not silently delete

A worked example, all the way through

A trainer compares two coaching formats. Format A: n1 = 12, x̄1 = 78.4, s1 = 8.2. Format B: n2 = 15, x̄2 = 71.9, s2 = 9.6. Participants were randomly assigned, so the groups are independent. Test at α = 0.05, two-sided.

Step 1 — the observed difference. 78.4 − 71.9 = 6.5 points.

Step 2 — the standard error. First the two pieces:
s12/n1 = 8.22/12 = 67.24/12 = 5.6033
s22/n2 = 9.62/15 = 92.16/15 = 6.1440
Sum = 11.7473, so SE = √11.7473 = 3.4274.

Step 3 — the test statistic. t = 6.5 / 3.4274 = 1.896. The observed gap is about 1.9 standard errors from zero.

Step 4 — degrees of freedom.
Numerator: 11.74732 = 138.00
Denominator: 5.60332/11 + 6.14402/14 = 31.397/11 + 37.749/14 = 2.8543 + 2.6963 = 5.5506
df = 138.00 / 5.5506 = 24.9.

Step 5 — the p-value and the decision. With about 25 degrees of freedom, the two-sided cutoff at α = 0.05 is t* ≈ 2.06. Our 1.896 does not reach it; the two-sided p-value is roughly 0.07. Since 0.07 > 0.05, we fail to reject H0.

Step 6 — say it in English. "A 6.5-point advantage for Format A was observed, but with these sample sizes and this much spread a gap at least that large, in either direction, would arise about 7 times in 100 even if the two formats were truly identical (t = 1.90, df ≈ 24.9, p ≈ 0.07). The data do not provide convincing evidence of a difference." Note what we did not say: we did not say the formats are equal. Failing to reject is a failure to convict, not a verdict of innocence — the study may simply be too small.

Use Welch's test by default. The older "pooled" two-sample t-test assumes the two populations have equal variances and combines them into one estimate, buying slightly more degrees of freedom. That assumption is rarely knowable and, when it is wrong with unequal sample sizes, the pooled test's error rate is wrong too. Welch's version costs almost nothing when variances really are equal and protects you when they are not. If your software says "Welch two-sample t-test," you are in good hands.
Interactive Draw real samples from two populations you control, watch the difference between their means bounce around, and see exactly where its standard error comes from — variances add, standard errors do not. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Independent samples means no subject is in both groups and no natural pairing exists.
  • Variances add: SE = √(s12/n1 + s22/n2). Standard deviations never add.
  • t = (x̄1 − x̄2) / SE, referred to a t-curve with Welch degrees of freedom.
  • "Fail to reject" means insufficient evidence — never proof that the means are equal.

Section 2Paired samples

What makes a pair a pair

Sometimes the two columns of numbers are not two separate crowds of people but the same people twice, or two people deliberately matched to each other. Each observation in column 1 has a natural, non-arbitrary partner in column 2. That is a matched-pairs (or paired) design, and it shows up constantly:

DesignWhat is pairedTypical example
Before / afterThe same subject at two timesReaction time before and after a training module
Two treatments, one subjectThe same subject under both conditionsLeft eye vs. right eye; two tires on the same car
Matched subjectsTwo different subjects paired on key traitsTwins; customers matched on age, region, and spend
Two raters, one itemThe same item scored twiceTwo graders scoring the same 30 essays
Not pairedNothing links row i of one group to row i of the other40 people in treatment, 45 different people in control

Here is the beautiful part: a paired analysis is not a new theory. You compute each pair's difference, d = (value 1) − (value 2), throw away the original columns, and run an ordinary one-sample t-test on the single column of differences against a null value of 0.

H0: μd = 0     Ha: μd ≠ 0     t = d̄ / ( sd / √n )     df = n − 1

where n is the number of pairs, not the number of measurements. Eight people measured twice give you n = 8 and df = 7, not n = 16.

Because the procedure is a one-sample t-test, its conditions are the one-sample conditions — but applied to the column of differences, not to the original two columns. This is the point students most often skip.

ConditionWhat it meansHow to check it
Independence of pairsOne pair's difference tells you nothing about another pair'sRandom sample of pairs, or random assignment of order within pairs; sample under 10% of the population
Normality of the differencesThe population of differences is roughly normal, or there are enough pairsDot plot / histogram of the d column; n ≥ 30 pairs makes mild skew harmless. The two original columns may be skewed — only d matters
Outliers among differencesNo single pair is driving d̄Scan the d column; one huge difference in a small study can create or erase significance

In the worked example below there are only eight pairs, so normality of the differences carries real weight. The eight differences run from −3 to 14 with no gaps or outliers — consistent with a roughly symmetric population — so the t-procedure is reasonable. With a clearly skewed or outlier-ridden difference column and this few pairs, the Wilcoxon signed-rank test of Section 4 would be the safer choice.

Why pairing is worth doing

People differ enormously from one another, and that person-to-person variation is pure noise when you are trying to detect a treatment effect. Pairing subtracts that noise away: a naturally slow responder is slow in both columns, so the difference is unaffected by how slow they are. What is left in the difference column is the effect plus measurement noise — a much quieter signal to listen to. That is why a paired design can detect an effect with eight subjects that an independent design would miss with forty. Section 4 shows this on real numbers.

A worked example, all the way through

Eight participants completed a task; their reaction time in milliseconds was recorded before and after a short training module. Same eight people both times, so the data are paired. Test at α = 0.05, two-sided, with d = before − after (so a positive d̄ means they got faster).

ParticipantBefore (ms)After (ms)d = before − after
141240012
23853805
3401404−3
44304219
539638214
64214192
74084017
844543510
Mean412.25405.257.00

Step 1 — mean difference. The differences sum to 12 + 5 − 3 + 9 + 14 + 2 + 7 + 10 = 56, so d̄ = 56/8 = 7.00 ms. (Reassuringly, this equals 412.25 − 405.25 — the mean of the differences is always the difference of the means.)

Step 2 — standard deviation of the differences. Deviations from 7: 5, −2, −10, 2, 7, −5, 0, 3. Squares: 25, 4, 100, 4, 49, 25, 0, 9, summing to 216.
sd2 = 216 / (8 − 1) = 30.857, so sd = 5.555 ms.

Step 3 — standard error. SE = 5.555 / √8 = 5.555 / 2.8284 = 1.964 ms.

Step 4 — test statistic and df. t = 7.00 / 1.964 = 3.564, with df = 8 − 1 = 7.

Step 5 — p-value and decision. On 7 degrees of freedom the two-sided cutoff at α = 0.05 is 2.365, and the cutoff at α = 0.01 is 3.499. Our 3.564 clears both, so the two-sided p-value is a little under 0.01 — about 0.009. We reject H0.

Step 6 — say it in English. "Reaction times improved by an average of 7.0 ms after training. A mean change at least this large, in either direction, would occur in fewer than 1% of studies if training truly had no effect (paired t = 3.56, df = 7, p ≈ 0.009), so there is convincing evidence that reaction time changed." Because this was a before/after study with no control group, we should stop short of crediting the training itself — practice effects and simple familiarity with the task are unaddressed rival explanations. The test is valid; the causal claim is limited by the design.

Pairing is decided by the design, not by the data. You cannot look at two columns of equal length, notice they line up nicely, and declare them paired — nor can you unpair genuinely linked data because the independent test looks tidier. Ask one question of the study: does row 3 of column A have a specific, pre-existing reason to be matched with row 3 of column B? If yes, pair. If the rows could be shuffled without losing information, they are independent.
Interactive Sort ten real study designs into paired and independent, then watch one crossover study's identical numbers analysed both ways — live sliders for the effect, the spread, the pairing correlation ρ and the number of pairs drive six computed SVG charts that show exactly what the pairing is worth. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Paired data means every observation has one specific partner — same subject twice, or deliberately matched subjects.
  • Collapse the pairs to differences and run a one-sample t-test on them: t = d̄ / (sd/√n), df = n − 1.
  • n is the number of pairs. Eight people measured twice give df = 7.
  • Check the conditions on the difference column: independent pairs, roughly normal differences (or enough pairs), no dominating outlier.
  • Pairing removes subject-to-subject variability, which is why it is far more powerful for the same sample size.

Section 3Two proportions

When the outcome is yes-or-no

Now the outcome is not a number but a category with two levels: clicked or did not click, recovered or did not recover, passed or failed. Each group is summarized by a sample proportion, p̂ = (number of successes) / (group size). "Success" simply labels the outcome you are counting; it carries no cheerful connotation.

H0: p1 − p2 = 0     Ha: p1 − p2 ≠ 0

Here the reference curve is the standard normal, so the statistic is a z, not a t. The reason is that a proportion's standard deviation is determined entirely by the proportion itself — √(p(1−p)) — so once you assume a value for p there is no separate variance to estimate from data, and no degrees of freedom to lose.

The pooled proportion — and why it appears only in the test

The null hypothesis says the two population proportions are equal to some common value. If that is true, the best estimate of that common value uses every observation you have, ignoring group labels:

p̂pool = (x1 + x2) / (n1 + n2)

and the standard error used for the test is built from that single pooled value:

SEpool = √( p̂pool(1 − p̂pool) × (1/n1 + 1/n2) )     z = (p̂1 − p̂2) / SEpool

A confidence interval, by contrast, is not built under the null — it is an estimate of whatever the difference actually is — so it uses the unpooled standard error with each group's own proportion. That distinction is the single most common slip in this section, and Section 5 returns to it.

The conditions are independence (as before) plus the success–failure condition: the normal approximation only works if you have enough of both outcomes. For the test, check it with the pooled proportion; all four of n1p̂pool, n1(1−p̂pool), n2p̂pool, n2(1−p̂pool) should be at least 10.

A worked example, all the way through

An email is sent in two versions. Version 1: 45 of 150 recipients responded. Version 2: 30 of 160 responded. Recipients were randomly assigned to a version. Test at α = 0.05, two-sided.

Step 1 — the sample proportions.
p̂1 = 45/150 = 0.300   p̂2 = 30/160 = 0.1875
Observed difference = 0.300 − 0.1875 = 0.1125, that is, 11.25 percentage points.

Step 2 — the pooled proportion. p̂pool = (45 + 30)/(150 + 160) = 75/310 = 0.24194.

Step 3 — check success–failure. 150(0.24194) = 36.3 and 150(0.75806) = 113.7; 160(0.24194) = 38.7 and 160(0.75806) = 121.3. All four exceed 10. ✓

Step 4 — pooled standard error.
p̂pool(1 − p̂pool) = 0.24194 × 0.75806 = 0.18340
1/150 + 1/160 = 0.006667 + 0.006250 = 0.012917
Product = 0.18340 × 0.012917 = 0.0023688, so SEpool = √0.0023688 = 0.04867.

Step 5 — the test statistic. z = 0.1125 / 0.04867 = 2.31.

Step 6 — p-value and decision. For the standard normal, the area beyond z = 2.31 in one tail is 0.0104. Doubling it for a two-sided test gives p = 0.021. Since 0.021 < 0.05, we reject H0.

Step 7 — say it in English. "Version 1 drew responses from 30.0% of recipients versus 18.8% for Version 2, an 11.3-percentage-point difference. If the two versions were equally effective, a gap this large or larger, in either direction, would appear in about 2% of experiments (z = 2.31, p = 0.021), so there is convincing evidence that response rates differ." Because assignment was random, a causal reading is defensible here in a way it was not in Section 2.

QuantityFormulaUsed forIn our example
Differencep̂1 − p̂2Both test and interval0.1125
Pooled p̂(x1+x2)/(n1+n2)Test only0.24194
SE, pooled√(p̂pool(1−p̂pool)(1/n1+1/n2))The z-statistic0.04867
SE, unpooled√(p̂1(1−p̂1)/n1 + p̂2(1−p̂2)/n2)Confidence interval only0.04850
Percentage points are not percent. Going from 18.8% to 30.0% is a rise of 11.3 percentage points, but a 60% relative increase (0.1125 / 0.1875 = 0.60). Both numbers are true and they describe the same data; headlines choose whichever sounds larger. State which one you mean, every time.
Interactive Run a two-arm trial patient by patient, watch the gap between two sample proportions bounce around, and see why the test and the confidence interval are built from two different standard errors. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Two proportions use a z-statistic — there are no degrees of freedom to estimate.
  • The pooled proportion is used for the test because the null asserts the proportions are equal.
  • The confidence interval uses the unpooled standard error, because it is not assuming the null.
  • Check success–failure: at least 10 successes and 10 failures expected in each group.

Section 4Choosing the right test

Two questions get you there

Students often try to memorize a dozen tests. You do not need to. For the two-sample world, two questions settle it almost every time.

Question 1: what kind of outcome did you measure? If each subject produced a number you could sensibly average — minutes, dollars, millimetres of mercury — you are comparing means. If each subject produced a yes/no label you are counting, you are comparing proportions. (A 1–5 satisfaction rating is a genuine grey area; treating it as numeric is common and usually defensible, but say what you did.)

Question 2: how are the two groups related? If every value in one group has a specific partner in the other, the data are paired. Otherwise they are independent. This is answered by reading the study design, never by staring at the numbers.

OutcomeGroup relationshipTestStatisticReference curve
NumericIndependentTwo-sample (Welch) t-test(x̄1−x̄2)/√(s12/n1+s22/n2)t, Welch df
NumericPairedPaired t-testd̄/(sd/√n)t, df = n−1
Yes/noIndependentTwo-proportion z-test(p̂1−p̂2)/SEpoolStandard normal
Yes/noPairedMcNemar's testBuilt only from the discordant pairsChi-square, df = 1
Numeric, badly skewed / tiny nIndependentWilcoxon rank-sum (Mann–Whitney)Rank-basedIts own distribution
Numeric, badly skewed / tiny nPairedWilcoxon signed-rankRank-basedIts own distribution
Yes/no, small countsIndependentFisher's exact testExact enumeration— (exact)

What the choice actually costs you

This is not bookkeeping; the wrong choice changes the answer. Take the exact eight participants from Section 2 and pretend, wrongly, that the "before" and "after" columns came from two unrelated groups of eight people. The two column means are unchanged: 412.25 and 405.25, a difference of 7.00 ms. But now the person-to-person spread is back in the denominator. Working from those columns, sbefore = 19.33 and safter = 19.08, so

SE = √(19.332/8 + 19.082/8) = √(46.71 + 45.49) = √92.20 = 9.602

t = 7.00 / 9.602 = 0.73, df ≈ 14, p ≈ 0.48

The same seven-millisecond effect goes from p ≈ 0.009 (paired, correct) to p ≈ 0.48 (independent, wrong) — from convincing evidence to nothing at all. The paired standard error was 1.96 ms; the independent one was 9.60 ms, nearly five times larger, because it is dominated by the fact that participant 8 is simply slower than participant 2. Pairing sweeps that irrelevance out of the calculation. Analyze paired data as independent and you will routinely fail to find effects that are really there.

The reverse mistake is worse. If the data are genuinely independent, there is no way to line them up into pairs, and forcing a pairing (by sort order, or by row number in a spreadsheet) manufactures a relationship that does not exist and produces a p-value with no meaning at all.

Three decisions to make before you look

Fix these in advance, because deciding them after seeing the data invalidates the p-value:

  • One-sided or two-sided? Two-sided is the honest default. A one-sided test is legitimate only when a difference in the opposite direction would be as uninteresting as no difference at all — and it must be chosen before the data are seen. Switching to one-sided because your two-sided p came out 0.08 is a well-known way of manufacturing a false positive.
  • What is α? Conventionally 0.05, but that is a convention, not a law of nature. It is your tolerance for a Type I error — rejecting a true null.
  • How many tests will you run? Every additional test is another chance at a false positive. Twenty independent tests at α = 0.05 on data with no real effects yield, on average, one "significant" finding. Decide your comparisons in advance, and correct for multiplicity if there are many.
The design chooses the test; the p-value never does. If two procedures give different answers on the same data, that is not a menu — one of them is wrong for that design. Write down which test the design calls for before you compute anything, and stick to it whichever way the number lands.
Interactive Walk a decision tree from a study's description to the right two-sample test, then watch the test at the end of your path run live on the real data beside the test you would have reached by answering differently — and simulate thousands of studies to see what the last question is actually worth. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Two questions choose the test: numeric or yes/no outcome, and independent or paired groups.
  • Paired numeric → paired t; independent numeric → Welch t; independent yes/no → two-proportion z; paired yes/no → McNemar.
  • Analyzing paired data as independent inflates the standard error and hides real effects.
  • Fix your direction, your α, and your number of comparisons before seeing results.

Section 5Two-sample confidence intervals

From a verdict to a measurement

A test returns a verdict: reject, or fail to reject. That is a thin answer. It does not say how big the difference is, and "significant" and "important" are entirely different words. A confidence interval for the difference answers the better question — how big, give or take how much? — and every two-sample interval in this chapter has one shape:

(point estimate) ± (critical value) × (standard error)

ComparisonPoint estimateStandard errorCritical value
Two independent meansx̄1 − x̄2√(s12/n1 + s22/n2)t*, Welch df
Paired meansd̄sd/√nt*, df = n − 1
Two proportionsp̂1 − p̂2√(p̂1(1−p̂1)/n1 + p̂2(1−p̂2)/n2) — unpooledz* (1.96 for 95%)

The product of the last two columns is the margin of error. Nothing else changes from procedure to procedure.

Worked: the interval for Section 1's data

Return to the coaching study: x̄1 − x̄2 = 6.5, SE = 3.4274, df ≈ 24.9.

Step 1 — critical value. For 95% confidence with about 25 degrees of freedom, t* ≈ 2.06. (Rounding df down to 24 gives 2.064 — the difference is invisible at two decimals.)

Step 2 — margin of error. 2.06 × 3.4274 = 7.06 points.

Step 3 — the interval. 6.5 − 7.06 = −0.56 and 6.5 + 7.06 = 13.56, giving (−0.56, 13.56) points.

Step 4 — read it. The interval contains 0, so "no difference" is among the plausible values — exactly matching the earlier decision to fail to reject at α = 0.05. But it says far more than the test did: it says Format A could plausibly be a half-point worse or as much as thirteen and a half points better. That is an enormous range. The honest conclusion is not "the formats are the same"; it is "this study was too small to tell us anything useful, and it should be repeated with more participants."

The other two examples work identically. For the paired reaction times: 7.00 ± 2.365 × 1.964 = 7.00 ± 4.65, giving (2.35, 11.65) ms — entirely above zero, matching p ≈ 0.009. For the two email versions, using the unpooled SE = √(0.300×0.700/150 + 0.1875×0.8125/160) = √(0.001400 + 0.000952) = 0.04850, the interval is 0.1125 ± 1.96 × 0.04850 = 0.1125 ± 0.0951, giving (0.017, 0.208) — between 1.7 and 20.8 percentage points, all positive.

Duality — and the one place it leaks

For the two t-procedures, the interval and the two-sided test are two views of one calculation. A 95% interval that excludes 0 corresponds exactly to a two-sided test rejecting at α = 0.05; an interval containing 0 corresponds exactly to failing to reject. Both use the same point estimate, the same standard error, and the same critical value.

For two proportions this correspondence is only approximate, because the test uses the pooled standard error and the interval uses the unpooled one. When a result sits very near the boundary the two can disagree — a p-value of 0.048 alongside an interval whose lower limit is a hair below zero. Neither is a mistake; they are answering slightly different questions. Report both and describe the finding as borderline, which is what it is.

What a confidence interval actually means

Here is the sentence to memorize, because the tempting version is wrong. The parameter μ1 − μ2 is a fixed, unknown number. Your interval is what randomly varied — a different sample would have produced a different one. So the 95% describes the procedure: if you repeated this entire study endlessly, about 95% of the intervals it produced would cover the true difference. It does not say there is a 95% chance the true difference lies in the one interval you happen to be holding. That interval either covers the parameter or it does not; the coin has already landed.

The p-value needs the same care. p = 0.021 means: if the null were true, data at least this extreme would occur about 2.1% of the time. It is not the probability that the null is true, not the probability the result was a fluke, and not a measure of effect size. A trivial difference becomes "significant" with a large enough sample; a large difference stays "non-significant" with a small one. This is precisely why you report the interval alongside the p-value — the p-value tells you whether you can see the effect, and the interval tells you whether it is worth caring about.

"Zero is in the interval" is not "the groups are the same." An interval of (−0.56, 13.56) and an interval of (−0.20, 0.25) both contain zero and both fail to reject — but the first is an admission of ignorance and the second is real evidence that any difference is tiny. Only the width of the interval tells you which one you have, and the test statistic alone never will.
Interactive Build a confidence interval for a difference between two groups from live samples, watch it widen with confidence and narrow with sample size, and work out what it really means when the interval covers zero. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Every two-sample interval is (estimate) ± (critical value)(SE); only the SE and the critical value change.
  • Use t* for means (Welch df, or n−1 when paired) and z* for proportions, with the unpooled SE.
  • An interval containing 0 matches a two-sided test that fails to reject — exactly for t-procedures, approximately for proportions.
  • The 95% belongs to the long-run procedure, not to the single interval in front of you.
  • A p-value is not the probability that the null hypothesis is true.

The chapter in one breath

  • A two-sample test asks whether an observed gap between two groups is larger than chance alone would comfortably produce.
  • For independent means, variances add: SE = √(s12/n1 + s22/n2), and t = (x̄1 − x̄2)/SE on Welch degrees of freedom.
  • For paired data, collapse each pair to a difference and run a one-sample t-test on the differences with df = n − 1, where n counts pairs.
  • Pairing removes subject-to-subject variability — the same 7 ms effect gave p ≈ 0.009 paired and p ≈ 0.48 analyzed (wrongly) as independent.
  • For two proportions, use a z-statistic with the pooled proportion in the test and the unpooled SE in the interval; check success–failure.
  • The design picks the test: numeric vs. yes/no outcome, independent vs. paired groups — decided before any computing.
  • Every two-sample confidence interval is (estimate) ± (critical value)(SE), and it reports the size of the effect the test only votes on.
  • The 95% describes the long-run procedure, and a p-value is the probability of the data given the null — never the probability the null is true.

ReferenceWords worth knowing

A quick reference for the terms in this chapter — skim it now, or circle back whenever a word trips you up.

Alternative hypothesis (Ha)
The claim that there is a difference; in this chapter usually μ1 − μ2 ≠ 0 or p1 − p2 ≠ 0.
Confidence interval
A range of plausible values for a parameter, built as (estimate) ± (critical value)(standard error). Its confidence level describes the long-run success rate of the procedure.
Degrees of freedom
The parameter that sets the shape of a t-curve; n − 1 for a paired test, Welch's approximation for two independent means.
Independent samples
Two groups in which no subject appears twice and no observation is naturally matched to another.
Margin of error
The half-width of a confidence interval: the critical value times the standard error.
Matched pairs
A design in which each observation in one condition has one specific partner in the other — the same subject twice, or deliberately matched subjects.
Nonparametric test
A procedure that does not assume a normal population, typically working from ranks; e.g., Wilcoxon rank-sum and Wilcoxon signed-rank.
Null hypothesis (H0)
The claim of no difference between the two populations, against which the data are weighed.
Paired t-test
A one-sample t-test applied to the within-pair differences, testing μd = 0.
Point estimate
The single best guess at a parameter from the sample: here x̄1 − x̄2, d̄, or p̂1 − p̂2.
Pooled proportion
(x1 + x2)/(n1 + n2), the common proportion estimated under the null; used in the two-proportion test only.
Pooled two-sample t-test
The older variant that assumes equal population variances and combines them into one estimate; Welch's test is the safer default.
p-value
The probability of observing data at least as extreme as yours if the null hypothesis were true. It is not the probability that the null is true.
Sampling distribution
The distribution of a statistic across all possible samples; for a difference of means it is approximately a t-curve centered on the true difference.
Significance level (α)
The threshold, fixed in advance, at which a p-value counts as convincing; equivalently, the tolerated rate of Type I error.
Standard error
The estimated standard deviation of a statistic — how much the difference between the two samples would wobble from study to study.
Success–failure condition
The requirement of at least 10 expected successes and 10 expected failures in each group before trusting the normal approximation for proportions.
t-distribution
A bell curve with heavier tails than the normal, used when the standard deviation is estimated from data; it approaches the normal as df grows.
Test statistic
(Observed − hypothesized) divided by the standard error — the observed difference expressed in standard errors.
Two-sample t-test
The test of μ1 − μ2 = 0 from two independent samples of a numeric outcome.
Type I error
Rejecting a null hypothesis that is actually true — a false positive; its long-run rate is α.
Welch's t-test
The two-sample t-test that does not assume equal variances, using an approximate degrees-of-freedom formula; the recommended default.

Check yourself

1. A study measures cholesterol in 40 people before a diet and in the same 40 people after it. A colleague runs a two-sample t-test with n1 = n2 = 40. What is wrong, and what should be done instead?
The samples are not independent — they are the same 40 people twice, so this is a matched-pairs design. Compute the 40 within-person differences and run a paired t-test on them with df = 39. Treating paired data as independent leaves all the person-to-person variability in the standard error, inflating it and making a real effect much harder to detect.
2. Two independent samples have s1 = 6 with n1 = 9, and s2 = 8 with n2 = 16. Compute the standard error of the difference in means.
SE = 2.828. s12/n1 = 36/9 = 4; s22/n2 = 64/16 = 4; sum = 8; √8 = 2.828. Note that the variances are added, never the standard deviations — adding 6 and 8 would be a serious error.
3. A two-sided test gives p = 0.03 at α = 0.05. A classmate says, "There is a 3% chance the null hypothesis is true." Correct them precisely.
The p-value is the probability of data at least this extreme given that the null is true — not the probability that the null is true. The null is either true or false; it has no probability in this framework. The correct reading: if the two populations really were identical, results this extreme would occur about 3% of the time.
4. In the paired reaction-time example, d̄ = 7.00 ms, sd = 5.555, n = 8. Recompute t and give the degrees of freedom.
t = 3.56 with df = 7. SE = 5.555/√8 = 5.555/2.8284 = 1.964; t = 7.00/1.964 = 3.564. Degrees of freedom are n − 1 = 7, where n is the number of pairs — not 16 measurements and not df = 15.
5. Why does the two-proportion test use a pooled proportion while the confidence interval does not?
The test is computed assuming the null is true, and the null says both populations share one common proportion — so the best estimate of it uses all the data combined. A confidence interval makes no such assumption; it estimates whatever the difference actually is, so each group contributes its own p̂ to the standard error.
6. Group 1: 24 successes in 80. Group 2: 12 successes in 60. Compute the two sample proportions, the pooled proportion, and check the success–failure condition.
p̂1 = 0.300, p̂2 = 0.200, p̂pool = 36/140 = 0.2571; the condition is met. Expected counts: 80(0.2571) = 20.6 and 80(0.7429) = 59.4; 60(0.2571) = 15.4 and 60(0.7429) = 44.6. All four are at least 10, so the normal approximation is safe.
7. A 95% confidence interval for μ1 − μ2 is (2.1, 8.7). State two things you can conclude and one common statement you must avoid.
Conclude: (a) zero is not in the interval, so a two-sided test at α = 0.05 would reject the null; (b) the plausible size of the difference runs from about 2 to about 9 units, with group 1 larger. Avoid: "there is a 95% chance the true difference is between 2.1 and 8.7." The parameter is fixed; the 95% refers to the long-run performance of the procedure across repeated studies.
8. Explain why a paired design can find an effect that an independent design of the same size misses.
Pairing subtracts out subject-to-subject variability, which is noise with respect to the treatment effect. Each person serves as their own control, so a naturally high or low baseline cancels in the difference. The result is a much smaller standard error — in the chapter's example, 1.96 ms paired versus 9.60 ms unpaired, shifting p from about 0.009 to about 0.48 for the identical 7 ms effect.
9. Two independent means: x̄1 − x̄2 = 5.0, SE = 2.0, df ≈ 30, so t* = 2.042. Build the 95% confidence interval and state the test decision it implies.
(0.92, 9.08), and the two-sided test rejects H0 at α = 0.05. Margin of error = 2.042 × 2.0 = 4.084; 5.0 − 4.084 = 0.916 and 5.0 + 4.084 = 9.084. Because zero lies outside the interval, "no difference" is not a plausible value at this confidence level.
10. When would you reach for a Wilcoxon rank-sum test instead of a two-sample t-test, and when for McNemar's test?
Wilcoxon rank-sum: numeric outcome, independent groups, but small samples with clear skew or outliers so the normality condition fails. McNemar: a yes/no outcome measured on paired subjects. The two-proportion z-test assumes independent groups and would be wrong for paired yes/no data.
11. A researcher gets a two-sided p of 0.08, then switches to a one-sided test to obtain p = 0.04 and declares significance. What is the problem?
The direction of the test must be fixed before seeing the data; choosing it afterward doubles the real false-positive rate. A one-sided test is legitimate only when a difference in the opposite direction would be of no interest whatsoever, and only when that decision is pre-registered. Post hoc switching makes the reported p-value meaningless.
12. Interval A is (−0.56, 13.56); interval B is (−0.20, 0.25). Both contain zero, so both tests fail to reject. Why are these two results scientifically very different?
Interval A is wide — it is consistent with no effect but also with a large effect, so the study is simply uninformative and should be repeated with a bigger sample. Interval B is narrow and pinned near zero, which is genuine evidence that any real difference is too small to matter. "Fail to reject" is identical in both cases; only the interval reveals the difference between ignorance and precision.
Statistics · Chapter 13

Chi-Square & ANOVA

Every test you have met so far has compared one mean, or two. Real questions are rarely that tidy. You want to know whether a whole set of categories falls out the way somebody claimed, whether two categorical variables travel together, or whether three, four, or five groups have the same average. This chapter gives you three tools for exactly those jobs — the chi-square goodness-of-fit test, the chi-square test of independence, and one-way analysis of variance — and then it tells you the truth that most courses rush past: what these tests require before you may trust them, and what to do the moment one of them comes back significant. The arithmetic is mostly subtraction, squaring, and division. If you can do that, you can do this.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • Compute expected counts and the chi-square statistic, χ2 = Σ(O − E)2/E, by hand.
  • Run a goodness-of-fit test to decide whether data match a claimed distribution, using df = k − 1.
  • Run a test of independence on a two-way table, using df = (r − 1)(c − 1), and describe the association you find.
  • Partition variability into between-group and within-group sums of squares and build a complete ANOVA table.
  • Explain why the F statistic is a ratio of two variance estimates, and why many t tests are a bad substitute for one F test.
  • State and check the assumptions behind chi-square and ANOVA, and name the right alternative when one fails.
  • Carry out and interpret a post-hoc comparison (Tukey's HSD or a Bonferroni correction) after a significant F.
  • Interpret p-values, confidence intervals, and effect sizes precisely, without the usual overstatements.

Section 1Chi-square goodness of fit

The question this test answers

You have one categorical variable with k categories, and somebody has made a claim about how the population splits across those categories. A manufacturer says its bags of candy are 30% red, 30% brown, and 40% yellow. A textbook says a fair die lands on each face one-sixth of the time. A subscription service says its customers split 25/40/35 across three tiers. You collect a sample and count. The counts never match the claim exactly — sampling sees to that. The goodness-of-fit test asks a single question: is the mismatch bigger than sampling noise can comfortably explain?

The hypotheses are stated in words as much as symbols:

  • H0: the population proportions are exactly as claimed (p1 = 0.25, p2 = 0.40, p3 = 0.35, say).
  • Ha: at least one proportion differs from its claimed value.

Notice that the alternative is vague on purpose. It says something is off; it does not say which category or in which direction. That vagueness is the price of testing all the categories at once, and it comes back to bite us in Section 5 in the ANOVA setting.

Observed, expected, and the statistic

The observed counts O are what you actually tallied. The expected counts E are what the null hypothesis predicts on average for a sample of your size: Ei = n × pi. Expected counts are frequently not whole numbers — 200 × 0.35 = 70 is tidy, but 137 × 0.35 = 47.95 is not, and you must not round it. Rounding expected counts is one of the most common ways a chi-square gets quietly wrecked by hand.

The statistic adds up one contribution per category:

χ2 = Σ (O − E)2 / E

Read that formula slowly, because it is the whole chapter in miniature. The numerator, (O − E)2, measures how far a category strayed from prediction, squared so that overshoots and undershoots both count as evidence. Dividing by E puts that miss on a fair scale: being 10 counts off when you expected 20 is a scandal; being 10 counts off when you expected 2,000 is nothing. Because every term is a square divided by a positive number, χ2 is never negative, and a value near zero means the data hug the claim. Big values, and only big values, are evidence against H0 — which is why the chi-square test is always right-tailed.

The degrees of freedom are df = k − 1, one less than the number of categories. The intuition: once you know the total and all but one category count, the last one is forced, so only k − 1 counts are free to vary. (If you had to estimate m parameters from the data to build the expected counts — fitting a Poisson model, for instance — you subtract those too: df = k − 1 − m.)

A worked example, start to finish

A meal-plan service claims its subscribers split across three tiers as Bronze 25%, Silver 40%, Gold 35%. A random sample of n = 200 subscribers gives Bronze 40, Silver 95, Gold 65. Test at α = 0.05.

Step 0 — check the conditions first. The four requirements are laid out in Section 4, and they are met here: the data are raw counts, not percentages; the sample is random; each subscriber is counted once and lands in exactly one tier, so the observations are independent; and every expected count computed in Step 1 is at least 5 (the smallest is 50). Do this before you look at any p-value, not after.

Step 1 — expected counts. EBronze = 200(0.25) = 50; ESilver = 200(0.40) = 80; EGold = 200(0.35) = 70. They sum to 200, which is a useful arithmetic check — expected counts always total n.

CategoryObserved OClaimed pExpected E = npO − E(O − E)2/E
Bronze400.2550−10100/50 = 2.0000
Silver950.4080+15225/80 = 2.8125
Gold650.3570−525/70 = 0.3571
Total2001.002000χ2 = 5.170

Step 2 — the statistic. 2.0000 + 2.8125 + 0.3571 = χ2 = 5.17.

Step 3 — degrees of freedom and critical value. k = 3 categories, so df = 3 − 1 = 2. From the table below, the critical value at α = 0.05 with df = 2 is 5.991.

Degrees of freedomα = 0.10α = 0.05α = 0.01
12.7063.8416.635
24.6055.9919.210
36.2517.81511.345
47.7799.48813.277
59.23611.07015.086
610.64512.59216.812

Step 4 — decide. 5.17 is less than 5.991, so we fail to reject H0. The p-value is about 0.075, which is larger than 0.05 and confirms the same decision.

Step 5 — say what that means, carefully. "At the 5% level, these data do not provide sufficient evidence that the true tier proportions differ from the claimed 25/40/35 split." That sentence is doing something specific. It does not say the claim is true. We fail to reject H0; we never accept it. Those are different verdicts — "not guilty" is not "innocent" — and a sample of 200 simply may not have had the power to detect a mismatch of the size that is really there. Absence of evidence is not evidence of absence. And the p-value of 0.075 is not a 7.5% chance that the provider is right. It is this: if the claimed split were exactly correct, samples of 200 would produce a mismatch this extreme or worse about 7.5% of the time. The probability lives in the data-generating process, not in the hypothesis.

The biggest term tells you the story. Even when you fail to reject, look at which category contributed most. Here Silver contributed 2.81 of the 5.17 — the sample had 95 Silver where 80 were predicted. That is the direction to watch if you collect more data. The overall test is a smoke alarm; the individual terms tell you which room to check.
Interactive Set a claimed colour mix and a hidden truth, then open real sampled bags of candy and watch chi-square assemble itself one cell at a time — expected counts from the claim, misses squared and scaled, stacked against the critical value and verified over thousands of simulated bags. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Goodness of fit compares one categorical variable's observed counts against a claimed distribution.
  • E = n × p; never round expected counts, and check that they sum to n.
  • χ2 = Σ(O − E)2/E is never negative, and the test is always right-tailed; df = k − 1.
  • A p-value is the probability of data this extreme given H0 — not the probability that H0 is true.
  • Check the four conditions before the p-value, and remember that fail to reject never means accept.

Section 2Chi-square test of independence

Two variables, one table

Now you have two categorical variables measured on the same people, and you cross them into a contingency table (also called a two-way table or crosstab) with r rows and c columns. Does exercise habit go with sleep quality? Does region go with brand preference? Does treatment group go with outcome category? The test of independence asks whether knowing a person's row tells you anything about their column.

  • H0: the two variables are independent in the population — there is no association.
  • Ha: the two variables are associated — they are not independent.

The statistic is the same formula as Section 1, cell by cell, and the test is still right-tailed. Only two things change: how you get the expected counts, and how you count degrees of freedom.

Expected counts from the margins

Under independence, the probability of landing in a particular row and a particular column is the product of the two separate probabilities. Translate that into counts and you get the working formula every cell obeys:

E = (row total × column total) / grand total

Why it works: the row total divided by n estimates the probability of that row; multiply by the column total and you get how many of that column's members should fall in that row if the row split were the same everywhere. That last phrase is the practical meaning of independence — every row has the same column profile.

Degrees of freedom are df = (r − 1)(c − 1). Same logic as before: with the margins fixed, once you fill in an (r − 1) by (c − 1) block of the table, subtraction forces every remaining cell.

A worked example, start to finish

A researcher surveys n = 300 adults, recording whether they exercise regularly and how they rate their sleep. Test for association at α = 0.05.

Observed countsGood sleepFair sleepPoor sleepRow total
Exercises regularly755025150
Does not exercise455550150
Column total12010575300

Step 1 — expected counts. Top-left: (150 × 120)/300 = 60.0. Top-middle: (150 × 105)/300 = 52.5. Top-right: (150 × 75)/300 = 37.5. Because the two row totals happen to be equal here, the bottom row repeats those values. Every expected count is at least 5, so the test is safe to run.

CellObserved OExpected EO − E(O − E)2/E
Exercise & Good7560.0+15.0225/60 = 3.7500
Exercise & Fair5052.5−2.56.25/52.5 = 0.1190
Exercise & Poor2537.5−12.5156.25/37.5 = 4.1667
No exercise & Good4560.0−15.0225/60 = 3.7500
No exercise & Fair5552.5+2.56.25/52.5 = 0.1190
No exercise & Poor5037.5+12.5156.25/37.5 = 4.1667
Total300300.00χ2 = 16.071

Step 2 — the statistic. Adding the last column: χ2 = 16.07.

Step 3 — degrees of freedom. r = 2 rows, c = 3 columns, so df = (2 − 1)(3 − 1) = 2. Critical value at α = 0.05 is again 5.991.

Step 4 — decide and interpret. 16.07 far exceeds 5.991, and the p-value is roughly 0.0003. We reject H0: there is strong evidence of an association between regular exercise and self-rated sleep quality in this population.

Step 5 — describe the association. The test says "associated" and stops there, so you have to look at the cells yourself. The two largest contributions are Exercise-and-Good (observed 75, expected 60) and Exercise-and-Poor (observed 25, expected 37.5): exercisers landed in "Good" more often and "Poor" less often than independence predicts. A tidier way to say the same thing is the standardized residual, (O − E)/√E; for Exercise-and-Good that is 15/√60 = +1.94, and for Exercise-and-Poor it is −12.5/√37.5 = −2.04. Residuals beyond roughly ±2 mark the cells that drove the result.

Step 6 — measure how big it is. With 300 people, even a modest association gets a tiny p-value, so report an effect size. Cramér's V = √[χ2 / (n × min(r−1, c−1))] = √[16.071 / (300 × 1)] = √0.05357 = 0.23. On a 0-to-1 scale, that is a real but moderate association — statistically unmistakable, practically modest.

Association is not causation, and a survey cannot make it one. These data are observational. Exercise may improve sleep; good sleep may make people feel like exercising; or a third factor — age, stress, shift work, chronic pain — may drive both. A significant χ2 establishes that the variables move together. Only a randomized experiment establishes which one pushes.
Interactive Survey real people, cross-tabulate two categorical variables, build the expected counts from the row and column totals, and test whether the two variables are associated. Open full ↑
Key points
  • The test of independence crosses two categorical variables in an r × c table.
  • E = (row total × column total)/grand total; df = (r − 1)(c − 1).
  • Rejecting H0 means "associated," not "which way" — read the cells and standardized residuals to describe the pattern.
  • Report an effect size such as Cramér's V; with large n, significance alone says very little about importance.

Section 3One-way ANOVA

Why not just run a lot of t tests?

Suppose you have three training methods and you want to know whether their mean test scores differ. The obvious move is three t tests: A vs B, A vs C, B vs C. The obvious move is wrong, and it is worth seeing exactly why.

Each test at α = 0.05 has a 5% chance of a false alarm. If the three tests were independent, the chance that at least one raises a false alarm would be 1 − (0.95)3 = 1 − 0.857 = 0.143. With five groups there are ten pairwise tests, and the figure climbs to 1 − (0.95)10 = 0.401. Be honest about that calculation: pairwise tests on the same groups are not independent, because every pair reuses a sample the other pairs also used. So treat 14% and 40% as illustrations of the size of the problem rather than exact rates. The direction is what matters, and it is not in doubt — you set out to run a 5% risk and quietly took on a far larger one. This inflated risk is the family-wise error rate, and it is the reason ANOVA exists: one test, one α, one answer.

  • H0: μ1 = μ2 = μ3 = … = μk (all population means are equal).
  • Ha: at least one mean differs from at least one other. (Not "all means differ" — a very common misstatement.)

The idea: two ways to measure spread

The name is honest — you analyze variance in order to learn about means. Here is the trick. If all the group means were truly equal, then two completely different calculations would both estimate the same population variance σ2:

  • Within-group variability (MSW): pool the scatter inside each group. Nothing about group differences touches this number — it is pure background noise, and it estimates σ2 whether H0 is true or not.
  • Between-group variability (MSB): measure how far the group means sit from the grand mean. If H0 is true, the means differ only by sampling noise and this also estimates σ2. If H0 is false, real mean differences inflate it.

So the ratio

F = MSB / MSW = (signal + noise) / noise

should sit near 1 when the means are equal and grow large when they are not. That is why ANOVA, like chi-square, is a right-tailed test — only big F values are evidence against H0. And because both mean squares are sums of squares divided by positive degrees of freedom, neither can be negative, so F is never negative either.

The building blocks, with N = total observations, k = number of groups, nj and x̄j the size and mean of group j, and x̄ the grand mean:

  • SSbetween = Σ nj(x̄j − x̄)2, with df = k − 1
  • SSwithin = ΣΣ(x − x̄j)2, with df = N − k
  • SStotal = SSbetween + SSwithin, with df = N − 1
  • MS = SS / df in each row, and F = MSB/MSW with (k − 1, N − k) degrees of freedom

A worked example, start to finish

Three training methods, five participants each (N = 15, k = 3), test scores. Conditions first, as always: the groups are independent, each is roughly symmetric with no outliers, the response is quantitative, and the equal-variance condition is checked explicitly with these very numbers in Section 4. ANOVA is the right tool here.

GroupScoresSumnjMean x̄jΣ(x − x̄j)2
A68, 71, 72, 73, 7636057216+1+0+1+16 = 34
B72, 75, 77, 79, 8238557725+4+0+4+25 = 58
C78, 80, 82, 83, 8741058216+4+0+1+25 = 46
Overall—115515x̄ = 77SSwithin = 138

Step 1 — grand mean. (360 + 385 + 410)/15 = 1155/15 = 77.

Step 2 — between-group sum of squares. SSbetween = 5(72 − 77)2 + 5(77 − 77)2 + 5(82 − 77)2 = 5(25) + 5(0) + 5(25) = 125 + 0 + 125 = 250.

Step 3 — within-group sum of squares. Add the last column of the table: 34 + 58 + 46 = 138.

Step 4 — check the partition. SStotal should equal 250 + 138 = 388. Computing it directly from all fifteen deviations about 77 gives 159 + 58 + 171 = 388. The identity holds, which is a strong sign the arithmetic is right.

Step 5 — mean squares and F. MSB = 250/2 = 125.00. MSW = 138/12 = 11.50. F = 125.00/11.50 = 10.87.

SourceSSdfMS = SS/dfFp-value
Between groups250k − 1 = 2125.0010.87≈ 0.0020
Within groups (error)138N − k = 1211.50——
Total388N − 1 = 14———

Step 6 — decide. The critical value for F(2, 12) at α = 0.05 is 3.89. Our 10.87 sails past it, p ≈ 0.0020, so we reject H0.

Numerator df (k − 1)Denominator df (N − k)Critical F, α = 0.05
2123.89
2203.49
3203.10
3302.92
4302.69

Step 7 — interpret precisely. "There is strong evidence that at least one of the three method means differs from the others." That is all the F test earns you. It does not say A differs from B, and it does not say every pair differs. Section 5 is where you find out which pairs are really separated — and the answer will surprise you.

Step 8 — report an effect size. Eta squared, η2 = SSbetween/SStotal = 250/388 = 0.64. About 64% of the variation in test scores is explained by which method a participant received — a large effect, and a far more useful number to a decision-maker than "p < 0.05."

With exactly two groups, ANOVA and the pooled t test are the same test. Run both on the same two samples and you will find F = t2, with identical p-values. ANOVA is not a different idea from the two-sample t test; it is the same idea, generalized so it does not fall apart when you add a third group.
Interactive Drag each group's mean bar apart or widen the within-group scatter and watch the one-way ANOVA F statistic, its sums-of-squares decomposition, p-value, and a live power simulation all recompute in real time. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Running many pairwise t tests inflates the family-wise error rate — 14% with three groups, 40% with five.
  • ANOVA compares MSB (between-group variability) to MSW (within-group noise): F = MSB/MSW.
  • SStotal = SSbetween + SSwithin; df are k − 1, N − k, and N − 1.
  • A significant F means at least one mean differs — never "all means differ." Report η2 alongside it.

Section 4Assumptions

What chi-square requires

A p-value is a promise, and the promise is conditional. Every chi-square p-value assumes four things, and software will happily print a number even when all four are violated.

1. The data are counts. Chi-square consumes raw frequencies — how many people, how many items. Feeding it percentages, proportions, or group means is the single most frequent misuse. If your table holds "42%" rather than "42 people," convert back to counts first, and if you cannot, you do not have the data you need.

2. Observations are independent, and each falls in exactly one cell. One row of data per person or item. If the same person is counted twice — before and after an intervention, say — the observations are paired and chi-square is the wrong test; use McNemar's test for a paired 2 × 2 table.

3. Expected counts are large enough. The chi-square curve is a continuous approximation to a discrete count problem, and the approximation frays when expected counts are tiny. The common working rule: all expected counts ≥ 5. Cochran's slightly more forgiving version allows up to 20% of cells to have E between 1 and 5, provided none falls below 1. Note that this is a condition on expected, not observed, counts — an observed zero is perfectly legal.

4. The sample is random. Every inferential claim depends on the sample representing the population. No formula repairs a convenience sample.

When rule 3 fails, you have real options: collapse sparse categories into a sensible larger one (two adjacent, rarely used response levels become a single "other" category), collect more data, or switch to Fisher's exact test, which computes the probability directly and needs no large-sample approximation at all.

What ANOVA requires

1. Independence, both within and between groups. This is the assumption ANOVA is least willing to forgive, and no amount of data fixes a violation. If the same subjects appear in every group, you need repeated-measures ANOVA instead.

2. Approximate normality of the response within each group (equivalently, of the residuals). ANOVA is genuinely robust here: with roughly equal group sizes and a sample of decent size, moderate skew barely dents the F test, and the Central Limit Theorem is on your side. The condition matters most when groups are small (under about 15) or the data are strongly skewed or heavily outlier-ridden. Check it with a histogram or normal quantile plot per group, not with a formal normality test on tiny samples.

3. Homogeneity of variance — the population spreads are equal across groups. MSW pools all the groups' scatter into one number, and that pooling only makes sense if there is one common σ2 to pool. Two quick checks: the largest sample standard deviation should be less than about twice the smallest, or equivalently the largest variance less than about four times the smallest. Levene's test is the formal version.

4. The response is quantitative and measured on an interval or ratio scale. You cannot average a category.

Let us actually run check 3 on the Section 3 data. The within-group sums of squares were 34, 58, and 46, each with df = 4:

GroupSSs2 = SS/(n−1)s
A3434/4 = 8.502.92
B5858/4 = 14.503.81
C4646/4 = 11.503.39

Largest s divided by smallest s = 3.81/2.92 = 1.31, comfortably under 2. Largest variance over smallest = 14.50/8.50 = 1.71, comfortably under 4. The equal-variance condition is satisfied, so the pooled MSW of 11.50 was a legitimate thing to compute.

When a condition fails

A failed assumption is not the end of the analysis. It is a signpost to a different test.

Condition that failedSymptomWhat to do instead
Expected counts too smallAny E < 5, sparse tableCombine categories; or use Fisher's exact test
Paired categorical dataSame subjects measured twiceMcNemar's test
Unequal ANOVA variancessmax/smin > 2, especially with unequal nWelch's ANOVA (does not pool variances)
Strong non-normality, small groupsHeavy skew or clear outliersKruskal–Wallis test (rank-based)
Same subjects in every groupRepeated measurementsRepeated-measures ANOVA
Non-random sampleConvenience or self-selected dataDescribe only; no valid inference to a population
Check the conditions before you look at the p-value, not after. Once you have seen a promising p-value, the temptation to declare the assumptions "close enough" is nearly irresistible — and the decision is no longer honest. Write the assumption check as step one of your procedure, and let its verdict stand whichever way the test comes out.
Interactive A live assumption checker for the chi-square test and one-way ANOVA — move sliders to watch expected cell counts, group standard deviations, the variance ratio, normality, and independence get evaluated in real time, with the χ² statistic and p-value computed from a fresh sample. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Chi-square needs counts, independent observations, expected counts ≥ 5, and a random sample.
  • ANOVA needs independence, approximate normality within groups, equal variances, and a quantitative response.
  • Rules of thumb: all E ≥ 5; smax/smin < 2 (or variance ratio < 4).
  • Failed conditions point to a specific replacement test — Fisher's exact, McNemar, Welch, or Kruskal–Wallis.

Section 5After a significant ANOVA

The F test leaves you with a question

We rejected H0 in Section 3 with F = 10.87. What we know is that the three method means are not all equal. What we do not know is which ones differ. That gap is exactly what post-hoc ("after this") comparisons fill: they go back through the pairs, one at a time, while keeping the total false-alarm risk across the whole family of comparisons at α.

And this is where you cannot simply run ordinary t tests, for the very reason ANOVA existed in the first place. Three unadjusted pairwise tests carry a family-wise error rate in the neighborhood of 14%. Post-hoc procedures are pairwise tests with the inflation engineered out.

Two workhorses: Bonferroni and Tukey

The Bonferroni correction is the simplest honest fix in all of statistics: if you plan m comparisons, test each one at α/m. With three groups, m = 3 and each comparison is judged at 0.05/3 = 0.0167. Equivalently, multiply each raw p-value by 3 and compare to 0.05. It works for any set of comparisons, it takes ten seconds to explain, and it is somewhat conservative — it protects against false alarms so firmly that it can miss real differences, especially when m is large.

Tukey's HSD (Honestly Significant Difference) is purpose-built for the specific job of comparing all pairs after an ANOVA, and for that job it is more powerful than Bonferroni. Instead of a t distribution it uses the studentized range distribution q, which already accounts for the fact that you are looking at the largest gaps among k means. For equal group sizes the rule is beautifully simple: any two means further apart than the HSD value are significantly different.

HSD = qα, k, N−k × √(MSW / n)

Working it out on our data

From the ANOVA table: MSW = 11.50, N − k = 12, k = 3, n = 5 per group. The studentized range value q for α = 0.05, k = 3, df = 12 is 3.77.

Step 1 — the standard error piece. √(MSW/n) = √(11.50/5) = √2.30 = 1.517.

Step 2 — the HSD threshold. HSD = 3.77 × 1.517 = 5.72. Any two sample means more than 5.72 points apart are significantly different; any pair closer than that is not.

Step 3 — compare every pair. The means were A = 72, B = 77, C = 82.

ComparisonDifference in means|Difference|vs. HSD = 5.72Conclusion
A vs B72 − 77 = −55.005.00 < 5.72Not significant
A vs C72 − 82 = −1010.0010.00 > 5.72Significant
B vs C77 − 82 = −55.005.00 < 5.72Not significant

Read that result carefully, because it is the whole point of the section. The overall F was highly significant, yet only one of the three pairs separates. Method C beats method A; neither can be distinguished from the middle method B. An analyst who reported "the ANOVA was significant, so all three methods differ" would have gotten the science wrong while getting the arithmetic right.

Step 4 — give intervals, not just verdicts. Tukey also produces confidence intervals: difference ± HSD. For A vs C that is −10 ± 5.72, or (−15.72, −4.28). Because the interval excludes zero, the difference is significant — and the interval says far more than the p-value does, namely that the plausible advantage of C over A runs from about 4 to about 16 points. Say what such an interval means with care: the procedure that produced it captures the true mean difference in 95% of all possible samples. It is not a 95% chance that this particular interval, already computed and sitting on the page, contains the parameter. The parameter is fixed; the interval is what varies from sample to sample.

Step 5 — cross-check with Bonferroni. The standard error of a difference is √[MSW(1/n + 1/n)] = √[11.50(0.4)] = √4.60 = 2.145. For A vs C, t = 10/2.145 = 4.66; for A vs B, t = 5/2.145 = 2.33. The Bonferroni critical value is the t for α = 0.0167 two-tailed with df = 12, about 2.78. So A vs C (4.66) clears it and A vs B (2.33) does not — the same conclusion Tukey reached, which is reassuring.

Choosing a method, and when not to bother

MethodBest used forCharacter
Tukey's HSDAll pairwise comparisons after ANOVAThe default choice; most powerful for this exact job
BonferroniA small, pre-specified set of comparisonsSimple, universally valid, conservative
Dunnett's testEvery group vs. a single control groupMore powerful than Tukey when only control contrasts matter
Scheffé's methodAny contrast, including ones you thought of afterwardMost flexible, most conservative
Games–HowellPairwise comparisons with unequal variancesThe post-hoc partner to Welch's ANOVA

Two habits keep this honest. First, if the overall F is not significant, do not go fishing through the pairs hoping one turns up — that is precisely the error-inflation the F test protected you from. (Planned contrasts specified before seeing the data are the legitimate exception.) Second, always show the picture. Side-by-side boxplots of the three groups convey the overlap between A and B, and the clean separation between A and C, more immediately than any table of p-values.

A significant F is a beginning, not a conclusion. It tells you the groups are not all the same. Everything a reader actually wants — which groups, by how much, with what uncertainty, and does it matter practically — comes from the post-hoc comparisons, the confidence intervals, and the effect size. Report all four and your analysis is complete.
Interactive A significant ANOVA says the arms are not all alike but not which ones—run the post-hoc comparisons live, watch confidence intervals widen under a correction, and simulate how uncorrected pairwise testing inflates the false-alarm rate. Open full ↑
Key points
  • A significant F says "at least one mean differs"; post-hoc tests identify which pairs.
  • Tukey's HSD = q × √(MSW/n) is the standard for all pairwise comparisons; Bonferroni (α/m) is the simple general-purpose fix.
  • Significant overall does not mean every pair differs — here only A vs C separated.
  • A 95% interval is a statement about the long-run procedure, not a 95% chance for the interval in front of you.

The chapter in one breath

  • Every chi-square test uses χ2 = Σ(O − E)2/E, is never negative, and is always right-tailed.
  • Goodness of fit tests one categorical variable against a claimed distribution: E = np, df = k − 1.
  • Test of independence tests two categorical variables for association: E = (row × column)/total, df = (r − 1)(c − 1) — and association is not causation.
  • One-way ANOVA compares three or more means with F = MSB/MSW, avoiding the family-wise error that many t tests would create.
  • The partition SStotal = SSbetween + SSwithin is both the engine of ANOVA and a built-in arithmetic check.
  • Chi-square needs counts, independence, and expected counts ≥ 5; ANOVA needs independence, rough normality, and equal variances — each failure has a named alternative.
  • A significant F means at least one mean differs; Tukey's HSD or a Bonferroni correction tells you which pairs, without inflating α.
  • Always pair a p-value with an effect size (Cramér's V, η2) and an interval, and state both with the precision they demand.

ReferenceWords worth knowing

A quick reference for the terms in this chapter — skim it now, or circle back whenever a word trips you up.

Alpha (α)
The significance level: the risk of a false alarm you agree to accept before collecting data, most often 0.05.
Analysis of variance (ANOVA)
A procedure that compares three or more population means by comparing two estimates of variance, between-group and within-group.
Bonferroni correction
Testing each of m planned comparisons at α/m so the family-wise error rate stays at α. Simple and conservative.
Chi-square statistic (χ2)
Σ(O − E)2/E, a non-negative measure of total mismatch between observed and expected counts.
Contingency table
A two-way table of counts crossing two categorical variables, with row, column, and grand totals in the margins.
Cramér's V
An effect size for a contingency table, √[χ2/(n × min(r−1, c−1))], running from 0 (no association) to 1.
Degrees of freedom
The number of values free to vary once the constraints are imposed; k − 1 for goodness of fit, (r−1)(c−1) for independence, and (k−1, N−k) for ANOVA.
Eta squared (η2)
SSbetween/SStotal: the proportion of variation in the response explained by group membership.
Expected count (E)
The count a cell would show on average if the null hypothesis were true. Never round it.
F statistic
The ratio MSB/MSW. Near 1 when group means are equal, large when they are not; never negative, and always tested in the upper tail.
Family-wise error rate
The probability of at least one false alarm across a whole family of tests. It inflates rapidly as the number of comparisons grows.
Goodness-of-fit test
A chi-square test of whether one categorical variable's observed counts match a claimed distribution.
Homogeneity of variance
The ANOVA condition that all groups share the same population variance; checked with smax/smin < 2 or Levene's test.
Kruskal–Wallis test
A rank-based alternative to one-way ANOVA used when the normality condition fails badly.
Mean square (MS)
A sum of squares divided by its degrees of freedom — that is, a variance estimate. MSB and MSW are the two that build F.
Observed count (O)
The actual tally in a category or cell of your sample.
p-value
The probability of getting data at least as extreme as yours if the null hypothesis were true. It is not the probability that the null hypothesis is true.
Post-hoc test
A follow-up comparison run after a significant ANOVA to identify which specific groups differ, with the family-wise error rate controlled.
Standardized residual
